The Imam's Authority to Initiate Combat Against Rebels
Hanafi ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on initiating combat against rebels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on initiating combat against rebels and explain the Quranic proof that supports it, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
The Quranic command to fight rebels is unconditional – the imam need not wait for attack.

This lesson explores a critical ruling in Islamic public law: may the imam initiate military action against Muslim rebels before they attack? The Hanafi school answers yes, rooted in the general command of Quran 49:9. We will examine the proof, the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, and the rationale behind the Hanafi preference for preemptive action to prevent greater harm.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the precise ruling from the text: the imam may initiate combat against rebels (bughat) even if they have not attacked, provided they have gathered and prepared for war.
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the primary proof
Find the Quranic verse cited: Surah al-Hujurat (49):9. Note that the imperative 'قاتلوا' (fight) is general and not conditioned on initiation.
The verse commands fighting the rebellious party without qualification.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the verse proves the ruling: the imperative indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul, but here it is interpreted as permissibility (ibaha) because the context is fighting fellow Muslims; still, it permits initiation.
The general command overrides the presumption of not killing Muslims.
4
Examine the opposing view
Identify the Shafi'i position: the imam must not initiate, as killing Muslims is only allowed to repel harm. Understand their dalil and reasoning.
They prioritize the sanctity of Muslim life.
5
Understand the Hanafi response and tarjih
See how the Hanafis rebut: the Quranic command is unconditional; delaying may strengthen the rebels; the ruling is based on their gathering (imtina'). The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.
The response also addresses the narration of Abu Hanifa about avoiding fitna, restricting it to absence of a just imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البغاة وكشف شبهتهم، وبدأ بقتالهم ولو لهم فئة أجهز على جريحهم، واتبع موليهم، وإلا لا، ولم يسب ذريتهم، وحبس أموالهم حتى يتوبوا، أي وإن لم يكن لهم فئة أي الإمام أي البغاة ولا يقسمها فيرد عليهم أموالهم بالإجماع أي وتعاقب شبهتهم: أي التي استندوا إليها في خروجهم عن الطاعة (عيني) وبدأ بقتالهم: [وإن لم يبدؤوا بقتال الإمام إذا تعسكروا واجتمعوا للقتال. (عيني] أي وللإمام أن يبدأ بقتالهم، وهو ظاهر الرواية، وذكر القدوري أنه لا يبدؤوهم حتى يبدؤوا بالقتال، وهو قول الشافعي؛ لأنهم مسلمون فلا يجوز قتل المسلم إلا دفعاً لضررهم، بخلاف الكفار حيث يبدأ بقتالهم؛ لأن الكفر ،مبيح، ولنا: إطلاق قوله تعالى: ﴿فَقَاتِلُوا الَّتِي تَبْغِي حَتَّى تَفِيءَ إِلَى أَمْرِ الله (الحجرات:9) فصار قتالهم كقتال أهل الحرب؛ ولأن الحكم يدار على الدليل، وهو الاجتماع والامتناع، فإن تساهل الإمام في البداءة بالقتال فربما لا يمكنه دفعهم بعد ذلك. فيبدأ القتال بهم إذا تحيزوا وتهيؤوا للقتال، والمروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال: إذا هاجت الفتنة بين المسلمين ينبغي أن يعتزل الفتنة، وتحرز عنها، ويلزم البيت، ولا يخرج، فهو محمول على عدم الإمام الحق، يدعوه إلى قتال أهل البغى، وأما إذا كان له إمام فطاعته فيما ليس بمعصية فرض، فكيف بما هو طاعة؟ وما روي عن ابن عمر مع جماعة القعود عند الفتنة محمول على أنهم كانوا عاجزين. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) من الصحابة من ما
Chapter of Rebels: And to remove their doubt, and to initiate combat against them even if they have a group, to finish off their wounded, and pursue their fleeing, and otherwise no, and do not take their offspring as captives, and detain their wealth until they repent. (That is, even if they do not have a group? meaning the imam? meaning the rebels? And do not divide it, rather return their wealth to them by consensus. And punish? Their doubt: that which they relied on in their disobedience. (Ayni) And to initiate combat against them: [Even if they do not initiate combat with the imam when they gather and assemble for combat. (Ayni)] That is, the imam may initiate combat against them, and this is the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). Al-Quduri mentioned that they should not be initiated until they initiate combat, and that is the saying of al-Shafi'i; because they are Muslims, so it is not permissible to kill a Muslim except to repel their harm, unlike the disbelievers where combat is initiated against them because disbelief permits (killing). And our proof: the generality of His statement: ﴿So fight the one that rebels until it returns to the command of Allah﴾ (al-Hujurat:9). So their fighting becomes like fighting the people of war. And because the ruling revolves around the evidence, which is gathering and refusal. If the imam is lenient in initiating combat, perhaps he will not be able to repel them afterwards. So combat is initiated against them when they gather and prepare for combat. And what is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he said: When fitna arises among Muslims, one should avoid the fitna, be cautious, stay at home, and not go out. This is interpreted as when there is no just imam calling to fight the rebels. But if there is an imam, obedience to him in what is not disobedience is obligatory, so how about what is obedience? And what is narrated from Ibn Umar and a group of sitting during fitna is interpreted as their inability. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) from the companions, some.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling on initiating combat against rebels (bughat). The matn (base text) states that the imam may initiate combat even if the rebels have not attacked, finish off their wounded, pursue their fleeing, not take their children as captives, and detain their property until they repent. The commentator explains that the imam may begin fighting them even if they haven't started, which is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah. Al-Quduri and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that the imam should not initiate until they attack, arguing that Muslims cannot be killed except to repel harm. The Hanafi response provides two proofs: (1) The Quranic verse 49:9 commands fighting the rebellious party, with a general imperative that does not require them to start; (2) The ruling is based on the evidence of their gathering and refusal (imtina'), so delaying may make them stronger. The commentator also addresses the narration from Abu Hanifa about avoiding fitna: this applies when there is no just imam; when a legitimate imam calls to fight rebels, obedience is obligatory. The narration of Ibn Umar is interpreted as due to inability.

Key Terms
بغاة (bughat): Rebels who oppose the legitimate imam with a ta'wil (interpretation) and have a group (fata).
فئة (fi'ah): A group (of rebels) that serves as a rallying point or base.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six core books; considered the most authoritative narrations in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the imam initiating combat against rebels (bughat) before they attack?
A) It is not permissible until they attack first.
B) It is permissible when they gather and prepare for war.
C) It is obligatory to wait for them to attack.
D) It is forbidden because Muslims cannot be killed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the imam to initiate combat against rebels when they gather and prepare, based on the general imperative of Quran 49:9, prioritizing the prevention of greater harm.

Treatment of Wounded and Fleeing Rebels: Conditional Permissibility
Ruling on finishing off wounded and pursuing fleeing rebels based on presence of a group

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on finishing off wounded rebels and pursuing fugitives, and apply the condition of the existence of a group (fi'ah) to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
A wounded rebel may be killed only if he has a group to rejoin; otherwise, killing is prohibited by consensus.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the Hanafi position on dealing with rebels after battle. The ruling hinges on whether the rebels have an organized group (fi'ah) to return to. If they do, finishing off the wounded and pursuing fugitives is permissible to prevent them from rejoining the fight. If not, there is consensus that they must not be killed. The passage also contrasts the Hanafi view with that of the three other schools and cites a historical precedent from the Battle of the Camel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Finishing off wounded rebels (ajhaza 'ala jarihihim) and pursuing fugitives (ittiba' al-mawli). The hukm is jaez (permissible) but conditional.
2
Locate the condition
The key condition is whether the rebels have a group (fi'ah) to rejoin. If yes, killing is permitted; if no, it is prohibited by consensus.
The condition is explicitly stated: wa law lahum fi'ah.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The evidence: the precedent from the Day of Jamal where Imam Ali prohibited killing fugitives and wounded because the rebels had no group. The Hanafi reasoning is that the intention to continue fighting (niyyah al-qital) is what matters, not the actual cessation of fighting.
This is a talil argument based on the reality of the threat.
4
Analyze the cross-madhhab dispute
The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that wounded and fugitives should never be killed because they are Muslims who have stopped fighting. The Hanafi response: if they have a group, they still intend to fight, so killing is permissible to repel their evil.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: Hanafi allows killing in some cases; others never.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If rebels have no group (fi'ah), the ruling is by consensus: do not kill wounded or fugitives. This is because there is no fear of them regrouping.
The source emphasizes the consensus (ijma') when no fi'ah exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو لهم فئة: أي لو كانت للبغاة جماعة يرجعون إليها. (عيني) أجهز على جريحهم: [يعني يتم جرحهم وهو كناية عن إتمام القتل أي أتم قتلهم بالسرعة. (ط) عيني) من الإجهاز بمعنى قتل الجريح أي تم قتلهم، وكذا قتل أسيرهم، وإن شاء حبسهم وهو الأحسن؛ لأنه يؤمن شره من غير قتل. وقوله: واتبع موليهم بكسير اللام المشددة وسكون التحتية" هو الذي يولي ويهرب خوفاً على نفسه أي ويتبع هاربهم فيقتله دفعاً لشرهم كي لا يلحقوا وعند الثلاثة لا يجهز ولا يتبع؛ لأنهم إذا تركوا القتال لم يبق قتلهم دفعاً، فلا يقتل لكونه مسلماً، ولنا: أن المعتبر نية القتال لا حقيقته (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) بالفئة وإلا لا : [ أي لا يجهز على جريحهم ولا يتبع موليهم بالإجماع. (ط، عيني)] أي وإن لم يكن لهم جماعة لا يجهز على جريحهم ولا يتعاقب هاربهم بالإجماع؛ لأنه لا يخاف أن يلحقا بالفئة فلا ضرورة في قتلهما فلا يقتلان كما روي عن مروان بن الحكم أنه قال: صرخ صارخ لعلي الله يوم الجمل: "لا يقتلن مدبر ولا يدافف أي لا يجهز جريح، ومن أغلق بابه فهو آمن ومن ألقى السلاح فهو آمن ، رواه سعيد بن منصور، ويوم الجمل لم يكن لهم فئة وهو يوم وقعة عائشة لها وزبير وطلحة مع علي الله وهم يطلبون بدم عثمان وإنما سمي يوم الجمل؛ لأن عائشة ما كانت يومئذ على جمل يسمي . عسكرا. رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٨/١] (فتح)
And if they have a group: i.e., if the rebels have a group to which they return. (Ayni) To finish off their wounded: [means to complete their wounding, a metaphor for completing the killing, i.e., to kill them quickly. (T,Ayni) from 'ijhaz' meaning killing the wounded, i.e., to complete killing them. Similarly, killing their prisoner, or if he wishes, he may detain them, which is better because it secures from his evil without killing. And his statement: 'follow their fighter' (mawlihim) with a kasra on the lam and sukun on the ya, he who turns and flees fearing for himself, i.e., pursue their fugitive and kill him to repel their evil so that they do not rejoin (the group). And according to the three (other schools), he does not finish off nor pursue; because when they leave fighting, there remains no repelling killing, so he is not killed because he is Muslim. And for us: what is considered is the intention of fighting, not its reality. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) by the group. And otherwise no: [i.e., he does not finish off their wounded nor pursue their fugitive by consensus. (T, Ayni)] That is, if they have no group, he does not finish off their wounded nor pursue their fugitive by consensus; because there is no fear that they may rejoin the group, so there is no necessity to kill them. As narrated from Marwan ibn al-Hakam that he said: A crier cried out on the Day of Jamal: 'Let no one fleeing be killed, nor anyone wounded be finished off, and whoever closes his door is safe, and whoever lays down his arms is safe.' This was narrated by Sa'id ibn Mansur. On the Day of Jamal, they had no group, and it was the day of the battle of Aisha, Zubayr, and Talha against Ali (RA), seeking vengeance for Uthman's blood. It was called the Day of the Camel because Aisha (RA) was riding a camel that day. Symbol of Realities: 1/448] (Fath)
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on finishing off wounded rebels (ijhaz) and pursuing fugitives when they have a group (fata). The Hanafi position is that if the rebels have a base to rally to, the imam may kill the wounded and pursue the fleeing to prevent them from rejoining the fight. This is based on considering their intention to continue fighting. The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that once they have ceased fighting, they cannot be killed because they are Muslims. However, if the rebels do not have a group (fi'ah), there is consensus that neither the wounded nor the fugitives should be killed, as there is no danger of them regrouping. The commentator cites the precedent from the Battle of the Camel ('the Day of Jamal') where Ali (RA) ordered that no fugitive or wounded be killed, as the rebels had no group to return to.

Key Terms
أجهز على جريحهم (ajhaza 'ala jarihihim): To finish off their wounded; i.e., kill them quickly.
موليهم (mawlihim): The one who turns and flees; the fugitive from the battle.
فئة (fi'ah): A group, band, or party; the organized group to which the rebels can return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible to kill a wounded rebel (baghi) after the battle?
A) Always, because they have rebelled.
B) Only if the rebels have a group (fi'ah) to rejoin.
C) Only if the rebel initiated the attack.
D) Never, because they are Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits killing wounded rebels and pursuing fugitives only if they have a group to rejoin; otherwise, there is consensus it is prohibited.

Protection of Rebels' Children and Property
Ruling on rebels' children and property with proof from Ali (RA).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings that rebels' children are not to be taken captive and their property is detained, and explain the proof from the saying of Ali (RA).

Core Idea
Rebels are Muslims: their children are not captives and their property is detained until repentance.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary addresses the legal status of rebels (bughat) after they are subdued. It establishes two key protections: their offspring are not to be taken as captives, and their property is not to be divided but detained until they repent. The source of these rules is the practice of Ali (RA) during the Battle of the Camel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the opening line: 'ولم يسب ذريتهم' (do not take their offspring) and 'حبس أموالهم' (detain their wealth). Note the two prohibited/detained actions.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the saying of Ali (RA) on the Day of Jamal: 'لا يقتل أسير ، ولا يكشف ستر، ولا يؤخذ مال'. Recognize each phrase as proof for the corresponding ruling.
The three prohibitions map to: no killing prisoners (when no group), no uncovering women (i.e., no taking captives), no taking wealth.
3
Analyse the reasoning
Understand why these protections apply: rebels are Muslims, and their lives and property are inviolable by two inviolabilities (العصمتان): Islam and residence in Dar al-Islam.
This is why their offspring cannot be enslaved and their property must be returned after repentance.
4
Distinguish from disbelievers
Note that the same rules do not apply to non-Muslim combatants in Dar al-Harb. The key difference is Islamic identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يسب ذريتهم لقول علي الله يوم الجمل: "لا يقتل أسير ، ولا يكشف ستر، ولا يؤخذ مال"، وهو القدوة في هذا الباب؛ لأن البغاة كثرت في زمان خلافته والمراد بقوله : "لا يقتل أسير" إذا لم يكن لهم فئة، وإن كان لهم فئة فإن احتاج، قاتل بسلاحهم وخيلهم، وإن قتل باغ مثله، فظهر عليهم، لم يجب عمداً أو خطا شيء، وإن غلبوا على أهل مصر، فقتل مصري مثله، أي البغاة أي من أمصارنا أي رجل من أهل المصر أي على البغاة أي على القاتل = فالإمام بالخيار إن شاء قتله لئلا ينفلت ويلحق بهم، وإن شاء حبسه؛ لأن شره يندفع به، وليس له أن يسترقه؛ لأنه مسلم والإسلام يمنع الاسترقاق ابتداء، وهو المراد بقول علي الله : "لا يكشف ستر" حين طلب منه " النساء يقسم بينهم، فقال: إذا قسمت فلمن تكون عائشة هما ؟ فأبهتهم بذلك وقطع شبهتهم، وقوله: حبس أموالهم أي لا تقسم حتى يتوبوا لما مر من قول علي الله ولا يؤخذ مال؛ لأنهم مسلمون، فتكون أموالهم وأنفسهم معصومين بالعصمتين: الإسلام والدار؛ لكونهم في دار الإسلام فتحبس حتى يتوبوا فترد عليهم بالإجماع. تبيين الحقائق: ١٩٥/٤] (فتح)
And do not take their offspring as captives, due to the saying of Ali (RA) on the Day of Jamal: 'No prisoner shall be killed, no veil shall be removed, no wealth shall be taken.' He is the exemplar in this chapter because the rebels were many during his caliphate. And the meaning of his statement 'No prisoner shall be killed' is when they have no group. And if they have a group, then if need arises, fight with their weapons and horses. And if a rebel kills another like him, then we overcome them, nothing is obligatory whether intentional or by mistake. And if they overpower the people of a city, and a city man kills a rebel like him, meaning from our cities, meaning a man from the city people, meaning upon the rebels, meaning upon the killer, then the imam has the choice: if he wishes, he may kill him so that he does not escape and rejoin them; if he wishes, he may detain him, because his evil is repelled thereby. He does not have the right to enslave him because he is a Muslim, and Islam prohibits enslavement initially. That is the meaning of Ali's statement 'no veil shall be removed' when he was asked to distribute women among them. He said: 'If I distribute, to whom will Aisha belong?' Thus he silenced them and cut off their doubt. And his statement: 'detain their wealth': i.e., do not divide it until they repent, due to the statement of Ali (RA) 'no wealth shall be taken'. Because they are Muslims, so their wealth and lives are inviolable by two inviolabilities: Islam and the abode; because they are in the abode of Islam. So it is detained until they repent, then returned to them by consensus. (Tabiyin al-Haqa'iq: 4/195) (Fath)
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings regarding rebels: (1) Their children (offspring) are not to be taken as captives (saby) – citing the precedent of Ali (RA) on the Day of Jamal who declared no prisoner execution, no uncovering of women, and no taking of property. (2) Their property is to be detained (not divided among the loyalists) until they repent, then returned to them. The commentator explains the reasoning: rebels are Muslims, so their lives and property are protected by both Islam and the fact that they reside in Dar al-Islam. The text also clarifies that 'no prisoner shall be killed' applies when the rebels have no group; if they have a group, different rules apply (discussed later). The story about Ali refusing to distribute women (including Aisha) demonstrates that rebel women are not to be taken as captives.

Key Terms
سبي الذرية (saby al-dhurriyyah): Taking children (offspring) as captives of war.
العصمتان (al-'ismatan): The two inviolabilities: protection of life and property due to Islam and residence in Dar al-Islam.
البغاة (al-bughah): Rebels who defy a legitimate imam but are Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is done with the property of rebels (bughat) after they are subdued?
A) It is divided among the imam's soldiers as war booty.
B) It is detained until the rebels repent, then returned to them.
C) It is confiscated permanently.
D) It is distributed among the poor.

Key Takeaway
Rebels' offspring are never captives and their property is a trust, not spoils—both protected by their Islamic identity.

Use of Rebels' Weapons and Horses in Battle
Ruling on imam using rebels' weapons and horses in battle if needed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using rebels' weapons and horses in battle, explain the condition of need, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Need permits using rebels' property without liability – first analogy over the Prophet's loan of Safwan's armor.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a practical question of war: when the Muslim army is in need, may the imam use the weapons and horses of rebels? The Hanafi school answers yes, with no liability, based on the practice of Ali (RA) and the Prophet's example. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing property rights are inviolable. Let's examine the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Using rebels' weapons and horses in battle. State the Hanafi ruling: permissible when needed, no liability for damage.
The ruling is conditional on need.
2
Note the condition of need
The ruling applies only when the imam needs these items for battle. If no need, they are detained like other rebel property.
Need is explicitly mentioned: 'إذا احتاج'.
3
Present the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i argues that it is prohibited because it is Muslim property, and using without consent is unlawful.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different principles.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Two proofs: (1) Ali (RA) distributed weapons among his companions in Basra as a distribution of need, not ownership. (2) The Prophet (SAW) took Safwan's armor without his consent, calling it a loan ('ariyah) to be returned. This establishes that in need, Muslim property can be used for the common good.
The dalil is a hadith ahad, but it is supported by practice of the Companions.
5
Derive the first analogy (awla)
Since the Prophet's loan was from a just Muslim, using rebel property (who is less protected) is even more justified. Also, dhimmis aiding rebels have same protection as rebels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن احتاج إلخ : [ أي الإمام إلى السلاح والخيل لأهل البغي. (ط)] معناه إذا كان لهم فئة ولا ضمان بإتلافهما، وفي ذكر الاحتياج إشارة إليه أي لا بأس أن يقاتل المسلمون بسلاحهم وخيلهم إن احتاجوا، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز؛ لأنه مال مسلم فلا يجوز الانتفاع به إلا برضاه ولنا : أن عليا ليه قسم السلاح بين أصحابه بالبصرة، وكانت قسمة الحاجة لا التمليك؛ ولأن للإمام أن يفعل ذلك في مال العادل عند الحاجة. كما أخذ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم درعاً من صفوان بغير رضاه، حيث قال: غصباً يا محمد فقال: "لا بل عارية مؤداة" فيجوز في مال الباغي بالطريق الأولى عند الحاجة، وإن لم يحتاجوا إليها حبسها منهم كسائر أموالهم، والكراع أي الخيل يباع ويحبس ثمنه؛ لأنه أيسر؛ لاحتياجه إلى نفقة وقد تبلغ القيمة فكان بيعه أنفع لصاحبه، ولو كان معهم أهل الذمة يعينونهم على القتال، فحكمهم حكم أهل البغي حتى لا يجوز ،استرقاقهم، ولا أخذ أموالهم؛ لأن عهدهم لا ينتقض به رمز الحقائق: ٤٤۸/۱، ٤٤٩] (مستخلص، فتح) وخيلهم وإن لم يحتج إلى ذلك حبس خيلهم كسائر أموالهم . (ط)
And if he needs, etc.: [i.e., the imam needs weapons and horses of the people of rebellion. (T)] Its meaning: when they have a group and there is no liability for destroying them, and the mention of need indicates this: there is no harm in the Muslims fighting with their weapons and horses if they need. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible, because it is the property of a Muslim, so it is not allowed to benefit from it without his consent. And for us: Ali (RA) distributed weapons among his companions in Basra, and it was a distribution of need, not ownership. And the imam may do that with the property of the just person in need. As the Messenger of Allah (SAW) took a coat of mail from Safwan without his consent, when he said: 'A seizure, O Muhammad?' He replied: 'Rather, a loan with guarantee.' So it is permissible with the property of a rebel by the primary method in times of need. And if they do not need them, they detain them from them like the rest of their wealth. And with regard to cavalry (i.e., horses), they are sold and their price is detained because it is easier, as they need expenditure and the value may become high, so selling is more beneficial for the owner. And if there are dhimmis with them who help them in fighting, their ruling is the same as the ruling of the people of rebellion, so that their enslavement is not permissible, nor taking their wealth, because their covenant is not broken thereby. (Symbol of Realities: 1/448, 449) (Mukhtasar, Fath) And their horses: if he does not need that, he detains their horses like the rest of their wealth. (T)
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the imam may use the weapons and horses of rebels in battle when needed. The Hanafi ruling: yes, it is permissible, and there is no liability (damān) for destroying them during use. The need is a condition. Imam al-Shafi'i dissents, arguing that a Muslim's property cannot be used without consent. The Hanafi response provides two proofs: (1) The practice of Ali (RA) distributing weapons among his companions at Basra as a distribution of need (not ownership). (2) The prophetic precedent of taking Safwan's armor without his consent as a loan ('āriyah) for war. Since a rebel's property is less protected than a just Muslim's property (due to rebellion), allowing use in need is even more justified. If weapons are not needed, they are detained like other wealth. For horses, they are sold and the price detained because horses require upkeep and may be better preserved that way. The text also rules that dhimmīs who assist the rebels have the same status as rebels: their wealth is protected and they are not to be enslaved as their covenant remains intact.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation; here, the imam is not liable if weapons/horses are destroyed during use.
كراع (kura'): Horses (military mounts).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling regarding using rebels' weapons in battle when the imam is in need?
A) Prohibited because it is Muslim property.
B) Permissible and the imam is not liable for damages.
C) Permissible only if the owner consents.
D) The imam must pay compensation immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the imam to use rebels' weapons and horses in battle when needed, with no liability for damage, based on prophetic example and the principle of necessity overriding individual property rights.

No Liability When a Rebel Kills Another Rebel
Ruling on qisas and diya when a rebel kills another rebel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a rebel kills another rebel and explain the qiyas reasoning that distinguishes this case from killing in Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
A rebel's blood is unprotected: no qisas or diya when rebels kill each other.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a nuanced ruling in the Hanafi school: when a rebel kills another rebel in their own camp, no retaliation or blood money is due. The text derives this by analogy (qiyas) with killing in Dar al-Harb, but with a critical distinction: unlike the musta'min who retains inviolability, the rebel's blood is considered mubah. Understanding this distinction helps you see how the Hanafi school prioritizes the concept of ismah (inviolability) in criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
The ruling is that no qisas or diya is due when a rebel kills another rebel. The condition: both are rebels in active rebellion, and the imam has no jurisdiction at the time.
2
Locate the primary proof (dalil)
The text cites two reasons: (1) impossibility of qisas because the imam has no authority over them at the time of killing; (2) diya does not become an alternative because qisas was never obligatory.
3
Analyze the qiyas analogy
The text analogizes to a musta'min killing another musta'min in Dar al-Harb: there qisas is dropped but diya is required due to remaining ismah. Here both ismah and jurisdiction are absent.
The asl is the Dar al-Harb case; the far' is the rebel-on-rebel case.
4
Identify the illa (effective cause) extraction
The illa is the combined absence of imamic authority and loss of inviolability. The extraction method is tanqih al-manat: distinguishing that in Dar al-Harb ismah remains, while in rebellion it is lost.
This is a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) because the shared illa is not explicit.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Some schools argue that intentional killing of a Muslim, even a rebel, requires qisas or at least diya. The Hanafi response is that rebellion strips the killer of the usual protections.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتل باغ مثله إلخ: أي إذا قتل بعض البغاة بعضهم ثم ظهرنا عليهم فليس على القاتل شيء من القصاص والدية، أما القصاص؛ فلأنه لا يمكن استيفاءه إلا بمنعة، ولا ولاية للإمام عليهم حالة القتل، وأما الدية؛ فلأن القصاص لما لم يجب لم ينقلب موجباً بعده مالاً كالقتل في دار الحرب. رمز الحقائق ٤٤٩/١٥] يعني إذا قتل أحد المستأمنين الآخر في دار الحرب سقط القصاص لانقطاع ولاية الإمام، لكنه تجب الدية لبقاء العصمة؛ إذ دخول المسلم دار الحرب لا يوجب سقوط عصمته، بخلاف ما إذا قتل باغ مثله حيث لا يجب شيء؛ لعدم العصمة، سواء كان القتل عمدا أو خطأ. (عيني، فتح) مثله: أي الباغي الآخر في عسكرهم. لم يجب شيء من القصاص والدية لكونه مباح الدم. (ط، عيني) مثله: أي رجلا آخر من أهل المصر عمدا.
And if a rebel kills another like him, etc.: i.e., if some of the rebels kill each other, then we overcome them, then there is nothing upon the killer of qisas or diya. As for qisas, because it cannot be exacted except with protection (man'ah), and the imam has no authority over them at the time of killing. As for diya, because when qisas is not obligatory, it does not become a monetary obligation afterwards, like killing in Dar al-Harb. (Symbol of Realities: 15/449) That is, if one musta'min kills another in Dar al-Harb, qisas is dropped due to the cessation of the imam's authority, but diya becomes obligatory due to remaining inviolability; because a Muslim entering Dar al-Harb does not cause his inviolability to fall. In contrast, when a rebel kills another like him, nothing is obligatory because there is no inviolability, whether the killing is intentional or mistaken. (Ayni, Fath) 'Like him': meaning the other rebel in their army. Nothing is obligatory of qisas and diya because his blood is mubah (unprotected). (T, Ayni) 'Like him': meaning another man from the people of the city, intentionally.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling when a rebel kills another rebel: no qisas (retaliatory execution) and no diya (blood money) is due. The reasoning is twofold: (1) Qisas cannot be applied because the imam has no jurisdiction over the rebels at the time of the killing (they are in rebellion, outside his authority). (2) Diya is not imposed because qisas did not become obligatory, and according to Hanafi principle, diya is only an alternative when qisas is waived but not when qisas was never due. The commentator draws an analogy with killing in Dar al-Harb: if a musta'min (a Muslim residing in non-Muslim territory) kills another musta'min there, qisas is dropped (due to lack of jurisdiction) but diya is required because the victim retains his inviolability as a Muslim. However, for rebels, their blood is deemed unprotected (mubah al-dam) due to their rebellion, so neither qisas nor diya apply, whether intentional or accidental. The text also clarifies 'like him' means another rebel in the same army, and another case where a rebel kills a city dweller (from the loyal population) – that is handled separately with the imam having choice.

Key Terms
مباح الدم (mubah al-dam): One whose blood is legally unprotected; here, a rebel who is in active rebellion.
مستأمن (musta'min): A Muslim who enters Dar al-Harb with an aman (safe conduct).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a rebel (baghi) kills another rebel, what is the ruling?
A) Qisas is obligatory because it is intentional murder.
B) Diya is obligatory because the victim is a Muslim.
C) Neither qisas nor diya are due.
D) The imam may choose between qisas and diya.

Key Takeaway
When a rebel kills another rebel, the Hanafi school imposes no qisas or diya due to loss of inviolability and lack of imamic jurisdiction.

The Transitiveness of Evidence and Intransitiveness of Admission and Contradiction
Principle stating the distinct legal effects of evidence, admission, and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the transitiveness of evidence and intransitiveness of admission and contradiction, and apply it to cases of claim and price recovery.

Core Idea
Evidence binds all; admission only binds the admitter; contradiction cancels both claims.

In judicial proceedings, not all proofs are equal. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between evidence (bayyinah), admission (iqrar), and contradiction (tanaqud). Understanding who is bound by each is crucial for determining rights, especially when a buyer seeks to recover the price from a seller after a third party claims ownership. This lesson unpacks these distinctions using the classic text from Kanz al-Daqaiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three legal categories
The source states: 'Evidence is a transitive proof, not admission and contradiction.' Your first task is to recognize that the passage sets out three distinct categories: bayyinah (evidence), iqrar (admission), and tanaqud (contradiction). Each has a different legal effect.
These categories are not on the same plane: evidence becomes binding through a judge's ruling; admission is self-imposed; contradiction is a defect that prevents acceptance.
2
Explain why evidence is transitive
The commentary explains that evidence only becomes a proof through the judge's ruling, and the judge has general authority. Therefore, once a judgment is passed based on evidence, it binds everyone—not just the litigants. For example, if a buyer proves that a purchased slave is free, the freedom is established against all, including the seller.
Transitivity means the effect extends beyond the parties to the case.
3
Explain why admission is not transitive
Admission is a proof by itself and does not require a judge's ruling. However, the person who admits has authority only over himself, not over others. Therefore, admission only binds the admitter. If a buyer admits that a slave belongs to a third party, that admission does not affect the seller; the buyer cannot use it to recover the price.
The key is the limitation of authority: al-muqirr la wilayah lahu 'ala ghayrihi.
4
Explain why contradiction prevents acceptance
If a person makes two inconsistent statements, the judge cannot prefer one over the other, so both are disregarded. For example, if someone buys a slave girl and then claims she belongs to someone else, his action of buying contradicts his claim. Such contradiction prevents the claim from being accepted, unless the inconsistency is in a subsidiary matter (like freedom) rather than ownership.
Contradiction in ownership claims is fatal; contradiction in attributes may not be.
5
Apply to the buyer-seller scenario
Now apply the principles: If a buyer presents evidence that a purchased item rightfully belongs to a third party, the evidence is transitive, so the buyer recovers the price from the seller. If the buyer merely admits the item belongs to a third party, the admission binds only himself; he cannot recover the price. If the buyer contradicts himself (e.g., buys then claims for another), his claim is rejected.
This is the core practical outcome: evidence leads to price recovery; admission does not; contradiction blocks the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الاستحقاق أي في بيان أحكامه
البينة حجة متعدية، لا الإقرار والتناقض .
باب الاستحقاق [هو طلب الحق، وفي هذا الباب ذكر بيع الفضولي أيضاً. (عيني، مسكين)] ذكر هذا الباب عقيب باب الحقوق للمناسبة التي بينهما لفظاً ومعنى، يعني الاستحقاق طلب الحق، فالطلب يكون بعد الحق لا محالة. (مسكين، عناية البينة حجة متعدية [إلى الغير حتى تظهر في حق كافة الناس. (عيني)] لأنها لا تصير حجة إلا بالقضاء وللقاضي ولاية عامة فينفذ قضاؤه في حق الكافة كما إذا اشترى أمة، فادعى أنها حرة الأصل، أو أنها ملك فلان وقد أعتقها أو دبرها أو استولدها قبل شرائها، والأمة تدعي، وأقام المشتري البينة على دعواه تقبل بينته؛ لأن التناقض في الحرية وفروعها لا يمنع صحة الدعوى، ويرجع المشتري بالثمن على البائع، وإذا ثبت حريتها في حق البائع ثبتت في حق كافة الناس، حتى لا تسمع دعوى أحد، والحاصل أن القضاء بالبينة حجة متعدية إلى الكافة في العتق والنكاح والنسب والولاء، وفي الملك المؤرخ من وقت التاريخ لا قبله. (عيني، فتح) لا الإقرار: [ أي ليس الإقرار بحجة متعدية إلى الغير، حتى يقتصر على المدعي. (عيني)] يعني ليس الإقرار بحجة متعدية؛ لأن الإقرار حجة بنفسه لا يتوقف على القضاء، وللمقر ولاية على نفسه دون غيره، فيقتصر عليه، وهذا أصل لفروع كثيرة منها ما أشار إليها المصنف بقوله مبيعة ولدت إلخ منها: أنه إذا اشترى عبداً ثم ظهر له مستحق بالبينة، فإنه يرجع على البائع بالثمن؛ لأنه يثبت الملك بالبينة من الابتداء، فيتعدى إلى الكل أي البائع والمشتري، ولو أقر المشتري بالعبد لرجل أنه مستحق له لا يرجع بالثمن على البائع؛ لأن إقراره قاصر عليه، فيثبت به الملك في المقر به ضرورة صحة الإخبار وقد اندفعت الضرورة بإثبات الملك للمقر له، فلا يظهر الاستحقاق في حق المشتري في الرجوع بالثمن على البائع، وعند اجتماع الإقرار والبينة يقضى بالإقرار على الأظهر إلا عند الحاجة فبالبينة والحاجة رجوعه بالثمن. رمز الحقائق: ٦٤/٢] (فتح) والتناقض يقال: تناقض الكلامان تدافعا كأن كل واحد نقض الآخر، وفي كلامه تناقض إذا كان بعضه يقتضي إبطال بعض، واختلفوا في اشتراط كون الكلام عند القاضي فمنهم من شرطه ومنهم من شرط كون الثاني عند القاضي فقط، كما لو اشترى أمة ثم ادعى أنها ملك فلان وفلان يدعيها، وأقام المشتري بينة على دعواه ليرجع بالثمن لا تقبل؛ لأن إقدامه على الشراء دليل على أنها ملك البائع، فإذا ادعى لغيره كان متناقضا، ولو برهن على إقرار البائع أنه ملك فلان يقبل؛ لعدم التناقض، وإنما يمنع التناقض في الكلام دعوى الملك؛ لأن القاضي لا يمكنه أن يحكم بالكلام المتناقض؛ إذ أحدهما ليس بأولى من الآخر فسقطا، وهذا أيضاً أصل لفروع كثيرة وموضعها في كتاب الدعوى من "المبسوط". (عيني، فتح)
Chapter of Claim (Istihqaq): i.e., explaining its rulings.
Evidence is a transitive proof, not admission and contradiction.
Chapter of Claim: it is seeking a right, and in this chapter mention of the sale by a non-owner (fuduli) is also included. (Ayni, Miskin) This chapter is placed after the chapter of rights due to the linguistic and conceptual connection between them: claim is seeking a right, and seeking necessarily comes after the right. (Miskin, Inayah)
Evidence is transitive [to others such that it appears in the right of all people. (Ayni)] because it only becomes a proof through judgment, and the judge has general authority, so his judgment is effective regarding all, as if one buys a slave girl and claims she is originally free, or that she belongs to so-and-so who freed her, made a tadbir, or fathered a child before purchase, and the slave girl claims, and the buyer presents evidence for his claim, his evidence is accepted; because contradiction regarding freedom and its branches does not prevent the validity of the claim, and the buyer recovers the price from the seller. When her freedom is proven against the seller, it is proven against all people, so that no one's claim is heard. The conclusion: judgment based on evidence is transitive to all in matters of emancipation, marriage, lineage, and wala', and in ownership dated from the time of the date not before. (Ayni, Fath)
Not admission: [i.e., admission is not a transitive proof to others, so it is confined to the claimant. (Ayni)] Meaning, admission is not a transitive proof because admission is a proof by itself, not dependent on judgment, and the one admitting has authority over himself but not others, so it is confined to him. This is a principle for many branches, including what the author indicated by his statement: 'a sold item that gave birth' etc. Among them: if one buys a slave and then a claimant appears with evidence, he recovers the price from the seller because ownership is proven by evidence from the beginning, so it extends to all (seller and buyer). If the buyer admits to a man that the slave belongs to him (the claimant), he does not recover the price from the seller because his admission is limited to him; ownership is proven for the one admitted to by necessity of the truthfulness of the statement, but the necessity is removed by establishing ownership for the claimant, so the claim does not appear in the right of the buyer to recover the price from the seller. When admission and evidence coexist, judgment is given by admission according to the most apparent view, except when there is need, then by evidence, and the need is his recovery of the price. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 64/2) (Fath)
And contradiction: it is said that when two statements conflict as if each invalidates the other, and in his speech there is contradiction if part necessitates nullification of another. They differed on the condition of the speech being before the judge: some condition it, some only condition the second statement before the judge. For example, if one buys a slave girl and then claims she belongs to so-and-so, and that person claims her, and the buyer presents evidence for his claim to recover the price, it is not accepted because his action of purchase indicates she is the seller's property; so if he claims for another, he is contradictory. If he provides evidence on the seller's admission that she belongs to so-and-so, it is accepted due to lack of contradiction. Contradiction only prevents claims of ownership because the judge cannot rule on contradictory speech; since one is not more preferable than the other, both fall. This is also a principle for many branches, and their place is in the Book of Claims in al-Mabsut. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq introduces the chapter on Istihqaq (claim) and states a fundamental principle: 'Evidence is transitive; admission and contradiction are not.' The commentary explains that judicial evidence (bayyinah) presented before a judge becomes a proof that extends to all people because the judge has general authority; once a judgment is made, it binds everyone. For example, if a buyer presents evidence that a purchased slave girl is free or belongs to another, the judgment applies to all, and the buyer can recover the price from the seller. In contrast, admission (iqrar) is not transitive: it only binds the person who admits. If a buyer admits that a slave belongs to a third party, he cannot later claim the price back from the seller because his admission is limited to himself. When both admission and evidence coexist, the default is to give precedence to admission unless there is a need (such as recovering the price), in which case evidence is used. Contradiction (tanaqud) occurs when a person makes inconsistent statements, such as buying a slave girl and then claiming she belongs to someone else. Such contradictory statements are not accepted as evidence because the judge cannot prefer one over the other. However, if the contradiction is not in claiming ownership but in establishing a subordinate matter like freedom, it does not prevent the claim. This principle has many applications in fiqh.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Judicial evidence presented in court, which becomes a proof through the judge's ruling.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission or confession by a party, which is a proof only against the admitter himself.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction in one's statements that undermines credibility in claiming ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer admits that a purchased slave belongs to a third party, can he still recover the price from the seller?
A) Yes, because his admission is evidence.
B) No, because admission is not transitive and does not affect the seller.
C) Yes, but only if the third party confirms.
D) No, unless the seller also admits.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, evidence binds everyone once adjudicated; admission binds only the admitter; and contradiction in ownership claims invalidates them.

Contradiction Does Not Bar Claims of Freedom, Divorce, Lineage
Explaining the principle that tanāquḍ does not block da'wa in three specific cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that contradiction (tanāquḍ) does not bar a claim in three specific areas (freedom, divorce, lineage) and explain the underlying rationale of concealment (khafā').

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a contradictory claim is admissible if the matter was hidden from the claimant – applied to freedom, divorce, and lineage.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi rule in qada': that a claimant's prior contradictory conduct does not automatically invalidate a claim of freedom, divorce, or lineage. We will explore why the Hanafi school excuses contradiction in these three areas based on the principle that the true state of affairs was hidden from the claimant at the time of the earlier action. Understanding this principle is crucial for evaluating the admissibility of evidence in Islamic courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The text states: 'Contradiction does not prevent a claim of ownership but does not prevent claims of freedom, divorce, and lineage.' Recognize the general rule and the three exceptions.
The general rule is that contradiction bars a claim, but these three are exceptions.
2
Analyze the case of freedom
Understand the example: buying a slave-girl then claiming she was freed by someone else. Note the rationale: manumission is exclusive to the master, so it may be hidden.
The slave may not know of his manumission until later.
3
Analyze the case of divorce
Understand the example: woman initiates khul' then claims she was already divorced thrice. Note the restriction to triple divorce and why.
For less than three, the husband could claim remarriage.
4
Analyze the case of lineage
Understand the example: seller of a slave later claims the slave is his son. Note rationale: lineage based on conception, which is hidden.
Lineage is established by birth, which may be unknown to the father initially.
5
Synthesize the common rationale
All three cases share the element of khafā' (concealment). Contradiction is excused to avoid hardship (haraj).
If the matter were open and known, contradiction would bar the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمنع دعوى الملك لا الحرية والطلاق والنسب

لا الحرية: أي التناقض لا يمنع دعوى الحرية، كما لو اشترى أمة وقبضها ثم ادعى أنها معتقة فلان، وأقام البينة، تقبل، ويرجع بالثمن على البائع وكما لو ادعى المكاتب بدل الكتابة، ثم برهن على أن مولاه أعتقه قبل الكتابة، فإنه تقبل بينته، ورجع بما أدى، فالتناقض لا يمنع صحة الدعوى في دعوى العتق؛ لأنه أمر يجري فيه الخفاء؛ إذ هو أمر يتفرد به المولى، فربما لا يعلم العبد إعتاقه، ثم يعلم بعد ذلك، فيعفى التناقض دفعاً للحرج عن العبد.

والطلاق أي ولا يمنع التناقض دعوى الطلاق، كالمرأة إذا اختلعت نفسها من زوجها، ثم أقامت بينة على أن زوجها طلقها ثلاثاً قبل الخلع، فإنه تقبل بينتها، ولها أن تسترد بدل الخلع؛ لأنها وإن كانت متناقضة؛ إذ إقدامها على الخلع يدل على عدم الطلاق فتكون بدعوى الطلاق متناقضة، ومع هذا يعفى التناقض لاستقلال الزوج بإيقاع الثلاث عليها من غير أن يكون لها علم بذلك، وقيدنا المسألة بالثلاث؛ لأن في ما دون الثلاث يمكن أن يقيم الزوج البينة أنه قد تزوجها بعد الطلاق الذي أثبتته المرأة ببينتها قبل يوم أو يومين، وأما في الثلاث فلا يمكن.

والنسب: أي ولا يمنع التناقض أيضاً دعوى النسب، كما إذا باع عبداً ولد عنده، وقبضه المشتري وباعه المشتري من آخر، ثم ادعاه البائع الأول أنه ابنه تسمع دعواه، ويبطل البيع الأول والثاني؛ لأنه يدعي البنوة، فعلم حرا، وبيع الحر باطل وعدم منع التناقض في دعوى النسب أن النسب يبتني على العلوق، وهو مما يخفى فيعفى فيه التناقض.
Contradiction does not prevent a claim of ownership but does not prevent [claims of] freedom, divorce, and lineage. [Commentary:] Freedom: meaning contradiction does not prevent a claim of freedom, such as if someone bought a slave-girl and took possession, then claimed she was freed by so-and-so and presented evidence, it is accepted and he recovers the price from the seller. Similarly, if a mukatib claimed the substitution for kitabah, then proved that his master freed him before the kitabah, his evidence is accepted and he recovers what he paid. Contradiction does not prevent the validity of the claim in a claim of manumission because it is a matter in which concealment occurs; it is something exclusive to the master, so perhaps the slave did not know of his manumission, then learns of it afterward, so contradiction is excused to remove hardship from the slave. Divorce: meaning contradiction does not prevent a claim of divorce, such as a woman who sought khul' from her husband, then presented evidence that her husband divorced her three times before the khul', her evidence is accepted and she may recover the khul' compensation. Even though she is contradictory—her action of khul' indicates no divorce—contradiction is excused because the husband independently effected the triple divorce without her knowledge. We restrict the case to triple divorce because for less than three, the husband could present evidence that he remarried her after the divorce she proved. Lineage: meaning contradiction also does not prevent a claim of lineage, such as if someone sold a slave born in his possession, the buyer took possession and sold him to another, then the first seller claimed the slave is his son, his claim is heard and the first and second sales are void. The lack of contradiction in lineage claims is because lineage is based on conception, which is hidden, so contradiction is excused.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that in Hanafi law, a claimant's prior contradictory conduct does not bar the admissibility of a claim in three specific areas: freedom, divorce, and lineage. The reason is that these are matters often hidden from the claimant. The text provides examples: a buyer who first treats a slave-girl as property can later claim she was freed by someone, because manumission is a private act; a wife who initiates khul' can later claim she was already divorced thrice, because divorce is in the husband's exclusive knowledge; and a seller who sold a slave as property can later claim the slave is his son, because lineage is based on conception which is hidden. The commentary stresses that contradiction is excused to avoid hardship, as the claimant may not have known the true state at the time of the earlier action. The ruling applies only when the subject matter is inherently concealed; if the contradiction involves something the claimant clearly knew, the claim would be barred.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): Contradiction in a claimant's prior actions or statements that seem inconsistent with the current claim.
الخفاء (al-khafā'): Concealment or hidden nature of the matter, such that the claimant could plausibly be unaware of the truth at the time of the earlier action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Hanafi law excuse contradiction (tanāquḍ) in a claim of manumission?
A) Because manumission is a form of worship.
B) Because manumission is done by the master alone and may be hidden from the slave.
C) Because the slave can always claim freedom.
D) Because contradiction is never considered.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, contradiction does not bar claims of freedom, divorce, or lineage because these matters are inherently hidden, and excusing contradiction avoids hardship.

Confessions and Their Conditions: Valid vs Invalid
Rulings on confession (iqrar) with condition or suspension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid conditions attached to a confession of debt according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A confession cannot be suspended by an implausible condition but is valid when conditioned on death or time.

Confession (iqrar) is a serious legal admission. The Hanafi school draws a clear line: conditions that depend on unlikely natural events render the confession void. However, conditions tied to death or a specific time are valid and interpreted as a precaution against denial or to set a term. This lesson unpacks why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the attached condition
Recognize that the act is iqrar (confession) and the condition is an explicit clause (e.g., 'if it rains').
2
Classify the type of condition
Determine whether the condition refers to an implausible natural event (void) or a foreseeable boundary (death/time) – valid.
Implausible events: rain, wind. Foreseeable: death, arrival of a time.
3
Understand the legal rationale
Confession is an immediate admission; suspending it by an implausible condition contradicts its nature. Death/time conditions are seen as defensive measures against denial or to set a term.
Hanafi justification: the condition shows caution, not suspension of the admission.
4
Apply the rule to new conditions
Test borderline cases: 'if you arrive tomorrow' – is it valid? It is akin to a time condition, so valid. 'if the sun rises from the west' – impossible, thus void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإقرار: بأن قال: لفلان علي ألف درهم إن أمطرت السماء، أو هبت الريح، بخلاف ما إذا علق الإقرار بموته أو بمجيء الوقت، فإنه يجوز، ويحمل على أنه فعل ذلك؛ للاحتراز عن الجحود أو دعوى الأجل، فيلزمه للحال.
And the confession: such that he says, 'So-and-so has a thousand dirhams due from me if the sky rains,' or 'if the wind blows.' Contrary to when he suspends the confession by his death or by the arrival of time, then it is permissible, and it is interpreted that he did that to guard against denial or a claim of term, so it becomes binding immediately.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage discusses iqrar (confession of debt) with conditions. A confession suspended by an impossible or unlikely condition (like rain or wind) is invalid because confession is not something that can be suspended arbitrarily. However, if the confession is conditioned upon the confessor's death or a specific time, it is valid. The rationale is that such conditions are meant to prevent the confessor from later denying the debt or to establish a term of payment. The confession is then considered effective immediately (the debt is due at the specified time or at death). This shows that not all conditions render a confession invalid; some are interpreted as motivated by caution.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A statement acknowledging a right or debt in favor of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, which of the following confessions is valid?
A) 'I owe you a thousand if it rains.'
B) 'I owe you a thousand when I die.'
C) 'I owe you a thousand if the wind blows.'
D) 'I owe you a thousand if the sky falls.'

Key Takeaway
A confession of debt is void if attached to an implausible condition, but valid if conditioned on death or a specific time, binding immediately.

Conditional Arbitration in Hanafi Fiqh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional arbitration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on conditional arbitration and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Conditional arbitration is valid; the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf's view.

This passage presents a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether arbitration (tahkim) can be suspended by a condition. While both imams agree on the validity of such conditional arbitration, they differ in their legal reasoning. The operative ruling for fatwa follows Abu Yusuf's position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on conditional arbitration
Identify the common ground: both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that arbitration can be suspended by a condition. The ruling is valid according to both, but the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view.
Note that Abu Yusuf's view is simpler and relied upon.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant
Determine the differences: Abu Yusuf holds that conditional arbitration is valid unconditionally; Muhammad provides a more nuanced reasoning by analogy to agency and the dual nature of arbitration (appointment and settlement).
Muhammad's reasoning shows that the validity depends on which aspect is emphasized.
3
Analyze the proofs
Examine the arguments: Abu Yusuf's proof is based on analogy to agency (wakala), which can be conditional. Muhammad's argument acknowledges that arbitration has two aspects: appointment (can be conditional) and settlement (cannot be uncertain), but he resolves the tension by prioritizing the appointment aspect.
The dual nature is key to understanding the dispute.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Confirm that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view, as stated explicitly in the text: 'upon it is the fatwa'. This means that in practice, conditional arbitration is valid without the additional condition that the appointment aspect must be dominant.
The tarjih is based on reliance on Abu Yusuf's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحكيم: كرجلين قالا لآخر: إن قدم فلان فاحكم بيننا في هذه الحادثة، أو قالا لعبد أو كافر: إذا أعتقت أو أسلمت فاحكم بيننا، فهذا يصح عند أبي يوسف، وعليه الفتوى. وقال محمد: يجوز تعليقه بالشرط وإضافته إلى زمان، فصار كالوكالة. وله: أن التحكيم تولية صورة وصلح معنى؛ إذ لا يصار إليه إلا بتراضيهما بقطع الخصومة، فباعتبار الأول يصح تعليقه، وبالاعتبار الثاني لا يصح، ولا يصح بالشك والاحتمال.
And arbitration: like two men saying to another: 'If Zaid comes, then arbitrate between us in this incident,' or they say to a slave or an unbeliever: 'When you are freed or embrace Islam, then arbitrate between us.' This is valid according to Abu Yusuf, and upon it is the fatwa. And Muhammad said: Its suspension by condition and addition to a time is permissible, so it becomes like agency. And he has [the argument]: that arbitration is appointment in form and peace in meaning; because it is only resorted to by their mutual consent to cut off litigation. So by the first consideration, its suspension is valid; by the second consideration, it is not. And it is not valid with doubt and probability.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the validity of conditional arbitration (tahkim). Abu Yusuf holds that arbitration can be suspended by a condition, and this is the relied-upon position (upon it is the fatwa). Muhammad al-Shaybani also allows it, arguing that arbitration is like agency (wakala) because it involves delegation of authority. However, the text notes that arbitration has a dual nature: it is an appointment (tawliya) in form and a settlement (sulh) in meaning. By the form (appointment), it can be suspended; by the meaning (settlement), it cannot be suspended to avoid uncertainty. But Abu Yusuf's view prevails, and conditional arbitration is valid.

Key Terms
تحكيم (tahkim): Arbitration where disputants appoint a third party to resolve a conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi imam's view on conditional arbitration is the basis for fatwa?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Zufar

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, arbitration can be suspended by a condition; the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf's view that it is valid.

Qualifications of a Judge: Shurut and the Relation to Testimony
Defining qualifications for judgeship and linking to testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions (shurut) for a judge and explain the Hanafi rule that whoever is fit for testimony is fit for judgeship, but not vice versa.

Core Idea
A judge must have the same qualifications as a witness, but a faasiq can still be a judge.

The Book of Judging begins by defining qada and outlining who can serve as a judge. The matn states a key rule: 'a person fit for testimony is fit for judgeship,' but the converse is not absolute. This lesson covers the conditions for a judge and how they relate to the qualifications for a witness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for a judge
List the conditions mentioned: intellect, maturity, Islam, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, freedom from hadd qadhf.
These are the shurut for the validity of the judge's appointment.
2
Understand the relationship with testimony
Explain the rule that whoever is fit to testify is fit to judge, but not vice versa. Consider examples: an enemy's testimony is not accepted, yet he can be a judge.
The rule is one-directional: fitness for testimony is sufficient but not necessary for judgeship.
3
Apply the conditions to real cases
For each condition, consider a person lacking it: a blind person cannot be judge, a slave cannot be judge, etc.
The deaf person is an exception: some say he can be judge if he hears loud sounds.
4
Note the exception for the deaf person
The text mentions a difference of opinion on the judgeship of a deaf person.

Source Text

كتاب القضاء هو في اللغة الإتقان والإحكام، وفي الشرع : فصل الخصومات وقطع المنازعات. (عيني)] لما كان أكثر المنازعات تقع في الديون والمبايعات أعقبها بما يقطعها، والمراد بالقضاء الحكم، وهو أفضل العبادات، وبه أمر كل نبي، ونصب القاضي فرض ونصب الإمام الأعظم فرض بلا خلاف بين أهل الحق؛ لإجماع الصحابة على ذلك، والأولى في تعريفه أن يقال: هو قول ملزم صدر عن ولاية عامة وشرط صحة الحكم: أن يكون بعد تقدم دعوى صحيحة من خصم على خصم ولا يشترط له المصر، فالقضاء بالسواد صحيح، وبه يفتى، ولا يشترط أن يكون المتداعيان من بلد القاضي، ويشترط في الحاكم العقل والبلوغ، والإسلام والحرية، والسمع والبصر، والنطق والسلامة عن حد القذف، وأن يكون مولى الحكم دون سماع الدعوى فقط. (عيني، فتح) أهله إلخ [ أي من هو أهل للشهادة، فكل من صلح شاهدا صلح قاضيا ومن لا فلا. والفاسق إلخ: [ أي المسلم الذي أقدم على كبيرة أو أصر على صغيرة. (معدن)] وقيل: لا يصح قضاؤه أصلاً؛ لأنه لا يؤمن عليه لفسقه، وهو قول الثلاثة واختيار الطحاوي، وينبغي أن يفتى به خصوصاً في هذا الزمان. [رمز الحقائق: ١١٥/٢] قال في "النهر" [٥٩٧/٣]: ولو اعتبر هذا لانسد باب القضاء خصوصاً في زماننا، فلهذا كان ما جرى عليه المصنف هو الأصح، وقوله: "إلا أنه لا ينبغي أن يقلد"؛ لأنه لا يؤمن في أمر الدين؛ لقلة مبالاته فيه كما في حكم الشهادة، فإنه لا ينبغي أن يقبل القاضي شهادته، ولو قبل جاز عندنا بناء على أن العدالة ليست من شرائط الشهادة نظراً إلى أهل ذلك العصر الذين شهد لهم رسول الله الله بالخيرية، وإلى ظاهر حال المسلم في غيرهم.
ثم اعلم أنه اختلف الروايات في تقليد الفاسق القضاء، والأصح أنه يصح التقليد، ولا ينعزل بالفسق بقاء، ثم قال في "المحيط": ويستحق العزل عند عامة المشايخ إلا إذا شرط في التقليد أنه متى جار، ينعزل، وعند الشافعي: ينعزل بالفسق، والإمام يصير إماماً مع الفسق ولا ينعزل بالفسق بلا خلاف؛ لأن الإمارة والسلطنة مبنية على القهر والغلبة، وفي نوادر" هشام: قال محمد: لو فسق القاضي ثم تاب فهو على قضائه، وفي "الأجناس": ينعزل القاضي بفسقه، ولا ينعزل الخليفة بفسقه وفي "آداب القاضي" للحسن بن زياد قاض مكث وهو عدل، ثم فسق بعد ذلك ،وارتشى، وقد كان قضى بقضايا قبل أن يفسق وبقضايا بعد ما فسق بطل كل قضية قضى بها =
Book of Judging. 401. Rulings of Judging. Book of Judging: i.e., in explaining its rulings. So, its people are those fit for testimony, and the sinner is fit for judging. i.e., the people of judging, i.e., for ruling over Muslims. Book of Judging: linguistically, it means proficiency and firmness; legally, it means settling disputes and cutting off conflicts. When most conflicts occur in matters of debts and sales, it follows them with what cuts them off. The intended meaning of judging is the ruling, and it is the best of acts of worship; every prophet was commanded with it. Appointment of a judge is a communal obligation, and appointment of the supreme leader is an obligation without dispute among the people of truth due to the consensus of the Companions. The best definition is: it is a binding statement issued from a general authority. The condition for the validity of a ruling is that it be after a valid claim from one opponent against another, and being in a city is not a condition; so judging in rural areas is valid, and it is the fatwa. It is not a condition that the two litigants be from the city of the judge. The conditions for the judge are: intellect, maturity, Islam, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, and being free from the punishment of false accusation, and that he be given authority over the ruling, not just hearing the case. 'Its people, etc.' i.e., whoever is fit for testimony: everyone qualified to be a witness is qualified to be a judge, and whoever is not is not. Because each of them establishes authority over others: the witness, by his testimony, obligates the judge to rule, and the judge, by his ruling, obligates the opponent. So whoever is fit to be a witness is fit to be a judge, though the converse is not absolute; for the judgeship may be valid for someone who is not fit to be a witness (like the testimony of an enemy is not accepted). So the authority of a boy, a madman, a slave, a blind person, and a deaf person who only hears loud sounds is not permissible. And his judging: it is said that the judging of a deaf person is permissible. 'And the sinner, etc.' i.e., a Muslim who commits a major sin or persists in a minor sin. And it is said: his judging is not valid at all because he cannot be trusted due to his fisq. This is the saying of the three and the choice of al-Tahawi, and it should be given fatwa especially in this time. He said in al-Nahr: If this were considered, the door of judging would be closed especially in our time; for this reason, what the author went with is more correct. His statement: 'Except that he should not be appointed' because he cannot be trusted in matters of religion due to his lack of care. This is like the rule of testimony: a judge should not accept his testimony, but if he accepts it, it is permissible according to us, based on the fact that uprightness is not a condition for testimony, looking at the people of that era whom the Messenger of Allah testified to be good, and the apparent state of a Muslim among others. Then know that the narrations differ regarding appointing a faasiq to the judiciary. The most sound is that the appointment is valid and he is not removed from office by his fisq. Then he said in al-Muhit: He deserves removal according to the generality of the mashayikh unless it was stipulated in the appointment that if he acts unjustly he is removed. According to al-Shafi'i: he is removed by fisq. The imam (caliph) becomes imam even with fisq and is not removed by fisq without dispute, because leadership and authority are based on domination and force. In Nawadir Hisham: Muhammad said: If a judge becomes a faasiq and then repents, he remains in his judging. In al-Ajnas: The judge is removed by his fisq, but the caliph is not removed by his fisq. In Adab al-Qadi of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: A judge was upright, then he became a faasiq and took bribes. He had made rulings before his fisq and after his fisq. Every ruling he made after fisq is void.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage from the beginning of Book of Judging in Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the subject and discusses qualifications. The matn states two key principles: (1) Whoever is qualified to be a witness is qualified to be a judge; (2) A faasiq (sinner) is qualified to be a judge. The commentary expands: the definition of qada' (judging) is settling disputes. It is a fard kifayah (communal obligation). Conditions for a judge include: Islam, sanity, maturity, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, and being free from hadd qadhf (false accusation). The condition of 'adalah (uprightness) is not required for validity, though it is preferable. The central dispute: Can a faasiq be a judge? Some Hanafi authorities (the 'three', i.e., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad? Actually 'qawl al-thalathah' often means Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad? But here it seems they hold that the faasiq's judgment is invalid. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in later fatwa is that the faasiq's judgeship is valid, but he should not be appointed due to lack of trust. The proof is analogy with testimony: a faasiq's testimony is not accepted but his judging is valid because justice would otherwise be impossible. The passage also discusses the caliph: he is not removed by fisq because his authority is based on domination.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qadā'): Legally: settling disputes and cutting off conflicts; a binding statement issued from general authority.
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A Muslim who commits a major sin (kabirah) or persists in a minor sin (saghirah).
الشهادة (al-shahādah): Testimony; giving witness in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition (shart) for a judge?
A) Sight
B) Being free from hadd qadhf
C) Uprightness ('adalah)
D) Freedom

Key Takeaway
A judge must meet specific conditions including Islam, sanity, and sensory abilities, but uprightness ('adalah) is not required for the validity of his appointment.

The Ruling on a Faasiq Judge: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Addressing the permissibility of a faasiq judge and the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a faasiq judge and state the relied-upon position with its rationale.

Core Idea
A faasiq can be a judge: his appointment is valid, though he should not be appointed.

The passage then addresses a controversial issue: Can a sinful person (faasiq) serve as a judge? The matn declares that a faasiq is fit for judgeship, but the commentary reveals a significant intra-Hanafi dispute. This lesson unpacks the arguments of both sides and the final mufta bihi position that balances legal validity with practical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Whether a faasiq can be a judge and whether his rulings are valid.
The matn states he is fit, but some scholars disagree.
2
State the Hanafi position
The relied-upon view: a faasiq can be a judge, his appointment is valid, he is not automatically removed.
This is the position of the author and later fatwa.
3
Present the opposing view
The 'three' (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad?) and al-Tahawi hold that his judgeship is invalid because he cannot be trusted.
The text says 'qawl al-thalathah' which often means the three imams of the school.
4
Analyze the proof for each side
Opposing: faasiq cannot be trusted. Hanafi response: necessity; also the caliph is not removed by fisq.
The text cites al-Nahr: if we invalidated faasiq judges, the door of judging would close.
5
Determine the practical consequence
A faasiq judge's rulings are enforceable; he deserves removal but is not automatically removed.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

كتاب القضاء هو في اللغة الإتقان والإحكام، وفي الشرع : فصل الخصومات وقطع المنازعات. (عيني)] لما كان أكثر المنازعات تقع في الديون والمبايعات أعقبها بما يقطعها، والمراد بالقضاء الحكم، وهو أفضل العبادات، وبه أمر كل نبي، ونصب القاضي فرض ونصب الإمام الأعظم فرض بلا خلاف بين أهل الحق؛ لإجماع الصحابة على ذلك، والأولى في تعريفه أن يقال: هو قول ملزم صدر عن ولاية عامة وشرط صحة الحكم: أن يكون بعد تقدم دعوى صحيحة من خصم على خصم ولا يشترط له المصر، فالقضاء بالسواد صحيح، وبه يفتى، ولا يشترط أن يكون المتداعيان من بلد القاضي، ويشترط في الحاكم العقل والبلوغ، والإسلام والحرية، والسمع والبصر، والنطق والسلامة عن حد القذف، وأن يكون مولى الحكم دون سماع الدعوى فقط.
