[رمز الحقائق: ١١٥/٢] قال في "النهر" [٥٩٧/٣]: ولو اعتبر هذا لانسد باب القضاء خصوصاً في زماننا، فلهذا كان ما جرى عليه المصنف هو الأصح، وقوله: "إلا أنه لا ينبغي أن يقلد"؛ لأنه لا يؤمن في أمر الدين؛ لقلة مبالاته فيه كما في حكم الشهادة، فإنه لا ينبغي أن يقبل القاضي شهادته، ولو قبل جاز عندنا بناء على أن العدالة ليست من شرائط الشهادة نظراً إلى أهل ذلك العصر الذين شهد لهم رسول الله الله بالخيرية، وإلى ظاهر حال المسلم في غيرهم.
ثم اعلم أنه اختلف الروايات في تقليد الفاسق القضاء، والأصح أنه يصح التقليد، ولا ينعزل بالفسق بقاء، ثم قال في "المحيط": ويستحق العزل عند عامة المشايخ إلا إذا شرط في التقليد أنه متى جار، ينعزل، وعند الشافعي: ينعزل بالفسق، والإمام يصير إماماً مع الفسق ولا ينعزل بالفسق بلا خلاف؛ لأن الإمارة والسلطنة مبنية على القهر والغلبة، وفي نوادر" هشام: قال محمد: لو فسق القاضي ثم تاب فهو على قضائه، وفي "الأجناس": ينعزل القاضي بفسقه، ولا ينعزل الخليفة بفسقه وفي "آداب القاضي" للحسن بن زياد قاض مكث وهو عدل، ثم فسق بعد ذلك ،وارتشى، وقد كان قضى بقضايا قبل أن يفسق وبقضايا بعد ما فسق بطل كل قضية قضى بها =
Book of Judging. 401. Rulings of Judging. Book of Judging: i.e., in explaining its rulings. So, its people are those fit for testimony, and the sinner is fit for judging. i.e., the people of judging, i.e., for ruling over Muslims. Book of Judging: linguistically, it means proficiency and firmness; legally, it means settling disputes and cutting off conflicts. When most conflicts occur in matters of debts and sales, it follows them with what cuts them off. The intended meaning of judging is the ruling, and it is the best of acts of worship; every prophet was commanded with it. Appointment of a judge is a communal obligation, and appointment of the supreme leader is an obligation without dispute among the people of truth due to the consensus of the Companions. The best definition is: it is a binding statement issued from a general authority. The condition for the validity of a ruling is that it be after a valid claim from one opponent against another, and being in a city is not a condition; so judging in rural areas is valid, and it is the fatwa. It is not a condition that the two litigants be from the city of the judge. The conditions for the judge are: intellect, maturity, Islam, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, and being free from the punishment of false accusation, and that he be given authority over the ruling, not just hearing the case. 'Its people, etc.' i.e., whoever is fit for testimony: everyone qualified to be a witness is qualified to be a judge, and whoever is not is not. Because each of them establishes authority over others: the witness, by his testimony, obligates the judge to rule, and the judge, by his ruling, obligates the opponent. So whoever is fit to be a witness is fit to be a judge, though the converse is not absolute; for the judgeship may be valid for someone who is not fit to be a witness (like the testimony of an enemy is not accepted). So the authority of a boy, a madman, a slave, a blind person, and a deaf person who only hears loud sounds is not permissible. And his judging: it is said that the judging of a deaf person is permissible. 'And the sinner, etc.' i.e., a Muslim who commits a major sin or persists in a minor sin. And it is said: his judging is not valid at all because he cannot be trusted due to his fisq. This is the saying of the three and the choice of al-Tahawi, and it should be given fatwa especially in this time. He said in al-Nahr: If this were considered, the door of judging would be closed especially in our time; for this reason, what the author went with is more correct. His statement: 'Except that he should not be appointed' because he cannot be trusted in matters of religion due to his lack of care. This is like the rule of testimony: a judge should not accept his testimony, but if he accepts it, it is permissible according to us, based on the fact that uprightness is not a condition for testimony, looking at the people of that era whom the Messenger of Allah testified to be good, and the apparent state of a Muslim among others. Then know that the narrations differ regarding appointing a faasiq to the judiciary. The most sound is that the appointment is valid and he is not removed from office by his fisq. Then he said in al-Muhit: He deserves removal according to the generality of the mashayikh unless it was stipulated in the appointment that if he acts unjustly he is removed. According to al-Shafi'i: he is removed by fisq. The imam (caliph) becomes imam even with fisq and is not removed by fisq without dispute, because leadership and authority are based on domination and force. In Nawadir Hisham: Muhammad said: If a judge becomes a faasiq and then repents, he remains in his judging. In al-Ajnas: The judge is removed by his fisq, but the caliph is not removed by his fisq. In Adab al-Qadi of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: A judge was upright, then he became a faasiq and took bribes. He had made rulings before his fisq and after his fisq. Every ruling he made after fisq is void.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage from the beginning of Book of Judging in Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the subject and discusses qualifications. The matn states two key principles: (1) Whoever is qualified to be a witness is qualified to be a judge; (2) A faasiq (sinner) is qualified to be a judge. The commentary expands: the definition of qada' (judging) is settling disputes. It is a fard kifayah (communal obligation). Conditions for a judge include: Islam, sanity, maturity, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, and being free from hadd qadhf (false accusation). The condition of 'adalah (uprightness) is not required for validity, though it is preferable. The central dispute: Can a faasiq be a judge? Some Hanafi authorities (the 'three', i.e., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad? Actually 'qawl al-thalathah' often means Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad? But here it seems they hold that the faasiq's judgment is invalid. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in later fatwa is that the faasiq's judgeship is valid, but he should not be appointed due to lack of trust. The proof is analogy with testimony: a faasiq's testimony is not accepted but his judging is valid because justice would otherwise be impossible. The passage also discusses the caliph: he is not removed by fisq because his authority is based on domination.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qadā'): Legally: settling disputes and cutting off conflicts; a binding statement issued from general authority.
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A Muslim who commits a major sin (kabirah) or persists in a minor sin (saghirah).
الشهادة (al-shahādah): Testimony; giving witness in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to a judge who becomes a faasiq?
A) He is automatically removed from office
B) He remains judge but deserves removal
C) His appointment becomes invalid from the start
D) Only the caliph is removed, not the judge

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position holds that a faasiq's judgeship is valid and he is not automatically removed, ensuring legal stability even when judges are sinful.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
10 min
Definition of Qada and Qualifications of a Judge
Defines qada, states fasiq eligibility, lists judge conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qada, list the conditions for a qadi, and explain the Hanafi ruling on the eligibility of a fasiq for qada.

Core Idea
A fasiq can be a qadi, but should not be appointed; the door of judiciary must not be closed.

This lesson introduces the Book of Qada in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. We will learn the definition of qada, the obligation of appointing a qadi, and the qualifications required. A central issue is whether a fasiq can serve as a qadi – a point of contention between the Hanafis and Shafi'is. The Hanafi view balances practicality with religious caution.

Guided Path
1
Define qada linguistically and technically
Understand the literal meaning (precision) and the technical definition (separation of disputes and cutting off conflicts).
The author also notes that qada is a binding statement from a general authority.
2
Identify the ruling on appointing a qadi
The appointment of a qadi is fard kifayah (collective obligation) based on ijma of the Companions.
This is a matter of consensus among Ahl al-Haq.
3
Determine the criterion for eligibility
The text states: 'its people are eligible for testimony' – whoever is eligible to be a witness is eligible to be a qadi.
This is a key principle: shahada eligibility is the benchmark, but the reverse does not fully hold.
4
List the conditions (shurut) for a qadi
Intellect, adulthood, Islam, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, safety from hadd of qadhf, and being entrusted with judgment (not just hearing cases).
These are derived from the text and commentaries.
5
Evaluate the dispute on the fasiq qadi
The text holds that a fasiq is eligible for qada, but should not be appointed. This is practical to avoid closing the door of judiciary.
The opposing view (Shafi'i) requires uprightness; the Hanafis rely on the principle that uprightness is not required for testimony.

Book of Qada: i.e., explaining its rulings. So-and-so said: its people are eligible for testimony, and the fasiq is eligible for qada. i.e., the people of qada, i.e., to judge over Muslims. Qada in language is precision and firmness, and in Sharia: separation of disputes and cutting off of conflicts. (Ayni)] Since most disputes occur in debts and sales, it follows them with what cuts them off, and the intention by qada is the judgment, and it is the best of acts of worship, and every prophet was commanded with it, and the appointment of a qadi is fard and the appointment of the supreme imam is fard without disagreement among the people of truth; due to the consensus of the Companions on that. And the better in its definition is to say: it is a binding statement issued from a general authority, and the condition for the validity of the judgment is that it be after the presentation of a valid claim from one opponent against another, and for it, the [city] boundary is not stipulated, so judgment based on blackness (writing) is valid, and it is given fatwa, and it is not stipulated that the two litigants be from the qadi's city. And it is stipulated in the judge: intellect, reaching the age of majority, Islam, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, and safety from the hadd penalty of qadhf, and that he be entrusted with judgment without only hearing the claim. (Ayni, Fath) 'Its people etc.' [i.e., whoever is eligible for testimony, so everyone who is suitable as a witness is suitable as a qadi, and whoever is not, then not. (T, Ayni)] because each of them establishes authority over others, so the witness by his testimony obliges the judge to judge, and the judge by his judgment obliges the opponent, so whoever is suitable as a witness is suitable as a qadi, but it does not completely reverse; since it is valid for qada someone who is not suitable for testimony, like the testimony of an enemy is not accepted, but [his qada] is valid, so the appointment of a minor, a madman, a slave, a blind person, and a deaf person who hears strong voices is not permissible, and his qada [is not valid] and it is said: the qada of the deaf person is permissible. (Ayni, Fath) And the fasiq etc.: [ i.e., the Muslim who commits a major sin or persists in a minor sin. (Maden)] And it is said: his qada is not valid at all, because one is not secure from him due to his fisq, and it is the saying of the three (imams) and the choice of al-Tahawi, and it is appropriate to give fatwa with it especially in this time. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 115/2] He said in al-Nahr [597/3]: If this were considered, the door of qada would be closed especially in our time, so for this reason what the author followed is the most correct. And his statement: 'However, he should not be appointed' because he is not trustworthy in matters of religion, due to his lack of care in it, as in the ruling of testimony, so the qadi should not accept his testimony, and if he accepted, it is permissible according to us based on the fact that uprightness is not a condition of testimony, considering the people of that era whom the Messenger of Allah (Peace be upon him) attested to goodness, and considering the apparent state of the Muslim regarding others.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Qada in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It first defines qada linguistically and technically, emphasizing that the appointment of a qadi is a collective obligation (fard kifayah) based on consensus of the Companions. The text then presents the key rule: a judge must be eligible for testimony, meaning that the same qualifications required for a witness are required for a judge. However, being a fasiq does not render one ineligible for qada, though it is strongly discouraged and he should not be appointed. The commentary clarifies that while a fasiq's qada is valid, his appointment is not recommended due to lack of religious concern. The passage lists the conditions for a qadi: sanity, adulthood, Islam, freedom, hearing, sight, speech, and being free from the hadd penalty of false accusation. The deaf person is discussed separately. The commentary relies on the principle that uprightness is not a condition for witness testimony, citing the practice of the Companions and the apparent good state of a Muslim.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada): In Sharia: separating disputes and cutting off conflicts; also the judgment itself.
الشهادة (ash-shahada): Testimony in court; the ability to testify is seen as the criterion for being a judge.
فاسق (fasiq): A Muslim who commits a major sin or persists in a minor sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on appointing a fasiq as a qadi according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) It is invalid and his judgments are void.
B) It is valid but he should not be appointed.
C) It is valid and recommended.
D) It is invalid unless he repents.

Key Takeaway
The eligibility for qada is tied to eligibility for testimony; a fasiq is eligible but appointment is discouraged to maintain religious integrity.

Qada — Judiciary

The Fasiq Judge – Validity of Appointment and Dismissal
Text discusses appointment of a fasiq judge, effect of fisq on tenure, and validity of his judgments with intra and inter-madhhab differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a fasiq judge is not automatically dismissed but deserves dismissal, distinguish the Shafi'i objection, and explain how fisq affects the validity of his judgments under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A fasiq judge is not automatically dismissed but deserves dismissal; his judgments remain valid under Hanafi law.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a judge who becomes fasiq after appointment. While some schools, like the Shafi'i, hold that fisq automatically terminates the judgeship, the Hanafi school maintains that the appointment remains valid, though the judge deserves dismissal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of a fasiq judge's judgments and for practical application in Islamic courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the appointment of a fasiq as qadi and the effect of fisq on his tenure. The ruling is that the appointment is valid, and he is not automatically dismissed.
The passage begins by stating the most correct opinion: the appointment is valid and he is not dismissed by fisq.
2
State the Hanafi position
The qadi is not dismissed by fisq alone, but he deserves dismissal (istahaqqa al-azal). This is the zahir al-riwayah position, though some Hanafi works (e.g., al-Ajnas) say he is dismissed.
The text mentions that the ruler must dismiss him, but the judge's own fisq does not automatically nullify his tenure.
3
Identify the opposing school and their position
Al-Shafi'i holds that the fasiq judge is automatically dismissed by fisq, and his judgments are void.
The passage contrasts the Hanafi view with that of al-Shafi'i.
4
Analyse the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is based on the principle that appointment is not conditional on continued uprightness; analogy is drawn with the caliph, who is not dismissed by fisq because sovereignty is built on force.
The text also cites Nawadir Hisham that if the qadi repents, he remains in his post.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that under Hanafi, a fasiq judge's judgments are valid and enforceable unless overturned; under Shafi'i, they are void. This affects the status of past rulings.
The passage includes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether only post-fisq judgments are void or all if the judge was always fasiq.

ثم اعلم أنه اختلف الروايات في تقليد الفاسق القضاء، والأصح أنه يصح التقليد، ولا ينعزل بالفسق بقاء، ثم قال في "المحيط": ويستحق العزل عند عامة المشايخ إلا إذا شرط في التقليد أنه متى جار، ينعزل، وعند الشافعي: ينعزل بالفسق، والإمام يصير إماماً مع الفسق ولا ينعزل بالفسق بلا خلاف؛ لأن الإمارة والسلطنة مبنية على القهر والغلبة، وفي نوادر" هشام: قال محمد: لو فسق القاضي ثم تاب فهو على قضائه، وفي "الأجناس": ينعزل القاضي بفسقه، ولا ينعزل الخليفة بفسقه وفي "آداب القاضي" للحسن بن زياد قاض مكث وهو عدل، ثم فسق بعد ذلك ،وارتشى، وقد كان قضى بقضايا قبل أن يفسق وبقضايا بعد ما فسق بطل كل قضية قضى بها بعد ما فسق، ونفذت القضايا التي قضى بها قبل أن يفسق، وقال الشيخان لو أن قاضياً قضى بين الناس زماناً، وأنفذ قضايا كثيرة، ثم علم أنه فاسق مرتش لم يزل منذ ولي على ذلك كان ينبغي للقاضي الذي يختصمون إليه يبطل كل قضية قضى بها ذلك القاضي، ونقل الحموي عن "المفتاح" : أن كلمة "ينبغي" تستعمل فيما دون الواجب وفوق المستحب. (عيني، فتح، عناية) أهل للشهادة: أي لا دائماً على معنى أن القاضي لو قضى بشهادته نفذ وأثم أن يقلد: لأنه لا يؤمن عليه فكيف يؤمن منه على غيره. (عيني) ففسق [ أي صار فاسقا بأخذ الرشوة، أو شرب الخمر، أو الزنا أو غيرها بعد كونه عدلا. (نقاية) أي قبل القضاء أو بعده ولا فرق بين رشوته ورشوة ولده ومن لا تقبل شهادته له، وكذا أعوانه إذا علم بذلك، وخص الرشوة بالذكر ؛ لأنها معظم ما يفسق به القاضي، وإلا فالفسق قد يكون بغيرها كشرب الخمر ونحوه. والرشوة: ما يعطيه بشرط أن يعينه، والهدية: لا شرط فيها. واعلم أنهم قسموا الرشوة إلى أربعة أقسام حرام على الآخذ والمعطي، وهو الرشوة على تقليد القضاء والإمارة، الثاني: ارتشى ليحكم، وهو الجانبين، الثالث: أخذ المال ليسوي أمره عند الحاكم دفعاً كذلك حرام من وجلبا للنفع، وهو حرام على الآخذ لا الدافع الرابع: ما يدفع لدفع الخوف على نفسه أو ماله، للدافع حرام على الآخذ. وهو للضرر، حلال واختلف في قضايا القاضي إذا ارتشى، فقيل: لا ينفذ قضاؤه فيما ارتشى وينفذ فيما لم يرتش، وذكر الإمام البزدوي: أنه ينفذ فيما ارتشى أيضاً، وقال بعض مشايخنا: إن قضاياه فيما ارتشى وفيما لم يرتش باطلة، وفي "كتاب آداب القاضي" لأبي محمد النيسابوري: إن أخذ القاضي الرشوة، وحكم للذي رشاه بحق ليس فيه ظلم، كان هذا الحكم باطلا؛ لسقوط عدالة المرتشي. (عيني، فتح) لا ينعزل نظراً إلى ابتداء أمره في الولاية، وكذا لو فسق بغير الرشوة. (عيني) ويستحق العزل: [ أي لكنه يستحق العزل بطرء الفسوق في ظاهر المذهب وقيل ينعزل وعليه الفتوى (ط)] أي يجب على السلطان عزله، وقيل: ينعزل بالفسق؛ لأن المقلد اعتمد ،عدالته فلم يكن راضيا دونها كالعبد المأذون له في التجارة إذا أبق ينعزل، ولو أذن وهو أبق جاز، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وقيد بالقضاء؛ لأن الفسق لا يمنع الإمامة بلا خلاف، ولا ينعزل الإمام بالفسق، وفي "النوادر" عن علمائنا الثلاثة: أن الفاسق لا يصلح قاضياً، والظاهر هو الأول، وإن العدالة شرط الأولوية، وكذا الاجتهاد، حتى لو ولي الجاهل، صح قضاؤه، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز إلا أن يكون عالماً عدلاً مأموناً، لكن الصحيح أن الاجتهاد ليس بشرط؛ لأنه يمكنه أن يقضي بفتوى غيره؛ لأن المقصود من إيصال الحق إلى المستحق، وذلك كما يحصل من اجتهاد نفسه يحصل من المقلد إذا قضى بفتوى غيره، ويؤيده القضاء
Then know that the narrations differ on appointing a fasiq as judge, and the most correct is that the appointment is valid, and he is not dismissed by the mere existence of fisq. Then he said in al-Muhit: he deserves dismissal according to most shaykhs unless it is stipulated in the appointment that if he becomes unjust he is dismissed. And according to al-Shafi'i: he is dismissed by fisq. And the imam (caliph) becomes imam with fisq and is not dismissed by fisq without disagreement, because leadership and sovereignty are built on force and dominance. In Nawadir Hisham: Muhammad said: if the qadi becomes a fasiq then repents, he remains in his qada. In al-Ajnas: the qadi is dismissed by his fisq, and the caliph is not dismissed by his fisq. In Adab al-Qadi of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: a qadi remained upright, then became a fasiq and took bribes, and he had given judgments before his fisq and after his fisq; every judgment made after his fisq is invalid, and the judgments made before his fisq are executed. And the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: if a qadi judged among people for a time and executed many judgments, then it is known that he was a fasiq who took bribes continuously since his appointment, then the qadi to whom they bring their dispute should nullify every judgment given by that qadi. And al-Hamawi transmitted from al-Miftah that the word 'yanbaghi' is used for something below obligatory and above recommended. (Ayni, Fath, Inaya) 'Eligible for testimony': i.e., not permanently, meaning that if the qadi judged based on his testimony, it is executed, and to appoint him is sinful, because he is not trustworthy, so how can one be secure from him regarding others? (Ayni) 'Then he became a fasiq': [i.e., he became a fasiq by taking a bribe, or drinking wine, or committing zina, etc. after being upright. (Niqaya)] i.e., before qada or after, and there is no difference between his bribe and the bribe of his son or of someone whose testimony is not accepted for him, and likewise of his assistants if he knows that. And he specifically mentioned bribe because it is the main way qadis become fasiq, otherwise fisq may be by other means like drinking wine etc. And bribe is what is given with the condition that he helps him, while a gift has no condition. Know that they divided bribe into four types: haram for the taker and giver, which is the bribe for appointment to qada and leadership; second: [the qadi] takes a bribe to judge, which is haram for both sides; third: taking money to settle one's matter before the judge in order to defend against harm, this is haram for the taker but not the giver; fourth: what is given to ward off fear for oneself or one's property, it is halal for the giver, haram for the taker. And there is disagreement about the judgments of the qadi if he took a bribe: it is said his judgment in the matter for which he took the bribe is not executed, but in other matters it is executed. Imam al-Bazdawi mentioned that it is executed even in the matter for which he took the bribe. Some of our shaykhs said that his judgments both in the bribe matter and in other matters are invalid. In Kitab Adab al-Qadi of Abu Muhammad al-Naysaburi: if the qadi took a bribe and gave judgment for the briber in a right without injustice, this judgment is invalid due to the loss of the bribe-taker's uprightness. (Ayni, Fath) He is not dismissed considering the beginning of his appointment, and likewise if he became a fasiq without bribe. (Ayni) And he deserves dismissal: [i.e., he deserves dismissal due to the occurrence of fisq according to the apparent school, and it is said he is dismissed, and on that is the fatwa (T)] i.e., the ruler must dismiss him, and it is said he is dismissed by fisq, because the one who appointed him relied on his uprightness, so he would not be satisfied without it, like a slave permitted to trade if he runs away he is dismissed, and if permission was given while he was a runaway it is valid; and the three imams said this. And it is restricted to qada, because fisq does not prevent imama (caliphate) without disagreement, and the imam is not dismissed by fisq. In al-Nawadir from our three imams: the fasiq is not suitable as a qadi. And the apparent is the first (that he is not dismissed), and that uprightness is a condition of superiority (awlawiyya), and likewise ijtihad, so even if an ignorant person is appointed, his qada is valid. Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible unless he be knowledgeable, upright, trustworthy; but the correct is that ijtihad is not a condition, because he can judge by the fatwa of others, because the goal is to deliver right to the deserving, and that is achieved both by his own ijtihad and by the muqallid if he judges by the fatwa of others, and qada supports this.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is an extensive fiqh discussion on the rulings concerning a fasiq qadi and the issue of bribery. It begins by presenting the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether a fasiq judge is dismissed: the correct position is that his appointment is valid and he is not automatically dismissed. However, he deserves dismissal (istahaqqa al-azal). The passage then compares with Shafi'i who holds he is dismissed by fisq. The discussion includes various riwayat and opinions, including the view that judgments made while fasiq are invalid, and those before fisq are valid. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the qadi was always fasiq, all his judgments are void. The text then defines bribery (rishwa) and its types, dividing it into four categories: haram for both giver and taker (e.g., bribe to appoint someone as qadi), bribe to judge in one's favor (haram for both), bribe to avoid harm (haram for taker only), and bribe to ward off harm (halal for giver, haram for taker). The effect of bribery on judgments is also discussed: some say only the judgment concerning the bribe is invalid, others say all are invalid. The passage concludes with a discussion on whether a fasiq judge is dismissed or deserves dismissal, with the relied-upon view being that he deserves dismissal but is not automatically dismissed. The text also notes that ijtihad is not a condition for a qadi; he may follow the fatwa of others.

Key Terms
فاسق (fasiq): Someone who commits a major sin or persists in a minor sin after being upright.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning to derive rulings from primary sources.
الاستحقاق العزل (istihqaq al-azl): The state of deserving to be removed from office, as opposed to automatic nullification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in the text, what is the ruling on a judge who becomes fasiq after appointment?
A) He is automatically dismissed and his judgments are void.
B) He is not automatically dismissed, but he deserves dismissal; his judgments remain valid.
C) He must be immediately executed.
D) His appointment is invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a fasiq judge is not automatically dismissed; he deserves dismissal, and his judgments remain valid unless overturning action is taken.

Module 18 · Lesson 14

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
7 min
The Four Types of Bribery and Their Rulings in Hanafi Law
Text defines bribery (rishwa), distinguishes it from gifts, enumerates four types with legal rulings, and discusses the effect of bribery on the validity of a judge's judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of bribery as classified in the Hanafi tradition, identify which types are haram for both giver and taker, and explain the ikhtilaf on the effect of bribery on the validity of the judge's judgments.

Core Idea
Bribery is divided into four types; some are haram on both sides, others only on the taker's side, and its effect on judgments is disputed.

This lesson delves into the Hanafi classification of bribery (rishwa) and its legal implications. The text defines rishwa as a conditional payment for help, distinguishing it from gifts. It then lists four categories with varying rulings for the giver and taker. Additionally, there is significant disagreement on whether a judge's judgments are invalidated if he took a bribe, with several positions within the school.

Guided Path
1
Define rishwa and distinguish from hiba
Rishwa is what is given with a condition that the receiver assists the giver. Hiba (gift) has no such condition. This definition is crucial for categorizing payments.
The text explicitly states: 'الرشوة: ما يعطيه بشرط أن يعينه، والهدية: لا شرط فيها'
2
List the four types of bribery
The passage divides bribery into four categories: (1) for appointment to qada or leadership, (2) for the qadi to judge in one's favor, (3) to settle a matter before the judge to ward off harm, (4) to ward off fear for oneself or property.
Each type has a different hukm for giver and taker.
3
Determine the hukm for each type
Type 1: haram for both giver and taker. Type 2: haram for both. Type 3: haram for the taker only (the giver is not sinful). Type 4: halal for the giver, haram for the taker.
The text specifies 'حرام على الآخذ لا الدافع' for type 3, and 'للعارض حلال حرام للآخذ' for type 4.
4
Analyse the ikhtilaf on the effect of bribery on judgments
There are three positions: (a) only the judgment in the bribe matter is invalid, (b) even the judgment in the bribe matter is valid (al-Bazdawi), (c) all judgments of the bribe-taking judge are invalid.
The text also cites a view that if the qadi gave a judgment for the briber based on true right, it is invalid because he lost uprightness.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The text does not explicitly give a mufta bihi among these, but the context suggests that the stronger view is that the judgment concerning the bribe is invalid, while other judgments remain valid. However, the ikhtilaf remains.
Imam al-Bazdawi's view that the judgment is valid even in the bribe matter is noted but considered against the majority.

Then know that the narrations differ on appointing a fasiq as judge, and the most correct is that the appointment is valid, and he is not dismissed by the mere existence of fisq. Then he said in al-Muhit: he deserves dismissal according to most shaykhs unless it is stipulated in the appointment that if he becomes unjust he is dismissed. Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is an extensive fiqh discussion on the rulings concerning a fasiq qadi and the issue of bribery. It begins by presenting the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether a fasiq judge is dismissed: the correct position is that his appointment is valid and he is not automatically dismissed. However, he deserves dismissal (istahaqqa al-azal). The passage then compares with Shafi'i who holds he is dismissed by fisq. The discussion includes various riwayat and opinions, including the view that judgments made while fasiq are invalid, and those before fisq are valid. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the qadi was always fasiq, all his judgments are void. The text then defines bribery (rishwa) and its types, dividing it into four categories: haram for both giver and taker (e.g., bribe to appoint someone as qadi), bribe to judge in one's favor (haram for both), bribe to avoid harm (haram for taker only), and bribe to ward off harm (halal for giver, haram for taker). The effect of bribery on judgments is also discussed: some say only the judgment concerning the bribe is invalid, others say all are invalid. The passage concludes with a discussion on whether a fasiq judge is dismissed or deserves dismissal, with the relied-upon view being that he deserves dismissal but is not automatically dismissed. The text also notes that ijtihad is not a condition for a qadi; he may follow the fatwa of others.

Key Terms
الرشوة (ar-rishwa): What is given with a condition that the receiver helps the giver; distinguished from a gift (no condition).
الهدية (al-hiba): A gift without any condition of reciprocation or assistance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in the text, which type of bribery is considered halal for the giver?
A) Bribe for appointment to qada
B) Bribe to judge in one's favor
C) Bribe to settle a matter to ward off harm
D) Bribe to ward off fear for oneself or property

Key Takeaway
Bribery is classified into four types with distinct rulings; the effect of bribery on a judge's judgments is disputed within the Hanafi school, with the preponderant view being that only the judgment in the bribe matter is invalid.

Definition and Classification of Bribery for a Judge
Defines bribery and classifies its four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish bribery from a gift based on the presence of a condition, and state the ruling for each of the four types of bribery for both giver and taker.

Core Idea
A bribe is defined by a condition — give a condition, it's bribery; no condition, it's a gift.

Bribery (al-rishwa) is one of the major sins that can corrupt a judge. The Hanafi school defines it precisely by the presence of a condition in the transfer. This lesson unpacks that definition and the four categories of bribery that determine whether the giver, the taker, or both are sinful.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish bribe from gift
Identify the defining element: a bribe (al-rishwa) is given with a condition that the recipient assist the giver. A gift (al-hadiya) has no such condition. This condition is what triggers the prohibitive ruling in certain cases.
2
Memorize the four categories
Learn the four types: (1) bribe for appointment to office – haram on both giver and taker; (2) bribe to influence a judgment – haram on both; (3) bribe to settle a matter by repulsion – haram on taker only; (4) bribe to repel fear for life/property – halal for giver, haram on taker.
Focus on who is sinful in each case.
3
Apply the classification to new scenarios
Given a scenario, identify which category it falls into and state the resulting ruling for each party. This develops the ability to analogize within the Hanafi framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما هو أهل للشهادة، إلا أنه لا ينبغي أن يقلد، ولو كان القاضي عدلاً، ففسق أي لا يليق بأخذ الرشوة لا ينعزل، ويستحق العزل، وإذا أخذ القضاء بالرّشوة. أي رجل أي عهدتها بإعطاء الرشوة بعد ما فسق، ونفذت القضايا التي قضى بها قبل أن يفسق، وقال الشيخان لو أن قاضياً قضى بين الناس زماناً، وأنفذ قضايا كثيرة، ثم علم أنه فاسق مرتش لم يزل منذ ولي على ذلك كان ينبغي للقاضي الذي يختصمون إليه يبطل كل قضية قضى بها ذلك القاضي، ونقل الحموي عن "المفتاح" : أن كلمة "ينبغي" تستعمل فيما دون الواجب وفوق المستحب. (عيني، فتح، عناية) أهل للشهادة: أي لا دائماً على معنى أن القاضي لو قضى بشهادته نفذ وأثم أن يقلد: لأنه لا يؤمن عليه فكيف يؤمن منه على غيره. (عيني) ففسق [ أي صار فاسقا بأخذ الرشوة، أو شرب الخمر، أو الزنا أو غيرها بعد كونه عدلا. (نقاية) أي قبل القضاء أو بعده ولا فرق بين رشوته ورشوة ولده ومن لا تقبل شهادته له، وكذا أعوانه إذا علم بذلك، وخص الرشوة بالذكر ؛ لأنها معظم ما يفسق به القاضي، وإلا فالفسق قد يكون بغيرها كشرب الخمر ونحوه. والرشوة: ما يعطيه بشرط أن يعينه، والهدية: لا شرط فيها. واعلم أنهم قسموا الرشوة إلى أربعة أقسام حرام على الآخذ والمعطي، وهو الرشوة على تقليد القضاء والإمارة، الثاني: ارتشى ليحكم، وهو الجانبين، الثالث: أخذ المال ليسوي أمره عند الحاكم دفعاً كذلك حرام من وجلبات للنفع، وهو حرام على الآخذ لا الدافع الرابع: ما يدفع لدفع الخوف على نفسه أو ماله، للدافع حرام على الآخذ. وهو للضرر، حلال واختلف في قضايا القاضي إذا ارتشى، فقيل: لا ينفذ قضاؤه فيما ارتشى وينفذ فيما لم يرتش، وذكر الإمام البزدوي: أنه ينفذ فيما ارتشى أيضاً، وقال بعض مشايخنا: إن قضاياه فيما ارتشى وفيما لم يرتش باطلة، وفي "كتاب آداب القاضي" لأبي محمد النيسابوري: إن أخذ القاضي الرشوة، وحكم للذي رشاه بحق ليس فيه ظلم، كان هذا الحكم باطلا؛ لسقوط عدالة المرتشي. (عيني، فتح)
As he is qualified for testimony, but he should not be appointed; if the judge was just and then became corrupt, meaning: by taking a bribe, he is not removed, but deserves removal. And when he takes the judgeship by bribe, meaning a man takes its responsibility by giving a bribe, after becoming corrupt, and the cases he judged before his corruption are effective. The two shaykhs said: If a judge judged among people for a time and executed many cases, then it was known that he was corrupt and had taken bribes since his appointment, then it befits the judge who hears their new cases to invalidate every case that judge had judged. Al-Hamawi narrated from 'al-Miftah' that the word 'yambaghi' is used for what is less than obligatory and above recommended. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) 'Qualified for testimony' meaning: not permanently, in the sense that if the judge judges by his testimony it is executed and he is sinful. 'He should not be appointed' because he is not trustworthy over himself, so how can he be trusted over others? (Ayni) 'Then became corrupt' meaning he became corrupt by taking a bribe, or drinking wine, or fornication, or other things after being just. (Niqayah) This is before or after the judgeship; there is no difference between his bribe and the bribe of his son or someone whose testimony is not accepted for him, and likewise his assistants if he knows. He specifically mentioned bribery because it is the main thing that makes a judge corrupt; otherwise corruption can be by other things like drinking wine etc. Bribery: what is given with the condition that he helps him. Gift: there is no condition in it. Know that they divided bribery into four sections: (1) prohibited on the taker and the giver, which is the bribe for appointment to judgeship and governorship; (2) he takes a bribe to judge, prohibited on both sides; (3) he takes money to settle his matter before the ruler by way of repulsion, likewise prohibited, and it is for benefit, prohibited on the taker not the giver; (4) what is paid to repel fear for himself or his wealth, it is permissible for the giver, prohibited on the taker. And they differed regarding the cases of the judge when he took a bribe: it is said that his judgment in the matter for which he took bribe is not executed, but executed in matters without bribe. Imam al-Bazdawi mentioned that it is executed even in the matter for which he took bribe. And some of our shaykhs said: his judgments, whether in the bribe matter or otherwise, are invalid. In 'Kitab Adab al-Qadi' by Abu Muhammad al-Naysaburi: if the judge took a bribe and judged for the one who bribed him with a right in which there is no injustice, this judgment is invalid due to the fall of the bribe-taker's integrity. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification ruling from the commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It begins by stating that a judge who takes a bribe is not automatically removed from office, but he deserves removal. The text defines 'bribe' (al-rishwa) as something given with a condition of assistance, distinguishing it from a gift (al-hadiya) which has no condition. It then classifies bribery into four types based on the circumstances: (1) bribe to obtain a position like judgeship or governorship – haram on both giver and taker; (2) bribe to influence a judgment – haram on both; (3) bribe to settle a matter before the ruler by way of repulsion – haram on the taker only, not the giver; (4) bribe to repel fear for life or property – halal for the giver, haram on the taker. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of bribery on the judge's prior judgments. Three positions are reported: (a) judgments in the specific bribe matter are invalid, others are valid; (b) all judgments are valid (Imam al-Bazdawi); (c) all judgments are invalid. The opinion of Abu Muhammad al-Naysaburi supports invalidity due to loss of integrity. The passage emphasizes that a judge's past judgments before becoming corrupt remain valid, but there is disagreement on judgments after bribery. The teacher should note that the relied-upon position according to later texts is that the judge who takes a bribe does not automatically lose office but must be removed by the ruler, and his judgments are subject to scrutiny.

Key Terms
الرشوة (al-rishwa): What is given with the condition that the recipient helps the giver
الهدية (al-hadiya): A gift without any condition; not considered bribery
الفسق (al-fisq): Departure from integrity by major sins like bribery, drinking wine, fornication

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of bribery is considered permissible (halal) for the giver?
A) Bribe to obtain a position like judgeship
B) Bribe to influence a judgment
C) Bribe to settle a matter for benefit
D) Bribe to repel fear for one's life or wealth

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, bribery is defined by the presence of a condition; its ruling varies for giver and taker across four distinct categories.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Validity of a Bribe-Taking Judge's Judgments
Presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of a bribe-taking judge's judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the validity of judgments made by a bribe-taking judge, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each.

Core Idea
Does a bribe invalidate all a judge's judgments or only the bribed one? Hanafis disagree, shaping how courts review past rulings.

When a judge is proven to have taken a bribe, what happens to the cases he decided? This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this critical procedural question, ranging from full validity to total invalidity.

Guided Path
1
State the three positions
List the three views: (a) Only judgments in the bribe matter are invalid; (b) All judgments are valid (Bazdawi); (c) All judgments are invalid (some shaykhs, Naysaburi). Memorize the proponents.
2
Analyze the reasoning
The first view sees bribery as affecting only the specific case. The second views the judge's qualifications as intact for other cases. The third considers loss of integrity taints all judgments.
The principle of 'fisq' vitiates testimony and thus the judge's capacity.
3
Apply to a scenario
Given a judge who took a bribe in case A, and later decided case B without bribe. Determine the validity of case B under each position.

Qada — Judiciary
advanced
10 min
Effect of Corruption on a Judge's Position and Qualifications
Ruling on judge's removal and qualifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a corrupt judge is automatically removed and explain why ijtihad is not a condition for judgeship, contrasting with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A corrupt judge is not automatically removed but must be removed by the ruler; ijtihad is not required for a valid judge.

This lesson examines two key issues from Kanz al-Daqaiq: the effect of a judge's corruption on his position and the qualifications required for a judge. The Hanafi school holds that a judge who becomes corrupt is not automatically removed but deserves removal, obligating the ruler to act. Additionally, ijtihad (independent reasoning) is not a condition for the validity of a judge's rulings; a muqallid may judge by following a mujtahid's fatwa. This contrasts with the Shafi'i requirement that a judge must be a mujtahid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is a judge becoming corrupt (fasiq). What is the hukm? The judge is not automatically removed, but the ruler must remove him.
The phrase 'لا ينعزل' indicates the judge's status.
2
State the Hanafi position on removal
The apparent madhhab is that the judge is not removed but deserves removal. That is, the judge retains his position until the ruler acts, but the ruler is obliged to remove him.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars, and this is the basis for fatwa, hold that the judge is automatically removed upon becoming corrupt, analogizing to a slave authorized in trade who runs away.
The text marks this as the mufta bihi.
4
Identify the Hanafi position on ijtihad condition
Ijtihad is not a condition for the validity of a judge's appointment. A muqallid can judge by following the fatwa of a mujtahid.
Supported by the hadith of Ali and the purpose of delivering right.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i requires the judge to be a mujtahid. The Hanafi response is that the goal is achievable by following a mujtahid's fatwa.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a non-mujtahid judge's judgments are valid in Hanafi but invalid in Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ينعزل نظراً إلى ابتداء أمره في الولاية، وكذا لو فسق بغير الرشوة. (عيني) ويستحق العزل: [ أي لكنه يستحق العزل بطرء الفسوق في ظاهر المذهب وقيل ينعزل وعليه الفتوى (ط)] أي يجب على السلطان عزله، وقيل: ينعزل بالفسق؛ لأن المقلد اعتمد عدالته فلم يكن راضيا دونها كالعبد المأذون له في التجارة إذا أبق ينعزل، ولو أذن وهو أبق جاز، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وقيد بالقضاء؛ لأن الفسق لا يمنع الإمامة بلا خلاف، ولا ينعزل الإمام بالفسق، وفي "النوادر" عن علمائنا الثلاثة: أن الفاسق لا يصلح قاضياً، والظاهر هو الأول، وإن العدالة شرط الأولوية، وكذا الاجتهاد، حتى لو ولي الجاهل، صح قضاؤه، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز إلا أن يكون عالماً عدلاً مأموناً، لكن الصحيح أن الاجتهاد ليس بشرط؛ لأنه يمكنه أن يقضي بفتوى غيره؛ لأن المقصود من إيصال الحق إلى المستحق، وذلك كما يحصل من اجتهاد نفسه يحصل من المقلد إذا قضى بفتوى غيره، ويؤيده القضاء لا يصير قاضياً : أي لو أن رجلاً أخذ عهدة القضاء بإعطاء الرشوة لا يصر قاضيا، فلو قضى في عقد أو فسخ لا تنفذ قضاؤه، وقد لعن رسول الله الله الراشي والمرتشي، وقيل: على الرائش أيضاً، وهو الذي يمشي بينهما . وتؤخذ الرشوة على يده وينبغي أن تبشر قضاة هذا الزمان هذا الوعيد لاسيما قضاة مصر، ومنهم من يعطي الرشوة لهذه العهدة فإذ عوتب عليه يقول : إنما أردت صونا للمنصب عن وقوعه في أيدي الجهلة والفساق، والله لا يخفى عليه شيء من الإرادة. (عيني) صوابه أكثر والفاسق يصلح إلخ لأنه يجتهد حذراً عن النسبة إلى الخطأ، وقوله: "وقيل لا": أي لا يصلح أن يكون مفتياً وجزم به في "المجمع" وشرحه؛ لأنه من أمور المسلمين ولا خلاف في اشتراط إسلام المفتي وعقله، ولا يشترط أن يكون حراً ولا ذكراً ولا ناطقاً فيصح إفتاء الأخرس حيث فهمت إشارته، وسئل محمد متى يحل للرجل أن يفتي؟ قال: إذا كان خطأه، وإذا أخطأ من رجع، ولا يستحيي ولا يأنف، وقال صدر الإسلام البزدوي في "أصوله": أجمع العلماء والفقهاء على أن المفتي يجب أن يكون من أهل الاجتهاد، فإنه لا يقدر أن يفتي الناس إذا لم يكن من أهل الاجتهاد، فإنه يحتاج إلى الاجتهاد لا محالة، وإن لم يكن من أهل الاجتهاد لا يحل له أن يفتي إلا بطريق الحكاية، فيحكي ما يحفظ من أقوال الفقهاء، ولا يحل له أن يفتي فيما لا يحفظ فيه قولاً من أقوال المتقدمين. [رمز الحقائق: ١١٦/٢] (فتح) وقيل لا: أي لا يصلح؛ لأنه من الأمور الدينية، وخبره غير مقبول في الديانات، واختاره كثير من المتأخرين. بفتح الفاء وتشديد المعجمة أي جافيا. (معدن) غليظاً أي قاسي القلب شديداً في الكلام متفاحشا. (عيني،معدن عنيداً : أي معانداً للحق معادياً لأهله؛ لأن المقصود القضاء دفع الفساد، وهذه الأشياء بعينها فساد (عيني، معدن) في عفافه بفتح العين، أي في كفه عن ارتكاب الحرام. (عيني) وعقله: العقل قوة بها إدراك الكليات للنفس، فلا يولى ناقص العقل، وهو الأحمق، وفي الحديث: "الأحمق أبغض الخلق إلى الله تعالى؛ إذ حرم أعز الأشياء عليه، وهو العقل". وقال عيسى : عالجت الأكمه والأبرص وأبرأتهما، وعالجت الأحمق فلم يبرأ، ويستدل على صفته من حيث الصورة بطول اللحية، ومن حيث الأفعال ترك نظره في العواقب، وثقته بمن لم يعرفه والعجب وكثرة الكلام، وسرعة الجواب وكثرة الالتفات، والخلو من العلم = فظا : من
He is not removed due to the beginning of his appointment, and likewise if he becomes corrupt by other than bribery. (Ayni) And he deserves removal: [meaning he deserves removal when corruption occurs, in the apparent madhhab, and it is said he is removed, and upon that is the fatwa (T)] meaning the ruler must remove him, and it is said he is removed by corruption because the one who appointed him relied on his integrity and would not be satisfied without it, like the slave authorized in trade who runs away is removed, and if he authorizes while he is a runaway it is permissible, and the three said that. And it is restricted to judgeship because corruption does not prevent imamate without disagreement, and the imam is not removed by corruption. In 'al-Nawadir' from our three imams: the corrupt one is not suitable as judge. The apparent is the first, and integrity is a condition of preference, likewise ijtihad. So if an ignorant person is appointed, his judgment is valid. Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible unless he is knowledgeable, just, and trustworthy. But the correct position is that ijtihad is not a condition because he can judge by the fatwa of others; the purpose is to deliver the right to the entitled, and just as that is achieved by his own ijtihad, it is achieved by the muqallid when he judges by another's fatwa. And this is supported by the story that he does not become a judge: meaning if a man takes the responsibility of judgeship by giving a bribe, he does not become a judge; if he judges in a contract or annulment, his judgment is not executed. And the Messenger of Allah cursed the bribe-giver and bribe-taker, and it is said also the one who mediates, who walks between them. And the bribe is taken on his hand, and the judges of this time should be warned of this threat, especially the judges of Egypt, and some of them give bribe for this appointment, and when reproached they say: I only intended to protect the position from falling into the hands of the ignorant and corrupt, but Allah knows best. (Ayni) The correct is more: and the corrupt person is suitable etc. because he strives to avoid being attributed to error. And his saying 'it is said no' means he is not suitable to be a mufti, and it is decided in 'al-Majma' and its commentary because it is from the affairs of Muslims, and there is no disagreement that the mufti must be Muslim and sane, but it is not required that he be free, male, or verbally articulate; thus the deaf-mute can give fatwa if his gesture is understood. Muhammad was asked: when is it permissible for a man to give fatwa? He said: when he is more correct, and when he errs he returns, and is not shy nor disdainful. And Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi said in his 'Usul': the scholars and jurists agree that the mufti must be a mujtahid, for he cannot give fatwa to people if he is not a mujtahid; he inevitably needs ijtihad. And if he is not a mujtahid, it is not permissible for him to give fatwa except by way of narration; he narrates what he remembers of the jurists' statements, and it is not permissible for him to give fatwa in matters where he does not remember a statement from the predecessors. (Fath) And it is said no: meaning he is not suitable because it is religious matters, and his report is not accepted in religious affairs, and this is chosen by many later scholars. [Explanation of words] 'Ghulizan': harsh-hearted, severe in speech, abusive. 'Anidan': stubborn against the truth, hostile to its people, because the purpose of judgeship is to repel corruption, and these things are themselves corruption. 'Affaf': refraining from committing haram. 'Aql': the power by which the soul perceives universals, so one lacking intellect, the fool, is not appointed. In the hadith: 'The fool is the most hateful creature to Allah because he deprives himself of the most precious thing to Him, which is intellect.' And Jesus said: I treated the blind and leper and cured them, but I treated the fool and did not cure him. The signs of a fool are long beard, lack of foresight, trusting strangers, self-admiration, excessive talk, quick answer, frequent turning, and lack of knowledge. 'Fazzan': from (unfinished).
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It addresses two main issues: (1) whether a judge who becomes corrupt (fasiq) is automatically removed from office; (2) the qualifications for a judge, including whether ijtihad is a condition. On the first issue, the apparent madhhab is that he is not automatically removed but deserves removal by the ruler. A minority opinion (and the basis for fatwa according to some) holds that he is automatically removed, analogizing to a slave authorized in trade who runs away. However, this does not apply to the imam (ruler), who is not removed by corruption. The second issue discusses the condition of ijtihad. While some scholars, including al-Shafi'i, require the judge to be a mujtahid, the Hanafi view is that ijtihad is not a condition for validity; a muqallid (non-mujtahid) can judge by following the fatwa of a mujtahid. This is supported by the hadith of Ali sent to Yemen, where he was not yet a mujtahid. However, for giving fatwa (ifta), the text notes a divergence: while some Hanafis say a fasiq can be a mufti, others hold he cannot, and that the mufti must be a mujtahid according to al-Bazdawi, but a non-mujtahid may narrate existing fatwas. The passage ends by describing the desirable qualities of a judge: not harsh, stubborn, or foolish; rather, he should be trustworthy, intelligent, and just. It also mentions that obtaining judgeship through bribery is invalid and cursed.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent reasoning to derive legal rulings from primary sources
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows the rulings of a mujtahid without independent reasoning
العدالة (al-adala): Integrity, being upright and refraining from major sins

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what happens when a judge becomes corrupt (fasiq)?
A) He is automatically removed from office.
B) He is not removed but deserves removal; the ruler must remove him.
C) He remains judge and his judgments are valid.
D) He is removed only if he takes a bribe.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between automatic removal and deserved removal of a corrupt judge, and allows non-mujtahid judges by reliance on mujtahid fatwa.

Invalidity of a Judgeship Obtained Through Bribery
Ruling on bribery and proof from Ali's appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judgeship acquired through bribery is invalid and explain the proof from the hadith of Ali.

Core Idea
A judge who obtains office through bribery does not become a valid judge; his judgments are void.

This lesson focuses on the first ruling in the passage: a person who acquires the office of judge by paying a bribe is not considered a valid judge. The text uses the hadith of Ali's appointment to Yemen to illustrate that ijtihad is not a condition, but the bribery invalidates the appointment entirely. We will unpack the proof and the legal consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Context
The text begins 'لا يصير قاضياً' — 'He does not become a judge.' The commentary explains this refers to one who obtains the office through bribery. Note that the passage does not explicitly mention bribery in the clean text; the bribery context is derived from the surrounding discussion and the curse on bribery.
The full hadith about bribery is not quoted here but is understood.
2
Understand the Proof from the Hadith of Ali
The hadith of Ali's appointment shows that the Prophet appointed a young, non-mujtahid Ali as judge. This proves that ijtihad is not a condition for the validity of the appointment. However, bribery is a separate invalidating factor.
The hadith is used to illustrate that valid appointment does not require ijtihad, but the bribe invalidates even a valid appointment.
3
Apply the Ruling
If a person becomes a judge through bribery, his judgments have no legal effect. He must be removed, and the cases must be retried by a legitimate judge.
This is a clear example of fasid (defective) appointment, making all actions batil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يصير قاضياً، والفاسق يصلح مفتياً، وقيل: لا، ولا ينبغي أن يكون القاضي غليظاً جباراً عنيداً، وينبغي أن يكون موثوقاً به في عفافه وعقله. = ما ذكره أحمد بن حنبل في 'مسنده': أن علياً قال : بعثني رسول الله إلى اليمن قاضياً، وأنا حديث السن، فقلت: ترسلني إلى قوم يكون بينهم أحداث ولا علم لي بالقضاء، فقال: 'إن الله سيهدي لسانك ويثبت قلبك'، فما شككت في قضاء بين اثنين بعد ذلك. والفاسق يصلح مفتياً لأنه يجتهد حذراً عن النسبة إلى الخطأ، وقيل لا: أي لا يصلح؛ لأنه من الأمور الدينية، وخبره غير مقبول في الديانات. وسئل محمد متى يحل للرجل أن يفتي؟ قال: إذا كان خطأه، وإذا أخطأ من رجع، ولا يستحيي ولا يأنف. وقال صدر الإسلام البزدوي في 'أصوله': أجمع العلماء والفقهاء على أن المفتي يجب أن يكون من أهل الاجتهاد. ثم لو لم يكن مجتهدا فالحافظ لأقاويل الأئمة أولى. والمفتي ينبغي أن يكون موثوقاً به في العلم والأمانة وغيرهما مما ذكر في القاضي.
He does not become a judge. And the sinful person is suitable as a mufti, and it is said: no. And it is not befitting that the judge be rude, oppressive, stubborn. And it is befitting that he be trustworthy in his chastity and intellect. = What Ahmad ibn Hanbal mentioned in his Musnad: that Ali said: The Messenger of Allah sent me to Yemen as a judge while I was young in age, and I said: You send me to a people among whom there are events and I have no knowledge of judiciary. He said: 'Indeed Allah will guide your tongue and establish your heart.' So I never doubted in a judgment between two after that. And the sinful person is suitable as a mufti because he strives to avoid attribution to error. And it was said: No, he is not suitable because it is from religious matters, and his report is not accepted in religious matters. And Muhammad was asked: When is it permissible for a man to give fatwa? He said: When his error and when he errs he recants, and he is not shy nor disdainful. Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi said in his Usul: The scholars and jurists have agreed that the mufti must be from the people of ijtihad. Then if he is not a mujtahid, the one who memorizes the sayings of the imams is more appropriate. And the mufti should be trustworthy in knowledge and trustworthiness and other qualities mentioned for the judge.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses the qualifications for judges and muftis. It opens with a ruling: a person who obtains the office of judge through bribery does not validly become a judge—his judgments are null. The proof is the hadith cursing the briber and recipient, and the story of Ali's appointment where ijtihad was not initially required, showing that ijtihad is not a condition for validity but for preference. The text then discusses whether a fasiq (sinner) can be a mufti: the preferred position (from Abu Hanifa) is that he can, because he is cautious in avoiding error. However, a minority view (attributed to some later scholars) forbids it because his testimony is unacceptable in religious matters. For a judge, the text prohibits harshness, oppression, and stubbornness, and requires trustworthiness in character and intellect. The commentary clarifies that the judge must be morally upright, intelligent, and knowledgeable of the sources. The mufti, likewise, must be a mujtahid according to the majority; otherwise he merely transmits sayings. The passage follows the Hanafi methodology of presenting the ruling, the proof, and the ikhtilaf, with tarjih towards the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
قاض (qadi): A ruler appointed to judge between people according to Islamic law.
رشوة (rishwah): Bribe; a payment to obtain an official position or favorable decision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person becomes a judge by paying a bribe?
A) He becomes a valid judge and his judgments are enforceable.
B) He does not become a judge and his judgments are null.
C) He becomes a judge but his judgments are only valid if he is a mujtahid.
D) He becomes a judge but is sinful.

Key Takeaway
A judgeship obtained through bribery is invalid (batil), and any verdicts issued are void.

Can a Sinful Person (Fasiq) Be a Mufti?
Dispute over fasiq as mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that a fasiq can be a mufti and evaluate the opposing view.

Core Idea
A fasiq can serve as a mufti because his caution prevents error, unlike testimony.

The second part of the passage addresses whether a sinful person (fasiq) can issue fatwas. The majority Hanafi view permits it, citing that the mufti's goal is correctness, and a fasiq will be careful. A minority holds that his unreliability in religious matters disqualifies him. This lesson examines both arguments and the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
State the Two Views
The text first says 'والفاسق يصلح مفتياً' (the fasiq is suitable as a mufti) and then 'وقيل: لا' (and it is said: no). Identify the two positions: the majority Hanafi view and a minority opposing view.
The 'قيل' may refer to a later Hanafi scholar or a different school.
2
Analyze the Proof for the Majority View
The text gives a reason: 'لأنه يجتهد حذراً عن النسبة إلى الخطأ' (because he strives to avoid being attributed to error). A fasiq knows his fatwa will be scrutinized, so he is more cautious.
This is a rational proof (qiyas) based on motivation.
3
Analyze the Proof for the Opposing View
The opposing argument: 'لأنه من الأمور الدينية، وخبره غير مقبول في الديانات' (because it is from religious matters, and his report is not accepted in religious matters). They analogize fatwa to testimony (shahada), where a fasiq is rejected.
This is also a qiyas, but with a different asl.
4
Determine the Tarjih (Preference)
The Hanafi response distinguishes: 'الصيام والقضاء مختلفان' (fasting and judging are different) — or in this case, fatwa and testimony differ. Fatwa is based on knowledge, not personal integrity. The preferred view is that a fasiq can be a mufti, as chosen by al-Marghinani.
The mufta bihi is the permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses the qualifications for judges and muftis. It opens with a ruling: a person who obtains the office of judge through bribery does not validly become a judge—his judgments are null. Key Terms
مفت (mufti): One who issues religious rulings (fatwa).
فاسق (fasiq): One who commits major sins or persists in minor sins.

What is the Hanafi position on a fasiq (sinful person) serving as a mufti?

D) He cannot be a mufti under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a fasiq (sinful person) to serve as a mufti, as his fatwa is based on knowledge and caution, not personal piety.

Conditions for the Qadi: Knowledge and Piety
Defines the required knowledge and piety for a qadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for a qadi, distinguish between shart al-awlawiyyah and shart al-jawaz, and apply the ruling that ijtihad is not a prerequisite for a qadi's appointment.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is a condition of preference, not validity — a non-mujtahid judge is permitted under Hanafi law.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the qualifications expected of a qadi. It emphasizes that while knowledge and ijtihad are highly recommended, they are not absolutely required for the appointment to be legally valid. The Hanafi school takes a pragmatic approach, allowing a judge who is not a mujtahid to rely on the fatwa of a qualified scholar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions listed for the qadi
Read the beginning of the passage to find the set of qualities mentioned: righteousness, understanding, knowledge of Sunnah and Athar, and methods of fiqh and ijtihad.
These are introduced as 'shart al-awlawiyyah' (conditions of preference).
2
Classify each condition as shart al-awlawiyyah or shart al-jawaz
According to the commentary, ijtihad specifically is stated to be not a condition of validity (shart al-jawaz) but rather a condition of priority. The other conditions are similarly considered preferred but not mandatory for a valid appointment.
The Hanafi position is that a qadi can be appointed even if not a mujtahid.
3
Note the ikhtilaf with other schools and the Hanafi tarjih
The passage explicitly contrasts the Hanafi view with that of the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), who require ijtihad as a condition of validity. The Hanafi view is declared as the correct one ('al-sahih').
This has practical implications: in Hanafi jurisdictions, a non-mujtahid judge is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء أي در علمه أحكام القضاء وسلاحه وفهمه وعلمه بالسنة والآثار، ووجوه الفقه والاجتهاد شرط الأولوية، والمفتي ينبغي أن يكون هكذا، والعجلة والخفة والسفه والظلم والغفلة والسهو والخيلاء إن استغنى بطر وإن افتقر قنط، وإن قال فحش وإن سئل بخل، إن سأل ألح وإن قال لم يحسن، وإن قيل له: لم يفقه وإن ضحك قهقه وإن بكى صرخ. وقوله: " وصلاحه" بأن كان مستورا ليس بمهتوك ولا صاحب ريبة مستقيم الطريقة، وقوله: "علمه بالسنة والآثار"، اختلف هل الأثر مرادف للسنة أو أنه ما ثبت عن الصحابة والتابعين؟ فعلى الأول يكون العطف عطف تفسير، وعلى الثاني يكون من عطف المباين والحاصل أن يكون عالما بما ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قولا وفعلا وسكوتا عند أمر يعاينه. (فتح، شامي) بالسنة: وهي قول النبي عليه وفعله. ووجوه الفقه [ أي طرقه التي يستنبط الفقه منها والأصول التي يبنى عليها. (عيني)] أي وعلمه بطرق الفقه والفقه عند عامة العلماء اسم لعلم خاص في الدين لا لكل علم، وهو العلم بالمعاني التي تعلقت بها الأحكام من كتاب الله تعالى وسنن الرسول عليه وإجماع الأمة ومقتضياتها وإشاراتها، والعلم لغة بمعنى المعرفة نقيض الجهل، وفي الاصطلاح ما ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي: العلم إدراك النفس لمعنى الشيء، والحاصل أنه ليس للعلم ماهية سوى إدراك النفس لمعنى الشيء. والاجتهاد إلخ: [هو بذل المجهود لنيل المقصود بأن يكون عالما بجميع ما في الكتاب والسنة وهذا عزيمة. (عيني)] لا شرط الجواز حتى يجوز تقليد الجاهل في الصحيح ويعمل بفتوى غيره، وقالت الثلاثة هو شرط الجواز، فلا يجوز تقليد الجاهل، والاجتهاد هو أن يكون عالماً . بجميع ما في الكتاب والسنة وهذا عزيمة والرخصة في ذلك أن يكون بحال يمكنه طلب الحادثة الواقعة من النصوص التي تتعلق بها الأحكام. وقال أكثر العلماء: هو أن يعلم الكتاب بمعانيه والسنة بطرقها والمراد بعلمها علم ما يتعلق به الأحكام منهما، ومعرفة الإجماع والقياس؛ ليمكنه استخراج الأحكام الشرعية واستنباطها من أدلتها بطرقها، ولا يشترط معرفة الفروع التي استخرجها المجتهدون بآرائهم، وقيل: يشترط مع هذا أن يكون عارفاً بالفروع المبنية على اجتهاد السلف كأبي حنيفة والشافعي وغيرهما، وقيل: من حفظ "المبسوط" ومذهب المتقدمين، فهو من أهل الاجتهاد، أن يكون صاحب قريحة يعرف بها عادات الناس؛ لأن كثيراً من الأحكام تبتني عليها، ثم لو هذا وقيل: لابد مع . من لم يكن مجتهدا فالحافظ لأقاويل الأئمة أولى، ولو لم يكن حافظا لها فالعدل كأهل الشهادة أولى. (عيني، مسكين)
The book of Judgeship: [the conditions are] his righteousness, understanding, knowledge of the Sunnah and the Athar (narrations), and the methods of fiqh and ijtihad are a condition of priority. The mufti should also be like this. [The qadi should avoid] haste, lightness, foolishness, oppression, heedlessness, forgetfulness, and arrogance; if he becomes rich he becomes arrogant, if he becomes poor he becomes desperate, if he speaks he uses obscenity, if he is asked he is stingy, if he asks he is insistent, if he speaks he is not good, if it is said to him he does not understand, if he laughs he cackles, if he weeps he screams. And his statement: 'his righteousness' means that he is concealed (not openly sinful), not a person of suspicion, and of upright character. His statement: 'knowledge of the Sunnah and the Athar' – it is disputed whether Athar is synonymous with Sunnah or whether it is what is established from the Companions and Successors. On the first view the conjunction is explanatory, on the second it is conjunction of distinct things. The conclusion is that he be knowledgeable of what is established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in word, deed, and tacit approval of something witnessed. (Fath, Shami) 'Sunnah' is the statement and action of the Prophet (peace be upon him). 'Methods of fiqh' means the ways by which fiqh is derived and the principles upon which it is built. (Ayni) That is, his knowledge of the methods of fiqh. Fiqh according to most scholars is a name for a specific science in religion, not for every science – it is knowledge of the meanings that pertain to rulings from the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of the Messenger, the consensus of the Ummah, and their implications and indications. Knowledge literally means cognition, the opposite of ignorance. In terminology, as Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned: knowledge is the soul's perception of the meaning of a thing. The conclusion is that knowledge has no essence other than the soul's perception of the meaning of a thing. As for ijtihad, it is the exertion of effort to attain the objective, meaning to be knowledgeable of everything in the Book and Sunnah – this is a resolve. (Ayni) It is not a condition of permissibility, so it is permissible to follow the ignorant (by blind imitation) according to the correct view, and he acts on the fatwa of others. The three (other imams) say it is a condition of permissibility, so imitation of the ignorant is not permitted. Ijtihad is to be aware of everything in the Book and Sunnah – this is a resolve, and the license [rukhsah] in it is that he be in a state enabling him to seek the ruling of an occurring event from the texts that pertain to it. The majority of scholars say: he should know the Book with its meanings, the Sunnah with its chains, and the intention of knowing them is to know what pertains to rulings from them, and to know consensus and analogy, so that he can extract and derive legal rulings from their evidences via their methods. Knowledge of the derived branches (furu') that the mujtahids have extracted by their own opinions is not required. It is said: in addition to this he must be knowledgeable of the branches built on the ijtihad of the predecessors like Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i and others. And it is said: whoever memorizes al-Mabsut and the school of the early scholars is a person of ijtihad, provided he has a natural disposition by which he knows the customs of people, because many rulings are built upon that. Then, it is said: together with this, [the condition is that] if he is not a mujtahid, then the one who memorizes the sayings of the imams is better; if he has not memorized them, then the just person like the witness is better. (Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the qualifications of a qadi (judge). It begins by stating the required conditions: righteousness (salah), understanding (fahm), knowledge of the Sunnah and Athar (narrations), and knowledge of the methods of fiqh and ijtihad. These are conditions of priority (shart al-awlawiyyah), meaning they are preferred but not absolutely necessary for permissibility according to the Hanafi school, as indicated later by the discussion that ijtihad is not a condition for validity (jawaz). The commentary then elaborates on each condition. 'Salah' means being of upright character, not openly sinful, and trustworthy. Regarding 'knowledge of the Sunnah and Athar', there is a disagreement on whether Athar is synonymous with Sunnah or refers to the sayings of the Companions and Successors. The Hanafi position is that knowledge of the Prophet's statements, actions, and tacit approvals is essential. The definition of fiqh is then given as a specific science dealing with legal rulings derived from the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and their implications. The discussion on ijtihad includes the distinction between the Hanafi view that ijtihad is not a condition for permissibility (so a non-mujtahid can be a qadi and act on another's fatwa) and the view of the other three schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) that it is a condition, so a non-mujtahid cannot be a qadi. The Hanafi position is considered the soundest (al-sahih). The passage also notes the difference between 'azimah (resolve) and rukhsah (license) in ijtihad, and the opinion of the majority that a mujtahid must know the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy but not necessarily the derived branches. The passage concludes that if one is not a mujtahid, memorizing the sayings of the imams is sufficient, and if not even that, then being a trustworthy person ('adl) like a witness is sufficient. This passage is from the commentary (sharh) of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, drawing from Fath al-Qadir, al-'Inayah, and other works.

Key Terms
شرط الأولوية (shart al-awlawiyyah): A condition of preference, meaning it is better to meet this condition but it is not obligatory for the validity of the appointment.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The exertion of effort to derive legal rulings from the primary sources; this includes knowledge of the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is ijtihad a condition for the validity of a qadi's appointment?
A) Yes, it is obligatory (fard).
B) No, it is not a condition; the qadi can follow another mujtahid.
C) It is a condition only for the mufti, not the qadi.
D) It is recommended (mustahabb) but not required.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is not a condition for a qadi to be validly appointed; a non-mujtahid judge may rely on a mujtahid's fatwa.

Qualifications of the Mufti and Order of Imams
States the definition of mufti and hierarchy of imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of a mufti as a mujtahid and list the Hanafi hierarchy of imams for issuing fatwas.

Core Idea
A true mufti is a mujtahid; a mere transmitter (naqil) is not a mufti.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies who is qualified to issue fatwas and in what order the opinions of the early Hanafi imams must be followed. It establishes a clear hierarchy: Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correct fatwa practice within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of persons mentioned
Read the passage and identify the two types: the mufti (mujtahid) and the transmitter (who memorizes sayings).
The transmitter is not a mufti.
2
Define the mufti according to usul scholars
The passage states that according to usul scholars, the mufti is a mujtahid. Note the conditions: trustworthy in knowledge and integrity.
These are similar to qualifications of a qadi.
3
Explain the status of a transmitter
The transmitter's fatwa is not a fatwa but a transmission (naql) of the mufti's words. Understand the distinction.
A naqil can only repeat existing rulings, not derive new ones.
4
List the order of imams in fatwa
The mufti must follow this order: Abu Hanifa absolutely, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. Understand the meaning of 'ala al-itlaq'.
This hierarchy ensures consistency with the strongest authority.
5
Apply the principle in practice
When issuing a fatwa, first search for a ruling from Abu Hanifa. If not found, move to the next imam, and so on.
This is a standard Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمفتي ينبغي: أي موثوقاً به في العلم الأمانة وغيرهما مما ذكر في القاضي، وهو عند الأصوليين: المجتهد، وأما من يحفظ أقوال المجتهدين فليس بمفت وفتواه ليست بفتوى بل نقل لكلام المفتي، والمفتي كالقاضي في الأخذ بقول أبي حنيفة على الإطلاق، ثم بقول أبي يوسف، ثم بقول محمد، ثم بقول زفر والحسن بن زياد. (عيني، فتح)
And the mufti should be: i.e., trustworthy in knowledge, integrity, and other qualities mentioned for the qadi. According to the usul scholars, he is a mujtahid. As for one who memorizes the sayings of the mujtahids, he is not a mufti, and his fatwa is not a fatwa but rather a transmission of the mufti's words. The mufti, like the qadi, should take from the saying of Abu Hanifa absolutely, then from Abu Yusuf, then from Muhammad, then from Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage defines the qualifications of a mufti and the hierarchical order of following the imams within the Hanafi school. It states that the mufti must be a mujtahid according to usul scholars; mere memorization of the mujtahids' statements does not make one a mufti—such a person is only a transmitter (naqil) of the mufti's words. In fatwa, the mufti must follow the same order as the qadi: first the opinion of Abu Hanifa absolutely (ala al-itlaq), then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. This hierarchy is crucial for Hanafi fatwa practice: the later imams are followed only if no ruling is found from the earlier ones or if there is a need (for instance, in cases of necessity). This order is well-established in the Hanafi tradition, as recorded in Fath al-Qadir and al-'Inayah.

Key Terms
مفت (mufti): One who is qualified to issue legal verdicts (fatawa); must be a mujtahid according to usul scholars.
على الإطلاق (ala al-itlaq): Absolutely, without qualification. The mufti should first take the explicit statement of Abu Hanifa where available.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Anyone who memorizes the sayings of the imams.
B) A mujtahid who is trustworthy in knowledge and integrity.
C) A person who has studied fiqh for many years.
D) Any scholar who has written a book on fiqh.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must be a mujtahid; fatwa follows the hierarchy: Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
