Ruling on Accepting Judgeship When Fearing Injustice
Explains when accepting judgeship is disliked or permissible based on fear of injustice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on accepting judgeship based on one's fear of injustice, identify the conditions that change the ruling, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on this issue.

Core Idea
The ruling on judgeship depends on the qadi's ability to be just: fear makes it prohibitively disliked; security makes it permissible.

This passage addresses a critical ethical dilemma: when is it permissible or even obligatory to accept the position of a judge? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that changes based on the individual's confidence in being just. We will explore the conditions, the evidence, and the scholarly differences within the school itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two scenarios
The act is accepting the post of qadi (taqallud al-qada). Identify the two scenarios: when the person fears injustice (hayf) and when he feels secure from it. Note the ruling for each.
2
Understand the condition: fear vs. security
Define 'hayf' as injustice or oppression. The passage explains its linguistic meaning and links it to Quranic usage. The condition of fear (khawf) or security (aman) determines the ruling.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Whoever is afflicted with judgeship, it is as if he is slaughtered without a knife' is cited. Analyze how this hadith supports the dislike of accepting judgeship when there is risk of injustice.
The hadith is a general warning, but its application is conditioned by the individual's capability to be just.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage mentions an alternative view stated as 'qila' (it is said) that judgeship is disliked even when secure. This is attributed to Abu Hanifa and al-Sha'bi through their actions. However, the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that it is not disliked when secure.
The ikhtilaf is practical: should a qualified person accept judgeship when offered by a just ruler? The mufta bihi allows it.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The passage earlier stated that judgeship is the best of acts of worship. Here it is cautioned against. The reconciliation: the best act becomes fard ayn when no one else can do it; otherwise it can be fard kifayah, disliked, or even haram depending on circumstances.
This shows the Hanafi method of combining texts (jam' al-adilla).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره التقلّد لمن خاف الحيف، وإن أمنه لا ولا يسأله، ويجوز تقلد القضاء من العادل والجائر ومن أهل البغي. وكره التقلد أي كراهة تحريم لمن خاف الحيف فيه وهو الجور والظلم والحيف بالحاء المهملة هو الميل ويرادفه جنف بالجيم والنون، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ خَافَ مِنْ مُوصٍ جَنَفَا﴾ (البقرة: ۱۸۲)، والحاصل: أن من خاف العجز عن أداء فرض القضاء ولا يأمن على نفسه الجور فيه كره له الدخول فيه لئلا يصير الدخول وسيلة إلى مباشرة القبيح وهو الحيف في القضاء.(فتح، عناية) وإن أمنه لا أي إن أمن على نفسه الجور في القضاء لا يكره له الدخول فيه اقتداء بكثير من الصحابة والتابعين، وقيل: يكره مختاراً وإن أمن على نفسه الحيف ألا ترى أنه امتنع كثير من العلماء كالشعبي وأبي حنيفة، وقد روي أن أبا حنيفة دعي إلى القضاء ثلاث مرات فأبى حتى حبس وجلد كل مرة ثلاثين سوطا حتى قال أبو يوسف: لو تقلدت لنفعت الناس، فنظر إليه شبه المغضب، فقال له: لو أمرت أن أقطع البحر سباحة لكنت أقدر عليه وكأني بك قاضيا؛ ولقوله عليه: "من ابتلي بالقضاء فكأنما ذبح بغير "سكين"، ولا تعارض بين هذا وبين ما سبق أن القضاء أفضل العبادات؛ لأن ما سبق محمول على ما إذا تعين عليه والحاصل أنه قد يكون فرض عين إن تعين، وفرض كفاية للأهل عند وجود غيره، ومكروها عند خوف الظلم، وحراما إن غلب على ظنه ذلك، ومباحا كما مر وإنما وجب عليه إذا تعين صيانة لحقوق المسلمين. (عيني، فتح)
And it is disliked to accept [the post of qadi] for one who fears injustice (hayf), but if he is secure from it, then it is not [disliked]. And do not ask for it. And it is permissible to accept judgeship from the just, the unjust, and from the rebels. 'And it is disliked to accept' – meaning with abominable dislike (karahah tahrirn) for one who fears injustice (hayf) in it, which is oppression and tyranny. Al-hayf (with ha) means inclination, and its synonym is janaf (with jim and nun). From it is the verse: {But if one fears from the testator an inclination [janaf]...} (al-Baqarah 2:182). The conclusion is that one who fears inability to perform the duty of judgeship and does not feel secure from oppression in it, entering it is disliked so that his entry does not become a means to commit evil, i.e., oppression in judgment. (Fath, 'Inayah) 'And if he is secure from it' – meaning if he feels secure from oppression in judgeship, then entering it is not disliked for him, following the example of many Companions and Successors. And it is said: it is disliked even if voluntary, even if he feels secure from oppression; do you not see that many scholars like al-Sha'bi and Abu Hanifa refrained? It is narrated that Abu Hanifa was called to judgeship three times and refused, until he was imprisoned and flogged each time thirty lashes, until Abu Yusuf said: 'If you accept, you would benefit the people.' He looked at him with anger and said: 'If I were commanded to cross the ocean swimming, I would be more capable of that, and I see you as a judge!' And due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever is afflicted with judgeship, it is as if he is slaughtered without a knife.' There is no contradiction between this and the earlier statement that judgeship is the best of acts of worship, because the earlier is carried on the case when it becomes personally obligatory. The conclusion is: it may be fard ayn if it becomes personally obligatory, fard kifayah for the qualified when others exist, disliked when one fears injustice, haram if that becomes his overwhelming assumption, and permissible as mentioned. It only becomes obligatory on him when it becomes personally obligatory to safeguard the rights of Muslims. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on accepting the position of a judge (qadi). It begins by stating that it is disliked (makruh) for one who fears injustice (hayf), but if he feels secure from injustice, it is not disliked. The commentary specifies that the dislike is of the abominable type (karahah tahrirniyyah), close to prohibition. The reasoning is that fear of being unable to fulfill the duties or of falling into oppression makes acceptance a means to evil. The text then discusses that if one is secure from injustice, acceptance is not disliked, citing the examples of many pious predecessors who accepted the position. However, there is a view that it remains disliked even if voluntary, as evidenced by the reluctance of Abu Hanifa, who was forced into it and suffered punishment. The hadith 'Whoever is afflicted with judgeship is as if slaughtered without a knife' is quoted to show the severity. The commentary resolves the apparent contradiction with the earlier statement that judgeship is the best of acts of worship: the excellence applies when it is personally obligatory (fard ayn); otherwise it can be fard kifayah, or even haram if one is certain he will be unjust. The final ruling is that acceptance becomes obligatory only when it is personally necessary to protect the rights of Muslims. This shows the nuanced Hanafi approach: the ruling changes based on the individual's circumstance and capability. The passage explicitly mentions the intra-Hanafi difference (Abu Hanifa's refusal vs. others' acceptance) but does not resolve it; the relied-upon view is that it is not disliked if one feels secure.

Key Terms
الحيف (al-hayf): Injustice, oppression, or deviation from truth in judgment.
كراهة تحريم (karahah tahrirniyyah): Abominable dislike, close to prohibition. An action that is not haram but strongly discouraged, and there is sin if done without necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is accepting judgeship prohibited (haram)?
A) When the qadi fears he will be unjust.
B) When the qadi is confident he can be just.
C) When it is a fard kifayah and others can do it.
D) When the ruler is unjust.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on accepting judgeship is conditional: it is prohibitively disliked when one fears injustice, but not disliked when one feels secure from it, and it becomes obligatory only when it is personally necessary to protect Muslim rights.

Prohibition of Seeking Judgeship
Ruling that seeking judgeship is prohibited, with hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on seeking judgeship, identify the hadith proof, and explain the consequence of seeking versus being compelled.

Core Idea
Seeking judgeship leaves one without divine support; only if compelled is one aided.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that one should not seek the position of a judge. The matn declares it impermissible to ask for judgeship, and the commentary provides a hadith as evidence. Understanding this prohibition helps you grasp the broader principle regarding seeking positions of authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is seeking judgeship (requesting it by tongue or heart). The ruling is that it is not permissible (la yanbaghi) – a prohibition.
Note the double emphasis: not to ask with the tongue nor desire with the heart.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever seeks judgeship is entrusted to himself.' The hadith is the core evidence.
This is a dalil naqli (textual evidence).
3
Analyse the hadith's meaning
'Wukkila ila nafsihi' means delegated to his own self – abandoned without divine assistance. The self commands evil, so he will fail. Contrast with the exception: if compelled (ujbira), an angel descends to guide him.
The exception shows that acceptance without seeking is permissible and even supported by divine aid.
4
Extend the ruling
The commentary extends the prohibition to all positions of authority (wilayah). Al-Khulasa states: the seeker of appointment is not appointed unless it becomes obligatory or the appointment is conditional (e.g., waqf trustee).
This is an expansion (expansion_probe) of the original rule.
5
Apply the ruling
The Hanafi school strongly discourages actively seeking any public office. If one is forced to accept or if it becomes an obligation, it becomes permissible.
Practical takeaway: avoid campaigning for positions of authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسأله: [ أي لا ينبغي لأحد أن يسأل القضاء؛ لأنه حينئذ يعتمد على نفسه فيعجز منه. (عيني، مسكين)] أي من صلح للقضاء لا يسأل القضاء بلسانه ولا يطلبه بقلبه؛ لقوله عليه: "من طلب القضاء وكل إلى نفسه، أجبر عليه نزل عليه ملك يسدده ، وكل على صيغة المبني للمفعول بالتخفيف أي فوض أمره إليها، ومن فوض أمره إلى نفسه كان مخذولا غير مرشـ شد إلى الصواب؛ لأن النفس أمارة بالسوء، ويسدده" أي يلهمه الرشد ويوفقه للصواب، وكما لايجوز طلب القضاء لا يجوز طلب التولية ولهذا قال في "الخلاصة": طالب التولية لا يولى إلا إذا تعين عليها أو كانت التولية على الوقف مشروطة. (عيني، فتح)
And do not ask for it: [meaning, it is not fit for anyone to ask for judgeship, because then he relies on himself and thus becomes incapable. (Ayni, Miskin)] That is, one who is fit for judgeship should not ask for judgeship with his tongue nor seek it with his heart, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever seeks judgeship is entrusted to himself. If he is compelled [to accept], a king descends upon him and directs him aright.' 'Entrusted to himself' (wukkila ila nafsihi) is in the passive form (mabni li al-maf'ul) with tashdid? No, with takhfif, meaning his affair is delegated to it (his self). Whoever delegates his affair to himself becomes abandoned and not guided to what is correct, because the self is prone to evil. 'And directs him aright' means He inspires him with guidance and helps him to the truth. Just as seeking judgeship is not permissible, so too seeking any appointment (wilayah) is not permissible, and for this reason it is stated in al-Khulasa: The seeker of appointment is not appointed unless it becomes incumbent upon him or the appointment is conditional upon the waqf. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of actively seeking the position of a judge. The matn states: wala yas'aluhu ('and do not ask for it'). The commentary explains that one who is qualified for judgeship should not ask for it verbally nor desire it in his heart. The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever seeks judgeship is entrusted to himself.' This means that Allah leaves him to his own devices, and since the human self (nafs) commands evil, he will be unsupported and prone to error. The hadith also mentions that if one is compelled (ujbira) to accept, then an angel (malak) descends to direct him. This implies that seeking is blameworthy, but if the position is imposed, there is divine assistance. The commentary extends the ruling to all positions of authority (wilayah), citing al-Khulasa: the seeker is not appointed unless it becomes obligatory on him (ta'ayyana) or in the case of a conditional appointment like a trustee of a waqf. Thus, the Hanafi position strongly discourages seeking public office, but allows acceptance if it is not sought.

Key Terms
وكل إلى نفسه (wukkila ila nafsihi): Entrusted to his own self, meaning left without divine support because he sought the position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is appointed and rewarded.
B) He is entrusted to his own self and left without support.
C) He is automatically disqualified.
D) An angel descends to guide him.

Key Takeaway
Seeking judgeship or any position of authority is prohibited in the Hanafi school due to the hadith warning that the seeker is left to his own self, unless one is compelled to accept.

Accepting Judgeship from Unjust and Rebel Rulers: The Hanafi Position
Ruling that it is permissible to accept judgeship even from unjust rulers and rebels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting judgeship from unjust and rebel rulers, and identify the condition that changes the ruling to prohibition.

Core Idea
A qualified judge may accept appointment even from an unjust ruler, provided he can uphold truth.

This lesson explores a critical Hanafi fiqh ruling: that it is permissible to accept the position of judge (qadi) even when the appointing authority is unjust or a rebel. This ruling, based on the practice of the Salaf and Companions, is conditional on the judge's ability to implement justice. Understanding this ruling highlights the pragmatic balance the Hanafi school strikes between political realities and the imperative to uphold the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify Key Terms: Just, Unjust, Rebel
Read the definitions of 'adil (just), ja'ir (unjust), and ahl al-baghy (rebels). Understand that 'just' means one who places things in their proper place, and 'unjust' means oppressor. Rebels are those who revolt against the legitimate imam but are still Muslims.
These definitions set the stage for the ruling.
2
State the General Ruling: Permissibility
The text states that there is no difference in the permissibility of accepting judgeship for a qualified person, whether the appointing authority is just, unjust, or a rebel. The general ruling is jawaz (permissibility).
This is based on the practice of the Salaf, e.g., accepting from al-Hajjaj.
3
Note the Exception: Inability to Judge with Truth
If the judge cannot judge according to truth (e.g., due to coercion or corruption), then accepting the position becomes haram (prohibited). This exception is critical.
The condition for permissibility is the ability to uphold justice.
4
Understand the Evidence: Practice of Salaf and Companions
The Salaf accepted judgeship from al-Hajjaj, an unjust governor. Similarly, the Companions accepted from Mu'awiyah, who was considered a rebel (baghi) in his conflict with 'Ali. This shows the precedent.
The hadith of 'Ammar ibn Yasir and 'Ali's statement "our brothers have rebelled" support this.
5
Apply the Condition to a Modern Scenario
Consider a modern judge in a country with an oppressive regime. If the judge can rule independently according to Islamic law on personal status matters, acceptance is permissible. If the regime dictates verdicts, it becomes haram.
This illustrates the practical application of the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز تقلد إلخ العادل: هو الواضع كل شيء ،موضعه، وقيل: هو المتوسط بين طرفي الإفراط والتفريط، وقيل: الجامع بين أمهات كمالات الإنسان والجائر هو الظالم أي لا فرق في جواز التقلد لأهله بين أن يكون المولي عادلا أو جائراً؛ لأن علماء السلف تقلدوه من الحجاج، إلا إذا كان لا يمكنه القضاء بالحق، فيحرم عليه حينئذ، وفي هذا الزمان لا يوجد السلطان العادل حتى قالوا من قال لسلطان هذا الزمان عدلت أو أنت عادل يكفر. وقوله: "ومن أهل البغي" أي ويجوز التقلد أيضاً من الذين خرجوا عن طاعة الإمام؛ لأن الصحابة تقلدوه من معاوية، وكان باغيا في نوبة عليه، وكان الحق بيد علي دل على ذلك حديث عمار بن ياسر، وقد قال علي ه: إخواننا بغوا علينا، وقال الإمام أبو محمد الناصحي في "تهذيب أدب القاضي" للخصاف قاضي أهل البغي -
And it is permissible to accept [judgeship] etc. 'The just' (al-adil) is one who places everything in its proper place. It is said: he is the middle between excess and deficiency. It is said: he is the one who combines the quintessential human perfections. 'The unjust' (al-ja'ir) is the oppressor. That is, there is no difference in the permissibility of accepting [judgeship] for its qualified person whether the appointing authority is just or unjust, because the Salaf accepted it from al-Hajjaj. Except when he cannot judge according to truth, then it becomes prohibited for him. In this time, there is no just sultan, so much so that they said: whoever says to the sultan of this time 'You are just' or 'You are a just ruler' commits disbelief. And his statement: 'and from the rebels' means it is permissible to accept also from those who have revolted against the imam, because the Companions accepted it from Mu'awiyah – and he was a rebel (baghi) in his conflict with 'Ali, though the truth was with 'Ali. This is indicated by the hadith of 'Ammar ibn Yasir. And 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us.' And Imam Abu Muhammad al-Nasihi said in Tahdhib Adab al-Qadi by al-Khassaf: 'The judge of the rebels...'
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of accepting the post of judge from any appointing authority, whether just or unjust, or even from rebels. It defines 'adil (just) and ja'ir (unjust), then states the ruling: there is no difference in the permissibility of acceptance for a qualified person, citing that the righteous predecessors (salaf) accepted judgeship from al-Hajjaj, an unjust governor. However, this is conditional on being able to implement justice; if the judge cannot rule with truth, then it becomes prohibited (haram). The passage then notes that in the current time (addressing the author's era) there is no just ruler, so much so that calling the ruler just is tantamount to disbelief. The second part discusses accepting judgeship from rebels (ahl al-baghy). It is permissible, just as the Companions accepted judgeship from Mu'awiyah during his conflict with 'Ali, even though Mu'awiyah was considered a rebel. The evidence is that 'Ali acknowledged them as 'brothers who rebelled' and the hadith of 'Ammar ibn Yasir indicates that the truth was with 'Ali. Thus, the Hanafi position is practical: judgeship serves the interests of the people, and as long as the judge can maintain justice, accepting from any de facto authority is allowed. This shows the flexibility of the madhhab in dealing with political realities.

Key Terms
الجائر (al-ja'ir): The unjust ruler or oppressor.
أهل البغي (ahl al-baghy): Rebels who revolt against the legitimate imam, but are still Muslims.
العادل (al-adil): The just ruler, one who places everything in its proper place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the ruler is always just in such cases.
D) Because rebels are not Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Accepting judgeship from unjust or rebel rulers is permissible in the Hanafi school, provided the judge can uphold truth; otherwise it becomes prohibited.

Rulings on Accepting and Seeking Judgeship
Rulings on accepting judgeship and seeking it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting judgeship based on the individual's ability to avoid injustice, and identify when it becomes makruh tahrimi or permissible.

Core Idea
Accepting judgeship is prohibitively disliked if you fear injustice, but if safe, it is not disliked—and you should never seek it.

This lesson from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the foundational Hanafi ruling on the office of the judge (qadi). It addresses when it is disliked, when it is permissible, and whether one should actively seek the position. The text also cites the prophetic warning about the heavy responsibility of judgeship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditions
The act under discussion is accepting the position of judge (taqallud al-qada). The ruling depends on the individual's condition regarding fear of injustice (hayf).
Hayf means deviating from the truth in judgment.
2
Determine the two scenarios
First scenario: the person fears he will commit injustice (khafa al-hayf). Second scenario: he is safe from injustice (amina min al-hayf).
3
Apply the corresponding ruling
For the one who fears injustice: it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). For the one who is safe: it is not disliked.
The text explicitly says 'wa kariha al-taqallud liman khafa al-hayf, wa in amina la' (it is disliked for one who fears injustice, and if safe, it is not).
4
Note the additional rule: do not seek it
Even for the safe, one should not ask for the position. The hadith warns that whoever seeks judgeship will be entrusted to himself.
The text says 'wa la yas'aluhu' (he should not ask for it).
5
Recognize the evidence (dalil)
The hadith: 'Whoever is afflicted with judgeship has been slaughtered without a knife.' This indicates the grave responsibility, discouraging seeking it.
The hadith is cited in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أحكام القضاء

في يده لاحتمال الحاجة إليها، والأخرى في يد الخصم، وما في يد الخصم لا يؤمن عليه التغيير بزيادة أو نقصان. فإن كانت الأوراق من بيت المال، فلا إشكال في وضعها في يد القاضي الجديد، وكذا إذا كان من مال الخصوم أو مال القاضي في الصحيح، ويبعث عدلين من أمنائه، أو عدلاً واحداً، والاثنان أحوط؛ ليقبضا ديوان المعزول بحضرته، أو بحضرة أمينه، ويسألان المعزول شيئاً فشيئاً، فما كان فيها من نسخ السجلات يجمعانه في خريطة، وما كان من نصب الأوصياء يجمعانه في خريطة وما كان من نسخ فيها قيم الأوقاف يجمعانه في خريطة، وما كان الصكوك كذلك، فإذا قبضا ذلك حتما عليه تحرزاً عن التغيير رمز الحقائق: ۱۱۸/۲] (فتح) السجلات: جمع سجل بكسر السين والجيم وتشديد اللام وهو الصك. من
Book of Judgement. Rulings of Judgement: And it is disliked (with dislike of prohibition) for one who fears injustice (hīf), and if he is safe from it, it is not disliked. And he should not ask for it. And it is permissible to accept judgeship from a just sultan, an unjust one, and from the people of rebellion (ahl al-baghy). Then if he accepts, he asks for the dīwān of the previous judge, which are the files containing the records (sijillāt) and minutes (mahāḍir) and other things. And the Khawārij: if a judge is from among them and he judges something, then it is raised to another judge from the people of justice, he does not enforce it and invalidates it, because the Khawārij, when they rebel against the people of justice with weapons and deem their wealth permissible, their testimony is not permissible. And if their testimony is not permissible, their judgement is not permissible, so his ruling is not executed. This is the saying of Ibrāhīm al-Nakha‘ī. And his document is also not acted upon, because his judgement is not permissible, so his document is not acted upon. (Fath) Symbol of al-Haqā'iq 2:117. And 'from' means: it is also permissible to accept from, etc. (Aynī) 'He accepts' means he accepted judgeship and became a judge. (Ma'din) 'He asks' etc.: so that the condition of the prisoners becomes apparent to him, and it does not necessitate acting on the statement of the removed judge. (Fath) That is, when someone becomes a judge, he asks for the dīwān of the removed judge so as not to necessitate acting on his statement. And dīwān originally is 'dawnān', the first wāw changed to yā' for lightening, and it returns in the plural and diminutive like dawāwīn and duwaywīn. And his saying 'which are the files' means the dīwān is the files. This is a metaphor; the dīwān is the register, and it was called files due to the relationship of state and place, because files are the loci of the register. And 'kharā'iṭ' is plural of 'kharīṭah', which is a container of leather etc., i.e., a bag. And 'sijill' is the deed, i.e., what is written in it of sale, pledge, acknowledgment, etc. And 'mahḍar' is what is written in it of the dispute of the litigants and what occurred of acknowledgment or denial by the defendant, and judgement by evidence or refusal (nukūl). And 'hujjah' and 'wathīqah' encompass the three. He said in al-Jalālayn in the interpretation of the verse 'Kāṭay as-siḥill li al-kutub': 'sijill' is the name of the angel, and 'kitāb' is the scroll of the son of Adam at his death, and the lām is extra; or 'sijill' is the scroll and 'kitāb' is the written document and the lām means 'against'; and in a reading: 'lil-kutub' plural. And from this is the judge's 'isjāl' and 'tasjīl'. And the reason why the records and minutes etc. are placed in the dīwān is to be a proof when needed, and it is placed in the hand of the one who has the authority of judgement. And this is because the judge writes two copies: one in his hand for the possibility of need, and the other in the hand of the litigant. And what is in the hand of the litigant is not safe from change by increase or decrease. If the papers are from the public treasury (bayt al-māl), there is no issue in placing them in the hand of the new judge. Similarly if it is from the property of the litigants or the property of the judge according to the correct view. And he sends two just witnesses from his trustees, or one just witness, and two is more cautious, to take possession of the dīwān of the removed judge in his presence or in the presence of his trustee. And they ask the removed judge little by little. What is in it of copies of records, they collect in a file; what is of appointments of executors, they collect in a file; what is of copies of valuations of endowments, they collect in a file; and what is of deeds similarly. So when they have taken possession of it, he seals it to guard against change. (Fath) Symbol of al-Haqā'iq 2:118. 'Sijillāt' is plural of 'sijill' with kasra on sīn and jīm and tashdīd on lām, which is the deed. (Aynī) 'Maḥāḍir' is plural of 'maḥḍar', which is what is written in it of the dispute of the litigants and the like, and it is said that 'maḥḍar' is the contract (qibāla). (Fath, Ma'din and others from the appointment of executors and trustees in endowment funds, and estimation of maintenance and the like. (Miskīn) From.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq presents the foundational rulings regarding the office of the judge (qāḍī) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating that it is makrūh taḥrīmī (prohibitively disliked) for a person to accept the position of judge if he fears that he will commit injustice (ḥayf) due to weakness or lack of ability. However, if he feels confident that he can judge justly, then it is not disliked. In fact, accepting judgeship can become obligatory (farḍ kifāya) if no other qualified person is available. The passage then emphasizes that one should not seek or ask for the position of judge, citing a ḥadīth: 'Whoever seeks judgeship will be entrusted to himself.' This is because seeking shows overconfidence and leads to failure. As for the appointment, it is permissible to accept judgeship whether the ruler is just or unjust, and even from rebels (ahl al-baghy), as long as the judge can rule by the truth. However, if the ruler is so oppressive that the judge cannot execute justice, then it becomes forbidden. The text also addresses the case of judges from the Khawārij (a rebel sect). Their judgments are not valid because their testimony is not accepted, and therefore their judicial decisions are not enforceable. Finally, the passage details the procedure for a new judge to request the previous judge's dīwān (record files) to ensure continuity and prevent tampering. The new judge should take possession of these records, which include deeds, minutes of court proceedings, appointments of guardians, and endowment valuations. This safeguards the rights of litigants and maintains the integrity of the judicial system. The commentary from Fatḥ al-Qadīr and other sources elaborates on the definitions of technical terms such as sijill (deed), maḥḍar (court minutes), and kharīṭa (file).

Key Terms
التقلّد (al-taqallud): Accepting the position of judge (qadi).
الحيف (al-hayf): Injustice, oppression, deviation from the truth in judgment.
البغي (al-baghy): Rebellion, transgression; here referring to rebel groups who revolt against the legitimate ruler.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is makruh tahrimi.
B) It is not disliked.
C) It is haram.
D) It is wajib 'ayn.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of accepting judgeship depends on one's ability to avoid injustice, and seeking it is prohibited.

Rulings on Khawarij Judgments and Transfer of Court Records
Rulings on Khawarij judgments and record transfer procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a Khawarij judge's ruling is invalid and describe the procedure for a new judge to request and secure the previous judge's court records.

Core Idea
Khawarij judgments are void because their testimony is invalid; a new judge must secure records to prevent fraud.

This lesson continues the rulings on judgeship by addressing two important topics: the validity of judgments from Khawarij (rebel) judges, and the proper procedure for a new judge to obtain the court records (diwan) from his predecessor. Both rulings ensure the integrity of the judicial system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: judgment of a Khawarij judge
The text discusses a case where a judge from the Khawarij sect has passed a ruling, and then the case comes before a just judge. What should the just judge do?
Khawarij are those who rebelled against the legitimate ruler and declared Muslims as disbelievers.
2
Determine the reason for invalidity
The text explains: because the Khawarij are rebels who use weapons and consider Muslim wealth permissible, their testimony is not accepted. If their testimony is invalid, their judgment (which depends on testimony) is also invalid.
The legal link: qada (judgment) relies on shahada (testimony); invalid testimony → invalid judgment.
3
State the ruling: do not enforce
The just judge does not enforce the Khawarij judge's ruling; he invalidates it (abtalahu). Also, any document written by the Khawarij judge is not acted upon.
The text says 'lam yajizhu wa abtalahu' (he does not enforce it and invalidates it).
4
Understand the record transfer procedure
When a new judge takes office, he must ask for the diwan (records) of the previous judge. The diwan contains sijillat (deeds), mahadir (court minutes), etc. This is to ensure continuity and prevent tampering.
The new judge sends two just trustees to take possession of the records in the presence of the removed judge or his deputy.
5
Apply the safeguarding measures
The records are classified by type (deeds, appointments of executors, endowment valuations, etc.) and placed in separate files (kharait). They are then sealed to prevent alteration.
This meticulous procedure protects the rights of litigants and maintains judicial integrity.

Source Text

من
Book of Judgement. Rulings of Judgement: And it is disliked (with dislike of prohibition) for one who fears injustice (hīf), and if he is safe from it, it is not disliked. And he should not ask for it. And it is permissible to accept judgeship from a just sultan, an unjust one, and from the people of rebellion (ahl al-baghy). Then if he accepts, he asks for the dīwān of the previous judge, which are the files containing the records (sijillāt) and minutes (mahāḍir) and other things. And the Khawārij: if a judge is from among them and he judges something, then it is raised to another judge from the people of justice, he does not enforce it and invalidates it, because the Khawārij, when they rebel against the people of justice with weapons and deem their wealth permissible, their testimony is not permissible. And if their testimony is not permissible, their judgement is not permissible, so his ruling is not executed. This is the saying of Ibrāhīm al-Nakha‘ī. And his document is also not acted upon, because his judgement is not permissible, so his document is not acted upon. (Fath) Symbol of al-Haqā'iq 2:117. And 'from' means: it is also permissible to accept from, etc. (Aynī) 'He accepts' means he accepted judgeship and became a judge. (Ma'din) 'He asks' etc.: so that the condition of the prisoners becomes apparent to him, and it does not necessitate acting on the statement of the removed judge. (Fath) That is, when someone becomes a judge, he asks for the dīwān of the removed judge so as not to necessitate acting on his statement. And dīwān originally is 'dawnān', the first wāw changed to yā' for lightening, and it returns in the plural and diminutive like dawāwīn and duwaywīn. And his saying 'which are the files' means the dīwān is the files. This is a metaphor; the dīwān is the register, and it was called files due to the relationship of state and place, because files are the loci of the register. And 'kharā'iṭ' is plural of 'kharīṭah', which is a container of leather etc., i.e., a bag. And 'sijill' is the deed, i.e., what is written in it of sale, pledge, acknowledgment, etc. And 'mahḍar' is what is written in it of the dispute of the litigants and what occurred of acknowledgment or denial by the defendant, and judgement by evidence or refusal (nukūl). And 'hujjah' and 'wathīqah' encompass the three. He said in al-Jalālayn in the interpretation of the verse 'Kāṭay as-siḥill li al-kutub': 'sijill' is the name of the angel, and 'kitāb' is the scroll of the son of Adam at his death, and the lām is extra; or 'sijill' is the scroll and 'kitāb' is the written document and the lām means 'against'; and in a reading: 'lil-kutub' plural. And from this is the judge's 'isjāl' and 'tasjīl'. And the reason why the records and minutes etc. are placed in the dīwān is to be a proof when needed, and it is placed in the hand of the one who has the authority of judgement. And this is because the judge writes two copies: one in his hand for the possibility of need, and the other in the hand of the litigant. And what is in the hand of the litigant is not safe from change by increase or decrease. If the papers are from the public treasury (bayt al-māl), there is no issue in placing them in the hand of the new judge. Similarly if it is from the property of the litigants or the property of the judge according to the correct view. And he sends two just witnesses from his trustees, or one just witness, and two is more cautious, to take possession of the dīwān of the removed judge in his presence or in the presence of his trustee. And they ask the removed judge little by little. What is in it of copies of records, they collect in a file; what is of appointments of executors, they collect in a file; what is of copies of valuations of endowments, they collect in a file; and what is of deeds similarly. So when they have taken possession of it, he seals it to guard against change. (Fath) Symbol of al-Haqā'iq 2:118. 'Sijillāt' is plural of 'sijill' with kasra on sīn and jīm and tashdīd on lām, which is the deed. (Aynī) 'Maḥāḍir' is plural of 'maḥḍar', which is what is written in it of the dispute of the litigants and the like, and it is said that 'maḥḍar' is the contract (qibāla). (Fath, Ma'din and others from the appointment of executors and trustees in endowment funds, and estimation of maintenance and the like. (Miskīn) From.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq presents the foundational rulings regarding the office of the judge (qāḍī) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating that it is makrūh taḥrīmī (prohibitively disliked) for a person to accept the position of judge if he fears that he will commit injustice (ḥayf) due to weakness or lack of ability. However, if he feels confident that he can judge justly, then it is not disliked. In fact, accepting judgeship can become obligatory (farḍ kifāya) if no other qualified person is available. The passage then emphasizes that one should not seek or ask for the position of judge, citing a ḥadīth: 'Whoever seeks judgeship will be entrusted to himself.' This is because seeking shows overconfidence and leads to failure. As for the appointment, it is permissible to accept judgeship whether the ruler is just or unjust, and even from rebels (ahl al-baghy), as long as the judge can rule by the truth. However, if the ruler is so oppressive that the judge cannot execute justice, then it becomes forbidden. The text also addresses the case of judges from the Khawārij (a rebel sect). Their judgments are not valid because their testimony is not accepted, and therefore their judicial decisions are not enforceable. Finally, the passage details the procedure for a new judge to request the previous judge's dīwān (record files) to ensure continuity and prevent tampering. The new judge should take possession of these records, which include deeds, minutes of court proceedings, appointments of guardians, and endowment valuations. This safeguards the rights of litigants and maintains the integrity of the judicial system. The commentary from Fatḥ al-Qadīr and other sources elaborates on the definitions of technical terms such as sijill (deed), maḥḍar (court minutes), and kharīṭa (file).

Key Terms
الخوارج (al-khawarij): A historical rebel group that revolted against the legitimate Imam; their testimony and judgments are invalid.
الديوان (al-diwan): The record office or collection of documents (files, deeds, minutes) of the court.
السجل (al-sijill): A written deed or document recording a transaction, judgment, or acknowledgment.
المحضر (al-mahdar): A written record of the proceedings in court, including the pleadings and evidence.
الخريطة (al-kharita): A file or bag for storing documents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the ruling of a Khawarij judge not enforced?
A) Because he is not appointed by a legitimate ruler.
B) Because his testimony is invalid, so his judgment is void.
C) Because the litigants are also Khawarij.
D) Because the case was not recorded in the diwan.

Key Takeaway
Judgments from those whose testimony is invalid (like the Khawarij) are automatically void, and proper record-keeping procedures are essential for judicial integrity.

Procedure for Transferring Judicial Records to the New Judge
Procedure for new judge to obtain predecessor's records

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the steps a new Hanafi judge must take to obtain and secure the predecessor's diwan, and explain the rationale for each step.

Core Idea
The new judge must secure the diwan through trustworthy men to preserve evidence and avoid relying solely on the dismissed judge's word.

This lesson explains the proper procedure for a new judge to take custody of the judicial archives (al-diwan) from his predecessor. The Hanafi school emphasizes the importance of written records and their secure transfer to ensure continuity of justice and prevent tampering. The text, drawn from Fath al-Qadir and other commentaries, details how the new judge should request the records, send reliable agents, and organize the documents by type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Initial Duty
Recognize that the new judge is required to request the diwan of his predecessor. The text begins with 'if he is appointed, he asks for the diwan' – this indicates a procedural duty, not a strict obligation.
This step ensures the judge has access to all prior case records.
2
Define the Diwan and Its Components
The diwan consists of wallets (khara'it) containing sijillat (deeds of sale, pledge, etc.), mahadir (minutes of litigation), and other documents. Understand the distinction between sijill and mahdar.
Sijill = final deed; mahdar = record of proceedings.
3
Determine the Source of the Papers
Classify who owns the papers: public treasury (bayt al-mal), litigants, or the judge. According to the correct view (as-sahih), the same procedure applies regardless of ownership.
If from bayt al-mal, it is straightforward; otherwise, the new judge still takes custody.
4
Describe the Transfer Process
The new judge sends two trustworthy men (or one, but two is more cautious) to receive the diwan in the presence of the dismissed judge or his trustee. They collect the documents, sort them by type (sijillat, appointments, endowments, etc.), and then seal them to prevent alteration.
The presence of witnesses and sealing ensure integrity.
5
Apply the Rationale
The reason for this procedure is to uncover the state of prisoners and other matters, and to avoid relying on the dismissed judge's word, which is not authoritative. The sealing protects against tampering.
This preserves the chain of custody and evidential value.

The diwan of the just and unjust sultan, and of the rebels, then if he is appointed as judge he asks for the diwan of the judge before him, and it is the wallets containing the records, minutes, and others. ... Then if the papers are from the public treasury, there is no problem in placing them in the hands of the new judge, and likewise if from the property of the litigants or the judge according to the correct view. And he sends two trustworthy men from his trustees, or one trustworthy man, and two is more cautious, to take possession of the diwan of the dismissed judge in his presence or in the presence of his trustee, and they ask the dismissed judge little by little. Whatever among them of copies of records they collect in a wallet, and whatever of appointment of guardians they collect in a wallet, and whatever of copies containing valuations of endowments they collect in a wallet, and what of documents likewise. So when they take possession of that, they seal it to guard against alteration.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from Fath al-Qadir, 'Ayni, and Ma'dan explaining the procedure for a new judge to take over the judicial records (diwan) from his predecessor. The new judge must request the diwan to uncover the state of prisoners and avoid reliance on the dismissed judge's word. The diwan consists of wallets (khara'it) containing various documents: records of transactions (sijillat), minutes of hearings (mahadir), and other legal documents. The commentary defines key terms: diwan originally means a register, metaphorically the wallets containing registers; sijill refers to a deed documenting sale, pledge, or acknowledgment; mahdir records the litigation proceedings. The judge writes two copies: one for his record and one for the litigant. When handing over, the new judge sends two trustworthy men (or one, but two is more cautious) to receive the diwan from the dismissed judge. They organize the documents by type—sijillat, appointments, endowments—and seal them to prevent tampering. This ensures continuity and preserves evidence.

Key Terms
الديوان (al-diwan): The collection of judicial records (wallets containing sijillat, mahadir, etc.) passed from the dismissed judge to the new judge.
السجل (al-sijill): A legal deed (صك) documenting sales, pledges, acknowledgments, etc.
المحضر (al-mahdar): A written record of litigation proceedings, including acknowledgment, denial, and judgment.
الخريطة (al-kharita): A wallet or bag used to store and organize documents by type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why does the new judge send two trustworthy men to take possession of the diwan from the dismissed judge?
A) To honor the dismissed judge
B) To ensure the documents are not tampered with and to have witnesses for the transfer
C) To have them witness the dismissal
D) To make the process faster

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi procedure for transferring judicial records requires the new judge to request the diwan, send trustworthy agents to collect and organize the documents, and seal them to prevent tampering.

New Judge's Duty to Inspect Prisoners
Rules for new judge inspecting prisoners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the procedure a new Hanafi judge must follow when inspecting prisoners from the previous judge, and explain the reasoning behind each step.

Core Idea
A new judge must verify every prisoner's case before release — no one is held on the previous judge's word alone.

When a new qadi takes office, he inherits the previous judge's prisoners. But he cannot simply release or keep them based on that judge's decisions. Instead, Hanafi law requires a careful inspection: this passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lays out the ruling with its rationale, ensuring justice and preventing arbitrary detention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judge's primary duty
The new judge must inspect the condition of every prisoner inherited from the previous judge. This is a wajib duty because he is appointed as an overseer for the affairs of Muslims.
Inspection applies only to prisoners in the judge's own prison; those in the governor's prison fall under the imam's authority.
2
Distinguish prisoners with established claims
If a prisoner has acknowledged a right (confessed) or there is evidence (bayyinah) against him, the judge must keep him imprisoned. Confession and evidence are binding proofs.
The judge does not re-try the entire case; he verifies the existence of a claim.
3
Handle prisoners without claims
If a prisoner has neither confessed nor has evidence against him, the judge must order a public announcement in the prisoner's locality, calling for any claimants.
The announcement is made by a town crier, specifying the prisoner's name and father's name.
4
Conclude the announcement process
If a claimant appears, the judge hears the case and adjudicates. If no one appears, the judge takes a surety (kafil) from the prisoner and releases him. This ensures the prisoner is not lost if a future claim arises.
The surety guarantees the prisoner's appearance if needed later.
5
Apply additional rules
No prisoner should spend the night in shackles except one demanded for a blood claim (qisas). The expenses of an indigent prisoner are borne by the public treasury (bayt al-mal).
These rules ensure humane treatment and prevent undue hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني)] أي في سجن القاضي، أما المحبوسين في سجن الوالي فعلى الإمام أو نائبه النظر في أحوالهم، فمن لزمه أدب أدبه، وإلا أطلقه ولا يبيت أحد في قيد إلا رجلاً مطلوباً بدم، ونفقة من ليس له مال في بيت المال. (فتح) بحق ألزمه إياه؛ لأن الإقرار ملزم (عيني) ألزمه: أي ألزم القاضي الجديد ذلك المحبوس بالحبس. عيني (ط) وإلا أي وإن لم يقر هو بشيء أو لم يكن عليه بينة. ينادى: [ أي نادى مناد من جهة القاضي الجديد. (عيني)] قيد الشارحون النداء بمحلة المحبوس، ويقول المنادي: من كان يطلب فلان ابن فلان المحبوس بحق فليحضر، حتى يجمع بينه وبينه، فإن حضر أحد وادعى عليه شيئاً، يحكم بينهما، وإلا يأخذ كفيلاً ويطلقه. عيني، فتح عليه: على المحبوس بقدر ما يرى.
He inspects the condition of the prisoners. So whoever has acknowledged a right or evidence is established against him, he (the judge) obliges him (keeps him imprisoned). Otherwise, a caller announces regarding him. From the prisoners, that. And he inspects, etc. [because he was appointed as an overseer for the affairs of Muslims. (Ayni)] i.e., in the judge's prison. As for those imprisoned in the governor's prison, it is upon the imam or his deputy to inspect their conditions. So whoever must be disciplined, he disciplines him; otherwise he releases him. And no one spends the night in shackles except a man demanded for blood. And the expenses of one who has no wealth are from the treasury. (Fath) 'With a right he obliges him' because acknowledgment is binding. (Ayni) 'obliges him' means the new judge obliges that prisoner to imprisonment. (Ayni) 'Otherwise' means if he does not acknowledge anything or there is no evidence against him. 'A caller announces' means a caller from the side of the new judge makes an announcement. The commentators restricted the announcement to the prisoner's neighborhood. The caller says: 'Whoever is seeking So-and-so son of So-and-so, the prisoner, with a right, let him come so that they may be brought together.' Then if someone comes and claims something against him, he judges between them; otherwise he takes a surety and releases him. (Ayni, Fath) 'upon him' means upon the prisoner to the extent he sees fit.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for a new judge regarding prisoners inherited from the previous judge. The judge must inspect each prisoner. If a prisoner acknowledges a debt or right, or if evidence is established against him, the judge continues his imprisonment. Otherwise, the judge must arrange for a public announcement in the prisoner's locality, calling for any claimants. If a claimant appears, the judge hears the case; if not, the judge takes a surety from the prisoner and releases him. The commentary distinguishes between prisoners in the judge's own prison (which he inspects) and those in the governor's prison (which falls under the ruler's authority). The rule applies to prisoners from the previous judge's tenure. The judge's expenses are borne by the treasury for those without means. The announcement is specifically made in the locality of the prison, not necessarily city-wide. The ruling ensures that no one is unjustly detained and that the new judge does not rely on the previous judge's word alone.

Key Terms
ينادى عليه (yunada 'alayhi): A public announcement is made to summon any claimants against the prisoner before release.
كفيل (kafil): A surety who guarantees the prisoner's appearance if needed later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the judge do for a prisoner who has not acknowledged a right and against whom no evidence is established?
A) Release him immediately
B) Keep him indefinitely
C) Make a public announcement and then release him on surety if no claimant appears
D) Transfer him to the governor's prison

Key Takeaway
A new judge must verify each prisoner's detention basis and release only after public announcement and surety.

Handling Deposits and Waqf Revenues by the New Judge
Ruling on deposits and waqf revenues: rely on evidence, not the dismissed judge's word

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on deposits and waqf revenues when a new judge takes office, distinguishing between valid and invalid sources of evidence.

Core Idea
A dismissed judge's statement alone does not bind the new judge; only evidence or the holder's acknowledgment suffices.

When a new judge assumes office, questions arise about deposits and waqf revenues previously handled by the dismissed judge. This lesson from the Hanafi fiqh of qada clarifies that the new judge must rely on objective evidence (bayyinah) or the acknowledgment of the current holder (dhul yad), not the dismissed judge's word. However, an exception exists when the holder acknowledges receipt from the dismissed judge, which constructively places the property in the judge's hand. Understanding these rules prevents property disputes and ensures proper administration of trusts and endowments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Evidentiary Rule
Read the opening lines of the passage. The new judge acts on deposits and waqf revenues based on bayyinah (clear evidence) or iqrar (acknowledgment) from the person in possession. He does not act on the mere statement of the dismissed judge.
This is the default rule.
2
Understand the Rationale for Rejecting the Dismissed Judge's Word
The commentary explains that the dismissed judge, after dismissal, becomes like any ordinary subject (ra'iyya). His testimony is not accepted, especially concerning his own actions, due to the principle that a single person's testimony is insufficient.
This prevents self-serving statements and protects property rights.
3
Examine the Exception: Holder's Acknowledgment
If the holder (dhul yad) acknowledges that the dismissed judge handed the property to him, this acknowledgment is accepted. The legal reason is that the holder's hand is like the judge's hand (yad al-mudi' ka yad al-qadi), making it as if the judge still possesses it.
This exception is based on the principle that the depositor's hand is equivalent to the judge's hand.
4
Analyze the Four Scenarios
The passage details four possibilities: (1) holder acknowledges to another person first, then to the judge; (2) holder denies receiving from the judge; (3) holder acknowledges receiving from the dismissed judge, then to another; (4) holder acknowledges receiving but doesn't know the owner. Each scenario has a distinct ruling regarding delivery and liability.
Work through each scenario to see how the ruling applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعمل في الودائع وغلات الوقف ببينة أو إقرار، ولم يعمل بقول المعزول إلا أن يقرّ القاضي الجديد أي محاصل الوقف ذو اليد أنه سلمها إليه ، فيقبل قوله فيها، الودائع: أي التي وضعها المعزول في أيدى الأمناء. (معدن) أو إقرار عمن في يده؛ لأن كل ذلك حجة، وإقرار غير مقبول. (عيني) بقول المعزول : لأنه بالعزل التحق بواحد من الرعايا، وشهادة الفرد ليست بمقبولة لاسيما على فعل نفسه. (عيني) إلا أن يقر إلخ لأنه ثبت بإقراره أنه مودع القاضي، ويد المودع كيده، فصار كأنه في يده في الحال؛ إذ لو كان بيده عياناً صح إقراره به، فكذا إذا كان في يد مودعه فيقبل إقراره به إلا إذا بدأ صاحب اليد بالإقرار لغيره، ثم أقر بتسليم القاضي إليه، والقاضي يقرّ به لغيره، فيسلم إلى المقر له الأول، ويضمن المقر قيمته للقاضي بإقراره الثاني والمسألة على أربعة أوجه: إما أن يقر بأنه سلمه إليه بعد ما أقر به لغيره، أو ينكر التسليم فحكمهما ما ذكرناه أو يقر بأن المعزول سلمه إليه، ثم يقرّ به لغيره، فلا يقبل إقراره للثاني؛ لأنه لما أقر بأن القاضي سلمه إليه صار كأنه في يد القاضي والرابع أن يقر بأن القاضي سلمه إليه، ثم يقول: لا أدري لمن هو، فحكمه ظاهر رمز الحقائق: ۱۱۸/۲] (فتح) فيها: الودائع وغلات الوقف.
And he acts regarding deposits and revenues of endowments based on evidence (bayyinah) or acknowledgment, and he does not act on the word of the dismissed judge. Except if the new judge—i.e., the holder of the endowment proceeds—acknowledges that he handed them over to him, then his statement is accepted. Deposits: i.e., those that the dismissed judge placed in the hands of trustees. (Ma'dan) Or acknowledgment from the one in whose hand it is, because all that is a proof, and acknowledgment is not acceptable (from the dismissed judge alone). (Ayni) 'On the word of the dismissed judge' because by dismissal he became like one of the subjects, and the testimony of a single person is not acceptable, especially regarding his own action. (Ayni) 'Except if he acknowledges, etc.' because it is established by his acknowledgment that he was the depositor of the judge, and the hand of the depositor is like his hand, so it is as if it is in his hand currently. For if it were in his hand physically, his acknowledgment would be valid; similarly, if it is in the hand of his depositor, his acknowledgment is accepted. Except if the holder first acknowledged it to someone else, then acknowledged that the judge handed it to him, while the judge acknowledges it to someone else, then it is given to the first acknowledgee, and the acknowledging party guarantees its value to the judge due to his second acknowledgment. The case has four possibilities: (1) he acknowledges that he handed it to him after having acknowledged it to someone else, (2) he denies the handover, (3) he acknowledges that the dismissed judge handed it to him, then acknowledges it to someone else, (4) he acknowledges that the judge handed it to him, then says 'I do not know to whom it belongs.'
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that the new judge must deal with deposits (wada'i') and waqf revenues (ghallat al-waqf) that were under the previous judge's administration based on clear evidence (bayyinah) or an acknowledgment from the current holder. The word of the dismissed judge alone is not accepted because after dismissal he is like an ordinary person, and a single person's testimony is not sufficient, especially regarding his own actions. However, an exception is made: if the person currently holding the property (the 'holder' or 'dhul yad') acknowledges that the dismissed judge had handed it to him, then his acknowledgment is accepted because it confirms that the new judge's intention is to recover the property. The commentary details four scenarios: (1) the holder acknowledges after already acknowledging to someone else, (2) he denies receipt, (3) he acknowledges receipt from the dismissed judge then acknowledges to another, (4) he acknowledges receipt but does not know the owner. Each scenario has its own rules for delivery and liability. The key principle is that the dismissed judge's statement is not proof, but the holder's acknowledgment is valid because it places the property constructively in the judge's hand.

Key Terms
الودائع (al-wada'i'): Deposits left with the judge or his agents by litigants or others.
غلات الوقف (ghallat al-waqf): Revenues generated from waqf (endowment) properties, such as rents.
ذو اليد (dhul yad): The person who is physically holding the property (the custodian or agent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the holder is a trustee
B) If the holder acknowledges that the dismissed judge handed it to him
C) If the dismissed judge confirms it
D) If the deposit is from a waqf

Key Takeaway
The new judge must not accept the dismissed judge's word; only bayyinah or iqrar from the holder is valid.

The Judge's Duty to Inspect Prisoners
Explains the judge's obligation to examine prisoners and act on evidence or confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's duty regarding prisoner inspection and apply the rule of acting on confession or evidence.

Core Idea
A judge must inspect prisoners; if confession or evidence exists, he binds them; otherwise, he proclaims and releases on guarantee.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq outlines the Hanafi ruling on the judge's obligation to examine prisoners. It distinguishes between prisoners under the judge's jurisdiction and those under the governor's, and specifies the conditions for continued detention or release. The underlying principle is that the judge is a guardian of the Muslims' interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge's inspection of prisoners. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) because the judge is appointed as a guardian of Muslims' affairs.
2
Distinguish the two categories of prisoners
The passage applies only to prisoners in the judge's prison. Prisoners in the governor's prison fall under the ruler or his deputy's authority.
3
Determine the action based on confession or evidence
If a prisoner confesses to a right or evidence (bayyinah) is established against him, the judge must bind him—i.e., continue his detention.
Confession (iqrar) is conclusive and binding in Hanafi law.
4
Apply the procedure when no confession or evidence exists
If neither confession nor evidence is present, the judge orders a public proclamation in the prisoner's neighbourhood for claimants to come forward. If no claimant appears, the judge takes a guarantor (kafil) and releases the prisoner.
The proclamation gives potential claimants an opportunity to assert their rights.
5
Note the special rule on shackling and maintenance
No one may be kept in shackles overnight except a man wanted for blood (homicide suspect). Prisoners without means are maintained from the public treasury (bayt al-mal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينظر في حال المحبوسين فمن أقرّ بحق أو قامت عليه بيّنة ألزمه، وإلا ينادى عليه. [لأنه نصب ناظرا لحال المسلمين. (عيني)] أي في سجن القاضي، أما المحبوسين في سجن الوالي فعلى الإمام أو نائبه النظر في أحوالهم، فمن لزمه أدب أدبه، وإلا أطلقه ولا يبيت أحد في قيد إلا رجلاً مطلوباً بدم، ونفقة من ليس له مال في بيت المال. (فتح) بحق ألزمه إياه؛ لأن الإقرار ملزم (عيني) ألزمه: أي ألزم القاضي الجديد ذلك المحبوس بالحبس. عيني (ط) وإلا أي وإن لم يقر هو بشيء أو لم يكن عليه بينة. ينادى: [ أي نادى مناد من جهة القاضي الجديد. (عيني)] قيد الشارحون النداء بمحلة المحبوس، ويقول المنادي: من كان يطلب فلان ابن فلان المحبوس بحق فليحضر، حتى يجمع بينه وبينه، فإن حضر أحد وادعى عليه شيئاً، يحكم بينهما، وإلا يأخذ كفيلاً ويطلقه. عيني، فتح عليه: على المحبوس بقدر ما يرى.
He looks into the condition of prisoners: whoever confesses to a right or against whom evidence is established, he binds him; otherwise, a proclamation is made about him. [Because he is appointed as a guardian of the Muslims' affairs.] That is, in the judge's prison. As for those imprisoned in the governor's prison, it is upon the Imam or his deputy to look into their conditions. Whoever is due discipline, he disciplines him; otherwise, he releases him. No one shall spend the night in shackles except a man wanted for blood. And maintenance for those who have no wealth is from the public treasury. (Fath) 'By a right'—he binds him with it, because confession is binding. (Ayni) 'He binds him' means the new judge binds that prisoner with imprisonment. (Ayni) 'Otherwise' means if he does not confess anything or no evidence is against him. 'A proclamation is made' means a caller from the new judge's side announces. (Ayni) The commentators restrict the proclamation to the prisoner's neighborhood. The caller says: 'Whoever has a claim against so-and-so, son of so-and-so, the prisoner, by a right, let him present himself,' so that he can bring them together. If someone appears and claims something against him, he judges between them; otherwise, he takes a guarantor and releases him. (Ayni, Fath) 'Upon him' means upon the prisoner to the extent he sees.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage details the judge's duty regarding prisoners. The matn states that the judge examines prisoners. If a prisoner confesses to a right or evidence establishes a claim against him, the judge must enforce it by keeping him imprisoned. If not, a proclamation is made so that potential claimants can come forward. The commentary clarifies that this applies to the judge's own prison; prisoners in the governor's prison are the concern of the ruler or his deputy. It also specifies that no one should be kept shackled overnight except a homicide suspect (talib bi dam). The judge may release the prisoner on a guarantor if no claimant appears. This ruling ensures that detention is not arbitrary and that the judge acts as a guardian of the Muslims' interests.

Key Terms
المحبوسين (al-mahbusin): Prisoners held in custody, specifically in the judge's prison or the governor's prison.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence that establishes a right or claim against a person.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or admission of a right or claim by the prisoner himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Release him immediately without any conditions
B) Keep him imprisoned until a claimant comes forward

D) Transfer him to the governor's prison

Key Takeaway
A judge must inspect prisoners; if no confession or evidence, he must make a proclamation and then release on a guarantor, ensuring no one is held unjustly.

How a New Judge Handles Deposits from a Dismissed Judge
Ruling on how the new judge deals with deposits and endowment proceeds left by the former judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on how a new judge deals with deposits and endowment proceeds left by a dismissed judge, and apply the exceptions regarding confessions.

Core Idea
A new judge cannot rely on a dismissed judge's word; only evidence or the holder's confession counts.

When a new judge takes office, he must verify the status of deposits and endowment proceeds left by his predecessor. The Hanafi ruling is clear: the dismissed judge's statement alone is not evidence. Instead, the new judge relies on clear proof or the confession of the person in possession. This lesson explains the rule and its nuances, including four scenarios of conflicting confessions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The new judge's handling of deposits and endowment proceeds left by the dismissed judge. The ruling: he acts on evidence (bayyinah) or confession (iqrar), not on the dismissed judge's word.
The dismissed judge is like an ordinary person after removal.
2
Understand the basis for the ruling
Why is the dismissed judge's word not accepted? Because dismissal makes him a common individual; his testimony is not acceptable, especially about his own actions.
This prevents self-serving statements that cannot be verified.
3
Recognize the exception
When is the dismissed judge's statement considered? Only if the holder (dhul yad) confesses that the judge had delivered the property to him. The holder's confession is accepted because his hand is like the judge's hand.
The holder's confession effectively makes the property as if it were in the judge's hand.
4
Apply the four scenarios
The passage details four scenarios of conflicting confessions by the holder. Understand each scenario's ruling.
Scenarios: (1) confesses to another then to judge, (2) denies delivery, (3) confesses judge delivered then to another, (4) confesses judge delivered but doesn't know owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعمل في الودائع وغلات الوقف ببينة أو إقرار، ولم يعمل بقول المعزول إلا أن يقرّ ذو اليد أنه سلمها إليه. الودائع: أي التي وضعها المعزول في أيدى الأمناء. (معدن) أو إقرار عمن في يده؛ لأن كل ذلك حجة، وإقرار غير مقبول. (عيني) بقول المعزول : لأنه بالعزل التحق بواحد من الرعايا، وشهادة الفرد ليست بمقبولة لاسيما على فعل نفسه. (عيني) إلا أن يقر إلخ لأنه ثبت بإقراره أنه مودع القاضي، ويد المودع كيده، فصار كأنه في يده في الحال؛ إذ لو كان بيده عياناً صح إقراره به، فكذا إذا كان في يد مودعه فيقبل إقراره به إلا إذا بدأ صاحب اليد بالإقرار لغيره، ثم أقر بتسليم القاضي إليه، والقاضي يقرّ به لغيره، فيسلم إلى المقر له الأول، ويضمن المقر قيمته للقاضي بإقراره الثاني والمسألة على أربعة أوجه: إما أن يقر بأنه سلمه إليه بعد ما أقر به لغيره، أو ينكر التسليم فحكمهما ما ذكرناه أو يقر بأن المعزول سلمه إليه، ثم يقرّ به لغيره، فلا يقبل إقراره للثاني؛ لأنه لما أقر بأن القاضي سلمه إليه صار كأنه في يد القاضي والرابع أن يقر بأن القاضي سلمه إليه، ثم يقول: لا أدري لمن هو، فحكمه ظاهر رمز الحقائق: ۱۱۸/۲] (فتح) فيها: الودائع وغلات الوقف.
He acts regarding deposits and endowment proceeds based on evidence or confession, and he does not act on the statement of the dismissed judge unless the holder (dhul yad) confesses that he had delivered them to him. 'Deposits' means those placed by the dismissed judge with trustees. (Ma'din) Or confession from the one in whose hand it is, because all that is a proof, and the confession of the dismissed judge is not accepted. (Ayni) 'He does not act on the statement of the dismissed judge' because by dismissal he has become like one of the subjects, and the testimony of a single person is not acceptable, especially regarding his own action. (Ayni) 'Unless he confesses, etc.' because his confession establishes that he is the depositary of the judge, and the hand of the depositary is like his hand, so it is as if it is in his hand at present; for if it were in his hand physically, his confession regarding it would be valid, so likewise if it is in the hand of his depositary, his confession is accepted, unless the holder first confesses to someone else then confesses that the judge delivered it to him, and the judge confesses to someone else, then it is given to the first confesse, and the confessor guarantees its value to the judge by his second confession. The issue has four aspects: either he confesses that he delivered it to him after having confessed to someone else, or he denies delivery—their ruling is as we mentioned—or he confesses that the dismissed judge delivered it to him then confesses to someone else—then his confession to the second is not accepted, because when he confessed that the judge delivered it to him, it became as if in the judge's hand—and the fourth that he confesses that the judge delivered it to him then says, 'I do not know who it belongs to'—its ruling is apparent. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:118) (Fath) 'In them' refers to deposits and endowment proceeds.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage explains how a new judge should handle deposits and endowment proceeds left by the previous dismissed judge. The matn states that the new judge acts based on evidence or confession, not on the word of the dismissed judge, unless the person in possession (dhul yad) confesses that the dismissed judge had delivered the property to him. The commentary elaborates: the dismissed judge, after dismissal, is like an ordinary person, and his statement is not evidence. However, if the holder confesses that the judge delivered it to him, that confession is accepted because the holder is like an agent of the judge. The passage then discusses four scenarios when the holder makes conflicting confessions. The overarching principle is that the new judge must rely on clear proof or confession from the holder, not on the dismissed judge's assertion.

Key Terms
الودائع (al-wada'i'): Deposits (goods left in trust) that the dismissed judge had placed with trustees.
غلات الوقف (ghallat al-waqf): Proceeds or revenues generated from endowment properties.
المعزول (al-ma'zul): The dismissed or removed judge; after dismissal, he is like a common individual and his word is not evidence.
بينة (bayyinah): Clear evidence, usually testimony of two witnesses.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or admission of a right against oneself.
ذو اليد (dhul yad): The person in whose possession the property currently lies; the holder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On what basis does a new judge act regarding deposits left by the dismissed judge?
A) The statement of the dismissed judge
B) Evidence or confession of the holder
C) The opinion of the community elders
D) The records of the previous judge

Key Takeaway
A new judge must rely on clear proof or the holder's confession, never on the unverified word of a dismissed judge.

Judging in the Mosque or the Judge's House
Dispute over mosque as court location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on holding court in a mosque and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A judge may hold court in a mosque; the Shafi'i objection based on impurity is answered by the judge stepping out.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses where a judge (qadi) may lawfully hold court. The Hanafi school permits the mosque and the judge's house, with the central mosque being preferred. A disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i arises over concerns about impure persons entering the mosque. This lesson unpacks the proofs and the Hanafi rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
Read the matn: 'يقضي في المسجد أو داره'. This establishes that a judge may hold court in a mosque or his own house. The commentator adds that the central mosque (المسجد الجامع) is preferable if it is centrally located.
The ruling is permissive; the preference (أولى) indicates recommendation, not obligation.
2
State the Hanafi proof
The text cites the Prophet's practice: he judged between two people who were cursing each other (متلاعنين) in the mosque. The Rightly Guided Caliphs also sat for judgment in mosques. This provides a strong Sunnah-based evidence.
The Hanafi school considers the Prophet's actions in his capacity as judge as general guidance, not restricted to a specific case.
3
Present the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that holding court in a mosque is makruh because polytheists (impure per Quran 9:28) and menstruating women (forbidden from entering mosques) may attend, causing impurity or prohibition.
The Shafi'i concern is about maintaining the sanctity of the mosque.
4
Explain the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply: the judge can step out of the mosque to handle such persons, just as he might step out to examine a case involving an animal. The potential presence of impure persons does not prevent the judge from sitting in the mosque itself.
This response shows a practical solution that preserves both the permissibility of the court and the sanctity of the mosque.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, Hanafi judges may freely hold court in mosques, especially the central one. Shafi'i judges avoid doing so unless unavoidable. This difference affects where judicial proceedings are conducted in regions following each school.
The thamarah al-khilaf is a real-world divergence in judicial practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقضي في المسجد أو داره. ويقضي وكذا السلطان يجلس للحكم في المسجد. (عيني) أي يجلس في المسجد للقضاء؛ لأنه عليه حكم بين متلاعنين في المسجد، والخلفاء الراشدون كانوا يجلسون للحكم في المساجد والمسجد الجامع أولى إن كان في وسط البلدة، وإذا كان في طرف منها، يختار مسجداً في وسطها وقال الشافعي يكره جلوسه في المسجد؛ لأنه يحضره مشرك، وهو نجس؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الْمُشْرِكُونَ نَجَسٌ (التوبة:۲۸) ويحضره الحائض، وهي ممنوعة عن الدخول في المسجد، قلنا: يمكن خروج القاضي للحائض وغيرها كما لو كانت الدعوى في دابة، وقال مالك: إنما يكره إذا تعمد الجلوس فيه لفصل الخصومات وأما لو كان في المسجد، فقدم إليه الخصمان، لا بأس بفصل الخصومة فيه، وقوله: "أو داره" أي أو يقضي في داره؛ لأن الحكم عبادة، فلا يختص بمكان دون مكان، ولا يمنع أحد من الدخول فيه.
He judges in the mosque or his house. And likewise the Sultan sits for judgment in the mosque. (Ayni) That is, he sits in the mosque for judgment, because he (the Prophet) judged between two cursers (mutala'inayn) in the mosque, and the Rightly Guided Caliphs used to sit for judgment in mosques, and the central mosque (al-masjid al-jami') is more deserving if it is in the center of the town; if it is at the edge, he chooses a mosque in its center. Al-Shafi'i said: It is disliked for him to sit in the mosque, because a polytheist may attend, and he is impure due to His saying: 'Indeed the polytheists are impure' (9:28), and a menstruating woman may attend, and she is forbidden from entering the mosque. We say: The judge can go out to the menstruating woman and others, similar to if the case concerns an animal. And Malik said: It is only disliked if he intentionally sits for deciding disputes; but if he is in the mosque and the litigants come to him, there is no harm in deciding the dispute in it. His statement: 'or his house' means he judges in his house, because judgment is an act of worship, so it is not restricted to a particular place, and he does not prevent anyone from entering it.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage discusses the proper place for a judge to hold court. The matn states that the judge may judge in the mosque or his own house. The commentary supports this by citing the Prophet's practice of judging in the mosque and the practice of the caliphs. The preferred mosque is the central one (jami') if it is in the middle of the town. A disagreement with al-Shafi'i is then presented: al-Shafi'i considers it disliked (makruh) because impure persons like polytheists or menstruating women might enter. The Hanafi response is that the judge can come out to such persons if needed, similar to handling a case involving an animal. Imam Malik's view is also cited: it is only disliked if the judge intentionally sits for that purpose, but incidental judgment in the mosque is fine. The commentator concludes that the judge may also judge in his house, as worship is not place-restricted.

Key Terms
المسجد الجامع (al-masjid al-jami'): The central or Friday mosque where congregational prayers are held.
الشافعي (al-Shafi'i): Founder of the Shafi'i school, opposing the Hanafi view on this issue.
داره (darihi): The judge's own house, an alternative location for hearing cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only in his own house
B) In the central mosque if it is in the middle of the town
C) In a place outside the city
D) Only in a special courthouse

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits holding court in a mosque, with the central mosque preferred, overcoming impurity concerns by having the judge step out.
