The Judge and Gifts: Ruling and Exceptions
Ruling on the judge's acceptance of gifts, with exceptions for relatives and those with prior habit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a judge accepting gifts and identify the exceptions and their conditions.

Core Idea
A judge rejects all gifts unless from a mahram relative or a pre-existing habit, because gifts to a judge are like bribery.

This lesson examines the strict Hanafi rule that a judge must reject gifts, a principle rooted in the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya. The passage also details the exceptions for relatives and those with a prior habit of gifting, and explains the treatment of gifts that cannot be returned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The matn states: 'He rejects a gift except from his relative or from one whose habit it was.' The general rule is that a judge must reject all gifts, as they resemble bribery.
This is based on the principle of avoiding suspicion (tuhma).
2
List the exceptions
Two exceptions: (1) a mahram relative, to avoid severing family ties, and (2) someone who habitually gave gifts before the judge's appointment, as long as the gift does not exceed the previous custom.
If the giver is a litigant, the gift is absolutely prohibited even if a relative or habitual giver.
3
Understand the proof
The hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya: the Prophet (ﷺ) rebuked a collector who kept gifts, saying, 'Why did he not sit in his father's house and see whether he would be given gifts or not?' This shows that gifts to officials are illicit.
The hadith is from al-Bukhari and is classified as ahad with zanni thubut.
4
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Categorize the giver: if a litigant, reject unconditionally; if a non-litigant, check if mahram relative or habitual giver without increase; otherwise reject.
Borrowing and loans are treated like gifts.
5
Determine disposition when return is impossible
If the gift cannot be returned (giver unknown or far), place it in the public treasury (bayt al-mal). If the giver would be hurt by return, give equivalent value. The gift is treated like luqata (lost property), as the judge is a deputy of the Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد هدية إلا من قريبه أو ممن جرت عادته بذلك، ودعوة خاصة، ويشهد بالمهاداة أي القاضي أحداً من الدخول فيه، ولا يحكم وهو ماش أو راكب، وإن اعتراه هم أو غضب أو جوع أو حاجة كف عن الحكم، ولا يقضي وهو يدافع أحد الأخبثين، وكذا في حال شغل قلبه بفرح أو برد أو حر شديد، ولا يضرب في المسجد حداً ولا تعزيراً، ولا يجلس وحده؛ لأنه يورث التهمة. (عيني، فتح) ويرد هدية لأنها تشبه الرشوة فيجتنب عنها، وعلى هذا كانت الصحابة (عيني)] فلو تعذر ردها لبعد معرفته أو لبعد مكانه وضعها في بيت المال، فإن جاء صاحبها، ردت إليه، ولو تأذى بردها أعطاه مثل قيمتها، والاستقراض والاستعارة كالهدية، والأصل في ذلك: ما رواه البخاري أنه عليه استعمل رجلاً من الأزد، يقال له: ابن اللتبية على الصدقة فلما قدم قال هذا لكم، وهذا أهدي لي ، فقال له: "فهلا" جلس في بيت أبيه، أو بيت أمه فلينظر أيهدى له أو لا، وجعله في بيت المال، وكذلك فعل عمر له مع أبي هريرة الله لما قدم بمال، فعرفنا أن قبول الهدية من الرشوة إذا كان بهذه الصفة. (فتح (عناية إلا أي إلا أن له أن لا يرد الهدية. (عيني) : من قريبه وهو ذو الرحم المحرم؛ لأن في ردها منهم قطيعة صلة الرحم وهي حرام. (عيني) أو ممن جرت إلخ: أي بالمهاداة قبل القضاء حتى لو كانت لهما خصومة، أو زاد على العادة يردها حينئذ؛ لأنه حينئذ لأجل القضاء، فيكون من الغلول، والحاصل أن المهدي للقاضي إما أن يكون ذا خصومة أو لا، والأول لا يجوز قبول هديته مطلقاً، سواء كان قريباً أو غيره، وسواء كان مهادياً قبل القضاء أو لم يكن، والثاني: إما أن يكون قريباً أو أو ممن جرت له العادة بالمهاداة من قبل أو لم يكن والثاني كذلك؛ لأنه أكل على القضاء، فيتحاماه، والأول يجوز إن لم يزد من له العادة على المعتاد، ثم إذا أخذ القاضي ما ليس له أخذه وتعذر الرد، يضعه في بيت المال، وحكمه حكم اللقطة؛ لأنه إنما أهدي إليه لعمله، وهو في هذا العمل نائب عن المسلمين، فكانت الهدايا من حيث المعنى لهم. (عيني، عناية)
He rejects a gift except from his relative or from one whose habit it was, and (he rejects) a private invitation, and he witnesses (attends)… He does not judge while walking or riding, and if he is overcome by worry, anger, hunger, or need, he refrains from judgment. He does not judge while suppressing one of the two excrements, nor in a state of heart occupied by joy, extreme cold or heat. He does not inflict a hadd or ta'zir punishment in the mosque, and he does not sit alone because that gives rise to suspicion. (Ayni, Fath) He rejects a gift because it resembles bribery, so he avoids it, and this was the practice of the Companions. (Ayni) If it is impossible to return it due to not knowing the giver or distance, he places it in the public treasury; if the owner comes, it is returned to him; if the giver would be hurt by its return, he gives him its equivalent value. Borrowing and requesting a loan are like gifts. The basis for this is what al-Bukhari narrated: that the Prophet employed a man from Azd called Ibn al-Lutbiyya to collect charity; when he came, he said, 'This is for you, and this was gifted to me.' The Prophet said, 'Why did he not sit in his father's or mother's house and see whether he would be given gifts or not?' and he placed it in the public treasury. Likewise, Umar did with Abu Hurayra when he came with wealth. So we know that accepting a gift is like bribery when it is of this description. (Fath, Inayah) 'Except' means that he may not reject the gift. (Ayni) 'From his relative' means a mahram relative, because rejecting from them would be severing ties of kinship, which is haram. (Ayni) 'Or from one whose habit it was' means by gifting before judgeship; so if they have a dispute, or if the gift exceeds the usual, he rejects it then, because then it is for the sake of judgment, and thus is from ghulul (unlawful gain). The summary: the giver to the judge is either a litigant or not. The first is absolutely impermissible to accept, whether relative or not, whether used to gift before or not. The second: either a relative or one whose habit it was from before; if the latter, it is permissible as long as it does not exceed the usual. Then if the judge takes what is not his and return is impossible, he puts it in the public treasury; its ruling is like a lost item (luqata), because it was gifted to him for his work, and he is in this work a deputy of the Muslims, so the gifts are in essence for them. (Ayni, Inayah)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage deals with gifts offered to a judge. The matn states that the judge rejects gifts except from a relative (mahram) or from someone who habitually gave gifts before the judge's appointment. The commentary explains that gift acceptance is akin to bribery and is prohibited, based on the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya. If return is impossible, the gift is placed in the public treasury. Borrowing is treated similarly. If the judge accepts a gift from a litigant, it is absolutely forbidden. From a non-litigant relative, it is permissible; from a habitual giver, permissible if not exceeding previous custom. If the gift cannot be returned, it is treated like a lost item (luqata), because the judge is a deputy for the Muslims.

Key Terms
الهدية (al-hadiyah): A gift offered to the judge, which is generally prohibited except from certain persons.
القريب (al-qarib): A close relative who is mahram, from whom accepting a gift is permissible to avoid severing family ties.
المهاداة (al-muhadah): The habit of exchanging gifts prior to a person becoming judge; if continued after appointment, it remains permissible as long as it does not increase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Accept it if it is not more than the customary amount

Key Takeaway
A judge must reject gifts from litigants entirely; only close relatives and pre-existing habits allow acceptance, and even then subject to conditions.

Defining Private Invitations for the Judge: The Ruling on Attending
Ruling on the judge attending invitations that are exclusively made for him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a private invitation (da'wa khassa) according to the Hanafi school, apply the correct criterion (host's motive) to classify a given invitation, and state the ruling for a judge.

Core Idea
A judge must reject any invitation made solely because of his position.

This lesson addresses a critical issue of judicial conduct: when may a judge accept an invitation? The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines 'private invitation' (da'wa khassa) — an invitation the host would not have made but for the judge's office. Understanding the distinction between private and public invitations preserves the judge's impartiality and avoids even the appearance of impropriety.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The matn says the judge rejects a private invitation. What is the ruling? It is prohibited (haram) for the judge to attend.
2
Identify the various definitions
The commentary lists several opinions: by occasion (non-wedding/circumcision), by number (5-10 people private, more public), and by intention.
These definitions represent an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Apply the correct definition
The correct view (al-sahih) is based on the host's motive: would the host have made the invitation if the judge could not attend?
4
Distinguish public vs private
If the host would still invite without the judge's presence, it is public (da'wa 'amma) and permissible; if not, it is private (da'wa khassa) and forbidden.
5
Consider the consequence
This ruling ensures the judge's integrity and prevents bias or the perception of bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودعوة خاصة: أي يرد الدعوة الخاصة يعني لا يحضرها؛ لأنها جعلت لأجله، واختلف في تعريفها فقيل: التي لا يتخذها صاحبها إلا لأجله، وقيل: كل دعوة اتخذت في غير العرس والختان فهي خاصة، و لم يفصل في الخاصة بين أن تكون من القريب أو من غيره، وقيل: إن كان خمسة نفر إلى عشرة فهي خاصة، وإن جاوز منها فعامة، والصحيح: أن صاحب الدعوة إن كان بحال لو علم أن القاضي لا يحضره لا يمتنع من اتخاذ الدعوة، فهذه عامة لا بأس بقبولها، وإن كان بحال لو علم أنه لو اتخذ الدعوة لا يحضرها القاضي يمتنع ولا يتخذ الدعوة، فهذه دعوة خاصة لا يجيبها القاضي. [رمز الحقائق: ١١٩/٢]
And a private invitation: he rejects the private invitation, meaning he does not attend it, because it is made for his sake. There is disagreement on its definition: it is said that it is the one that the host only makes for his sake; it is said that every invitation made for other than a wedding or circumcision is private; and it is not specified in the private whether it is from a relative or others. It is said that if it is between five to ten people, it is private; if more, it is public. The correct view is: if the host would not refrain from making the invitation even if he knew the judge would not attend, then it is public and there is no harm in accepting it; but if he would refrain from making the invitation if he knew the judge would not attend, then it is a private invitation that the judge does not accept.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage addresses the judge's attendance at invitations. The matn says he rejects a 'private invitation' (da'wa khassa). The commentary defines it: an invitation made specifically because of the judge's position. It mentions various definitions: some say it is any invitation outside of weddings and circumcisions; others say by number (5-10 people is private, more is public). The correct definition, according to the text, is based on the host's intention: if the host would still make the invitation even if the judge were not to come, it is public and permissible; if he would not make it without the judge's attendance, it is private and the judge must not attend. The ruling is that private invitations are akin to bribery, while public ones are not.

Key Terms
دعوة خاصة (da'wa khassa): An invitation made exclusively for the judge due to his position, which he must reject.
دعوة عامة (da'wa 'amma): A public invitation that is not dependent on the judge's presence, permissible to accept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No, because it is a private invitation according to the correct view
D) No, because all invitations are forbidden

Key Takeaway
The judge's attendance at an invitation is forbidden if the host's motive is the judge's position; the correct criterion is the host's hypothetical willingness to host without the judge.

The Judge's Duty to Fulfill Muslim's Rights
Ruling that the judge must fulfill six obligatory rights towards other Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six obligatory rights of a Muslim upon another and explain why a judge is not exempt from these obligations.

Core Idea
Six rights of a Muslim are wajib—even a judge must fulfill them.

Many assume that a judge, due to his authority, may be excused from common social duties. This lesson clarifies that the six rights of a Muslim—such as visiting the sick and attending funerals—are obligatory (wajib) on every believer, including a judge. The proof comes directly from a hadith narrated by Abu Ayyub al-Ansari, and the commentators emphasize that judicial status does not nullify these personal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the text and determine what act is being ruled upon. The act is fulfilling the six rights of a Muslim. The ruling is that these are obligatory (wajib).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) narrated by Abu Ayyub. Identify the source and the key phrase 'ستة خصال واجبة' (six obligatory traits).
This is a Sunnah ahad (single-narrator hadith) with zanni thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The explicit word 'واجبة' (obligatory) and the statement that leaving any of them is leaving an obligation establish the wujub.
In Hanafi usul, the explicit declaration of wujub in a hadith indicates a wajib ruling, not fard, because the thubut is zanni.
4
Apply the ruling to the judge
The text adds that the right of a Muslim does not fall due to being a judge (حق المسلم لا يسقط بالقضاء). This means the judge is personally obligated just like any other Muslim.
The judge's official duties do not override these personal obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشهد إلخ: لقوله عليه : "للمسلم على المسلم ستة حقوق"، وفي رواية أبي أيوب: "ستة خصال واجبة، إن ترك خصلة أو شيئاً، فقد ترك واجباً عليه إذا دعاه أن يجيبه، وإذا مرض أن يعوده ، وإذا مات أن يحضره، وإذا لقيه أن يسلم عليه، وإذا استنصحه أن ينصحه، وإذا عطس أن يشمته". وحق المسلم لا يسقط بالقضاء.(فتح، عناية)
He witnesses (attends) etc.: because of his saying: 'For a Muslim there are six rights upon another Muslim.' And in the narration of Abu Ayyub: 'Six obligatory traits: if he leaves any trait or anything, he has left an obligation upon him: when he invites him, he should respond; when he is ill, he should visit him; when he dies, he should attend his funeral; when he meets him, he should greet him; when he seeks his advice, he should advise him; when he sneezes, he should say 'Yarhamuk Allah'.' And the right of a Muslim does not fall due to being a judge. (Fath, Inayah)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This short passage covers a general principle: the judge, like any Muslim, must fulfill the six established rights of Muslims upon one another. These include responding to an invitation, visiting the sick, attending funerals, greeting, giving sincere advice, and saying 'Yarhamuk Allah' after a sneeze. These obligations are not waived by his judicial role. The commentary emphasizes that being a judge does not exempt one from these duties, as they are personal obligations (wajibat) towards fellow Muslims.

Key Terms
حق المسلم (haqq al-muslim): The right of a Muslim upon another, including the six specific obligations mentioned.
يشهد (yashhad): Here means 'attends' or 'witnesses' in the sense of attending a funeral or similar gathering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is sinful as he has left an obligation
C) It is recommended but not required
D) It is only required if he knows the deceased

Key Takeaway
A judge, like every Muslim, is obligated to fulfill the six rights of a Muslim; judicial office does not remove this duty.

Rulings on Gifts and Special Invitations for a Judge
Ruling with proof on gifts and special invitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a judge may accept a gift and when he must reject a special invitation, applying the Hanafi exceptions and conditions.

Core Idea
A judge may accept a gift only from a mahram relative or pre-appointment customary giver; special invitations made solely for his position are prohibited.

This lesson covers the Hanafi rules on gifts and invitations directed at a judge. The text distinguishes between permissible gifts (from relatives or pre-existing custom) and prohibited ones (given due to the judgeship). Special invitations are also regulated based on the host's intent. Understanding these boundaries preserves judicial integrity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling on gifts
The judge must reject gifts because they resemble bribery. This is wajib unless an exception applies.
2
Apply exceptions for relatives and customary givers
Gifts from mahram relatives are allowed to avoid severing family ties; gifts from those who customarily gave before appointment are allowed if they do not exceed the usual amount.
If the gift is from a litigant or exceeds custom, it is prohibited.
3
Determine the ruling on special invitations
A special invitation (da'wah khassah) made solely for the judge's sake must be rejected. The criterion is the host's intent: if he would have made the invitation even if the judge would not attend, it is general.
General invitations (e.g., weddings) are permissible.
4
Trace the dalil for the gift ruling
The hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya (Bukhari) shows the Prophet's condemnation of accepting gifts due to office, establishing the obligation to reject.
The hadith also shows placement in bayt al-mal if return is difficult.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء
أحكام القضاء
ويرد هدية إلا من قريبه أو ممن جرت عادته بذلك، ودعوة خاصة، ويشهد بالجنازة ويعود المريض، ويسوي بينهما جلوساً وإقبالاً، ويتقي عن مسارة أحدهما وإشارته وتلقين حجته وضيافته والمزاح وتلقين الشاهد
أي القاضي أحداً من الدخول فيه، ولا يحكم وهو ماش أو راكب، وإن اعتراه هم أو غضب أو جوع أو حاجة كف عن الحكم، ولا يقضي وهو يدافع أحد الأخبثين، وكذا في حال شغل قلبه بفرح أو برد أو حر شديد، ولا يضرب في المسجد حداً ولا تعزيراً، ولا يجلس وحده؛ لأنه يورث التهمة. (عيني، فتح) ويرد هدية لأنها تشبه الرشوة فيجتنب عنها، وعلى هذا كانت الصحابة (عيني)] فلو تعذر ردها لبعد معرفته أو لبعد مكانه وضعها في بيت المال، فإن جاء صاحبها، ردت إليه، ولو تأذى بردها أعطاه مثل قيمتها، والاستقراض والاستعارة كالهدية، والأصل في ذلك: ما رواه البخاري أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام استعمل رجلاً من الأزد، يقال له: ابن اللتبية على الصدقة فلما قدم قال هذا لكم، وهذا أهدي لي، فقال له: "فهلا جلس في بيت أبيه، أو بيت أمه فلينظر أيهدى له أو لا، وجعله في بيت المال، وكذلك فعل عمر رضي الله عنه مع أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه لما قدم بمال، فعرفنا أن قبول الهدية من الرشوة إذا كان بهذه الصفة. (فتح (عناية إلا أي إلا أن له أن لا يرد الهدية. (عيني)
:
من قريبه وهو ذو الرحم المحرم؛ لأن في ردها منهم قطيعة صلة الرحم وهي حرام. (عيني) أو ممن جرت إلخ: أي بالمهاداة قبل القضاء حتى لو كانت لهما خصومة، أو زاد على العادة يردها حينئذ؛ لأنه حينئذ لأجل القضاء، فيكون من الغلول، والحاصل أن المهدي للقاضي إما أن يكون ذا خصومة أو لا، والأول لا يجوز قبول هديته مطلقاً، سواء كان قريباً أو غيره، وسواء كان مهادياً قبل القضاء أو لم يكن، والثاني: إما أن يكون قريباً أو أو ممن جرت له العادة بالمهاداة من قبل أو لم يكن والثاني كذلك؛ لأنه أكل على القضاء، فيتحاماه، والأول يجوز إن لم يزد من له العادة على المعتاد، ثم إذا أخذ القاضي ما ليس له أخذه وتعذر الرد، يضعه في بيت المال، وحكمه حكم اللقطة؛ لأنه إنما أهدي إليه لعمله، وهو في هذا العمل نائب عن المسلمين، فكانت الهدايا من حيث المعنى لهم. (عيني، عناية) ودعوة خاصة: أي يرد الدعوة الخاصة يعني لا يحضرها؛ لأنها جعلت لأجله، واختلف في تعريفها فقيل: التي لا يتخذها صاحبها إلا لأجله، وقيل: كل دعوة اتخذت في غير العرس والختان فهي خاصة، و لم يفصل في الخاصة بين أن تكون من القريب أو من غيره، وقيل: إن كان خمسة نفر إلى عشرة فهي خاصة، وإن جاوز منها فعامة، والصحيح: أن صاحب الدعوة إن كان بحال لو علم أن القاضي لا يحضره لا يمتنع من اتخاذ الدعوة، فهذه عامة لا بأس بقبولها، وإن كان بحال لو علم أنه لو اتخذ الدعوة لا يحضرها القاضي يمتنع ولا يتخذ الدعوة، فهذه دعوة خاصة لا يجيبها القاضي. [رمز الحقائق: ١١٩/٢] ويشهد إلخ: لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : "للمسلم على المسلم ستة حقوق"، وفي رواية أبي أيوب: "ستة خصال واجبة، إن ترك خصلة أو شيئاً، فقد ترك واجباً عليه إذا دعاه أن يجيبه، وإذا مرض أن يعوده، وإذا مات أن يحضره، وإذا لقيه أن يسلم عليه، وإذا استنصحه أن ينصحه، وإذا عطس أن يشمته". وحق المسلم لا يسقط بالقضاء.(فتح، عناية)

يدخل فيه أي القاضي من يضيف خصمه، ويجتنب أيضاً مع الخصمين أو مع أحدهما أو مع غيرهما. (عيني) 
والمزاح: أي ويجتنب عن المزاح مطلقاً .
وتلقين الشاهد: الشهادة مطلقا، ومعناه أن يقول : أتشهد بكذا وكذا؛ لأن فيه إعانة لأحد الخصمين، واستحسن التلقين أبو يوسف في موضع ليس فيه ظن الإعانة كما إذا ترك لفظ الشهادة، وقال: "أعلم" مكان "أشهد"؛ لمهابة المجلس، أو ترك الإشارة، أو حصر في الكلام، أو لم يستفد زيادة علم بتلقينه، وينبغي أن يفتي بقول أبي يوسف؛ لأنه أكثر مهارة في مسائل القضاء، ولأن في تلقينه في غير موضع التهمة إحياء الحق، ولا تهمة في مثله، -
The Book of Judgement. Rulings of Judgement. He rejects a gift except from his relative or from one whose custom it was [to give gifts before his appointment], and [rejects] a special invitation. He attends the funeral and visits the sick. He makes equality between the two [litigants] in seating and attention. He avoids whispering to one of them, hinting to him, coaching his argument, his hospitality, joking, and coaching the witness. [Meaning: the judge] does not allow anyone to enter into it [the case] while he is walking or riding. If he is overtaken by worry, anger, hunger, or need, he refrains from judging. He does not judge while he is repelling one of the two evil things [urine/feces], and likewise in a state when his heart is occupied with joy, extreme cold, or extreme heat. He does not execute a hadd punishment or ta'zir in the mosque. He does not sit alone because it leads to suspicion. (Ayni, Fath) He rejects a gift because it resembles bribery, so he avoids it, and on this were the Companions (Ayni). If returning it is difficult due to not knowing [the giver] or distance, he places it in the public treasury. If its owner comes, it is returned to him. If he is harmed by returning it, he gives him its value. Borrowing and requesting a loan are like a gift. The basis for this is what al-Bukhari narrated: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) appointed a man from Azd, called Ibn al-Lutbiyya, over the sadaqah. When he came, he said, 'This is for you, and this was given to me as a gift.' He said to him, 'Why did he not sit in his father's house or his mother's house and see if it would be given to him or not?' And he placed it in the public treasury. Similarly, Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did with Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him) when he brought wealth. Thus we know that accepting a gift is bribery when it is of this description. (Fath, Inaya) 'Except' means that he has the option not to return the gift (Ayni). '[From] his relative' meaning a mahram relative, because returning it from them would be severing ties of kinship, which is forbidden. (Ayni) 'Or from one whose custom it was' etc.: meaning that the gift was before [his appointment to] judgment, even if they have a dispute, or it exceeds the custom, he rejects it then because it is for the sake of judgment, thus it is from ghulul (treachery). The summary: The giver to the judge is either a litigant or not. In the first case, it is absolutely impermissible to accept his gift, whether he is a relative or not, and whether he gave before judgment or not. In the second case: either he is a relative or one who customarily gave before, or not; and the second is likewise because it is eating on account of judgment, so he avoids it. The first [relative or customary giver] is permissible if the one with the custom does not exceed the usual. Then if the judge takes what he should not take and returning it is difficult, he places it in the public treasury; its ruling is that of a lost item (luqata), because it was given to him for his work, and in this work he is a representative of the Muslims, so the gifts are in meaning for them. (Ayni, Inaya) 'And a special invitation': meaning he rejects the special invitation, i.e., he does not attend it because it is made for his sake. There is a difference of opinion on its definition. It is said: it is that which the host does not make except for him. It is said: every invitation made other than for a wedding or circumcision is special. It is not detailed in the special whether it is from a relative or other. It is said: if it is five to ten people, it is special; if more, it is general. The correct view: the host, if he is such that if he knew the judge would not attend, he would not refrain from making the invitation, then this is general, and accepting it is fine. If he is such that if he knew that if he made the invitation the judge would not attend, he would refrain and not make it, then this is a special invitation which the judge does not accept. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 2/119) 'And he attends' etc.: due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'A Muslim has six rights over another Muslim.' And in the narration of Abu Ayyub: 'Six obligatory characteristics: if he leaves a characteristic or something, he has left an obligation upon him: when he invites him, he responds; when he is sick, he visits him; when he dies, he attends his funeral; when he meets him, he greets him; when he seeks his advice, he advises him; when he sneezes, he says yarhamukallah.' The right of a Muslim does not fall due to being a judge. (Fath, Inaya)
The judge refrains from hosting his opponent, and he also refrains [from hosting] with both opponents or with one of them or with others. (Ayni) 'And joking': meaning he absolutely avoids joking. 'And coaching the witness': coaching the witness absolutely, meaning he says, 'Do you testify to such and such?' because it is helping one of the litigants. Abu Yusuf considered coaching commendable in a place where there is no suspicion of help, such as when he [the witness] omitted the wording of testimony and said 'I know' instead of 'I testify' due to awe of the court, or omitted a gesture, or was stuck in speech, or he does not gain additional knowledge by his coaching. And it is proper to give fatwa according to the saying of Abu Yusuf because he is more skilled in matters of judgment, and because coaching in a place not subject to suspicion is reviving a right, and there is no suspicion in such a case. -
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq's commentary presents a series of rulings on the conduct of a judge (qadi). It begins with the general principle that a judge must reject gifts, as they resemble bribery. The exception is for gifts from a mahram relative (to avoid severing family ties) or from someone who customarily gave gifts before the judge's appointment. If the gift cannot be returned, it is placed in the public treasury (bayt al-mal) and treated like a lost item. Similarly, borrowing is equated with gifting. The proof is the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya, where the Prophet criticized him for accepting a gift because of his position. The passage then covers special invitations: a judge should not accept an invitation made solely because of his position; the criterion is whether the host would have made the invitation even if the judge would not attend. Next, the judge must attend funerals and visit the sick, as these are rights of Muslims that do not cease due to his office. The judge must treat litigants equally in seating and attention, based on the Prophet's command. He must avoid private conversations, hints, coaching arguments, hosting one litigant, joking, and coaching witnesses. Coaching a witness (talaqin al-shahid) is generally prohibited as it assists one party, but Abu Yusuf made an exception when there is no suspicion of bias, e.g., if the witness is simply nervous and uses an incorrect phrase. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is Abu Yusuf's, as it revives rights without suspicion. The passage emphasizes maintaining the dignity and impartiality of the court.

Key Terms
الهدية (al-hadiyyah): A gift given to the judge; it is prohibited if given because of his position.
دعوة خاصة (da'wah khassah): A special invitation made solely for the judge's sake; he must not attend.
محرم (mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently forbidden, e.g., mother, sister, daughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when may a judge accept a gift from someone who is not a relative and not a litigant?
A) Never, because all gifts to judges are bribery.
B) Only if the gift was customary before the judge's appointment and does not exceed the usual amount.
C) Only if the gift is given during a court session.
D) Only if the judge is poor.

Key Takeaway
A judge must reject gifts given due to his office, but may accept from mahram relatives or pre-appointment customary givers, ensuring no increase over custom.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Equality Between Litigants and the Rule on Coaching Witnesses
Ruling with proof on equality and witness coaching, including intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the requirement of equality between litigants and evaluate the Hanafi ikhtilaf on coaching witnesses, identifying when it is permissible.

Core Idea
A judge must treat litigants equally in every way; coaching a witness is generally prohibited, but Abu Yusuf allowed it when no suspicion of bias.

Judicial impartiality requires absolute equality between litigants in seating, attention, and conduct. The text also addresses coaching witnesses (talaqin al-shahid), which is normally helping one side. However, Abu Yusuf made an exception when the coaching merely helps the witness articulate without adding new information, reviving a right without suspicion. This lesson examines both rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement of equality
The judge must make the litigants equal in seating, attention (facing both equally), and speech. This is a duty to avoid bias.
Based on the Prophet's practice and the principle of fairness.
2
List prohibited actions towards litigants
The judge must avoid: whispering to one, hinting, coaching one's argument, hosting one, joking, and coaching witnesses.
These actions create suspicion of partiality.
3
Define coaching the witness (talaqin al-shahid)
It means prompting the witness, e.g., saying 'Do you testify to such and such?' which assists one litigant.
The general ruling is prohibition.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's exception
Abu Yusuf allowed coaching when there is no suspicion of bias, e.g., the witness is flustered and uses 'I know' instead of 'I testify'. Coaching here revives a right without partiality.
The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view, as he was more skilled in qada matters.

كتاب القضاء
أحكام القضاء
ويرد هدية إلا من قريبه أو ممن جرت عادته بذلك، ودعوة خاصة، ويشهد بالجنازة ويعود المريض، ويسوي بينهما جلوساً وإقبالاً، ويتقي عن مسارة أحدهما وإشارته وتلقين حجته وضيافته والمزاح وتلقين الشاهد
أي القاضي أحداً من الدخول فيه، ولا يحكم وهو ماش أو راكب، وإن اعتراه هم أو غضب أو جوع أو حاجة كف عن الحكم، ولا يقضي وهو يدافع أحد الأخبثين، وكذا في حال شغل قلبه بفرح أو برد أو حر شديد، ولا يضرب في المسجد حداً ولا تعزيراً، ولا يجلس وحده؛ لأنه يورث التهمة. (عيني، فتح) ويرد هدية لأنها تشبه الرشوة فيجتنب عنها، وعلى هذا كانت الصحابة (عيني)] فلو تعذر ردها لبعد معرفته أو لبعد مكانه وضعها في بيت المال، فإن جاء صاحبها، ردت إليه، ولو تأذى بردها أعطاه مثل قيمتها، والاستقراض والاستعارة كالهدية، والأصل في ذلك: ما رواه البخاري أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام استعمل رجلاً من الأزد، يقال له: ابن اللتبية على الصدقة فلما قدم قال هذا لكم، وهذا أهدي لي، فقال له: "فهلا جلس في بيت أبيه، أو بيت أمه فلينظر أيهدى له أو لا، وجعله في بيت المال، وكذلك فعل عمر رضي الله عنه مع أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه لما قدم بمال، فعرفنا أن قبول الهدية من الرشوة إذا كان بهذه الصفة. (فتح (عناية إلا أي إلا أن له أن لا يرد الهدية. (عيني)
:
من قريبه وهو ذو الرحم المحرم؛ لأن في ردها منهم قطيعة صلة الرحم وهي حرام. (عيني) أو ممن جرت إلخ: أي بالمهاداة قبل القضاء حتى لو كانت لهما خصومة، أو زاد على العادة يردها حينئذ؛ لأنه حينئذ لأجل القضاء، فيكون من الغلول، والحاصل أن المهدي للقاضي إما أن يكون ذا خصومة أو لا، والأول لا يجوز قبول هديته مطلقاً، سواء كان قريباً أو غيره، وسواء كان مهادياً قبل القضاء أو لم يكن، والثاني: إما أن يكون قريباً أو أو ممن جرت له العادة بالمهاداة من قبل أو لم يكن والثاني كذلك؛ لأنه أكل على القضاء، فيتحاماه، والأول يجوز إن لم يزد من له العادة على المعتاد، ثم إذا أخذ القاضي ما ليس له أخذه وتعذر الرد، يضعه في بيت المال، وحكمه حكم اللقطة؛ لأنه إنما أهدي إليه لعمله، وهو في هذا العمل نائب عن المسلمين، فكانت الهدايا من حيث المعنى لهم. (عيني، عناية) ودعوة خاصة: أي يرد الدعوة الخاصة يعني لا يحضرها؛ لأنها جعلت لأجله، واختلف في تعريفها فقيل: التي لا يتخذها صاحبها إلا لأجله، وقيل: كل دعوة اتخذت في غير العرس والختان فهي خاصة، و لم يفصل في الخاصة بين أن تكون من القريب أو من غيره، وقيل: إن كان خمسة نفر إلى عشرة فهي خاصة، وإن جاوز منها فعامة، والصحيح: أن صاحب الدعوة إن كان بحال لو علم أن القاضي لا يحضره لا يمتنع من اتخاذ الدعوة، فهذه عامة لا بأس بقبولها، وإن كان بحال لو علم أنه لو اتخذ الدعوة لا يحضرها القاضي يمتنع ولا يتخذ الدعوة، فهذه دعوة خاصة لا يجيبها القاضي. [رمز الحقائق: ١١٩/٢] ويشهد إلخ: لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : "للمسلم على المسلم ستة حقوق"، وفي رواية أبي أيوب: "ستة خصال واجبة، إن ترك خصلة أو شيئاً، فقد ترك واجباً عليه إذا دعاه أن يجيبه، وإذا مرض أن يعوده، وإذا مات أن يحضره، وإذا لقيه أن يسلم عليه، وإذا استنصحه أن ينصحه، وإذا عطس أن يشمته". وحق المسلم لا يسقط بالقضاء.(فتح، عناية)

يدخل فيه أي القاضي من يضيف خصمه، ويجتنب أيضاً مع الخصمين أو مع أحدهما أو مع غيرهما. (عيني) 
والمزاح: أي ويجتنب عن المزاح مطلقاً .
وتلقين الشاهد: الشهادة مطلقا، ومعناه أن يقول : أتشهد بكذا وكذا؛ لأن فيه إعانة لأحد الخصمين، واستحسن التلقين أبو يوسف في موضع ليس فيه ظن الإعانة كما إذا ترك لفظ الشهادة، وقال: "أعلم" مكان "أشهد"؛ لمهابة المجلس، أو ترك الإشارة، أو حصر في الكلام، أو لم يستفد زيادة علم بتلقينه، وينبغي أن يفتي بقول أبي يوسف؛ لأنه أكثر مهارة في مسائل القضاء، ولأن في تلقينه في غير موضع التهمة إحياء الحق، ولا تهمة في مثله، -
The Book of Judgement. Rulings of Judgement. He rejects a gift except from his relative or from one whose custom it was [to give gifts before his appointment], and [rejects] a special invitation. He attends the funeral and visits the sick. He makes equality between the two [litigants] in seating and attention. He avoids whispering to one of them, hinting to him, coaching his argument, his hospitality, joking, and coaching the witness. [Meaning: the judge] does not allow anyone to enter into it [the case] while he is walking or riding. If he is overtaken by worry, anger, hunger, or need, he refrains from judging. He does not judge while he is repelling one of the two evil things [urine/feces], and likewise in a state when his heart is occupied with joy, extreme cold, or extreme heat. He does not execute a hadd punishment or ta'zir in the mosque. He does not sit alone because it leads to suspicion. (Ayni, Fath) He rejects a gift because it resembles bribery, so he avoids it, and on this were the Companions (Ayni). If returning it is difficult due to not knowing [the giver] or distance, he places it in the public treasury. If its owner comes, it is returned to him. If he is harmed by returning it, he gives him its value. Borrowing and requesting a loan are like a gift. The basis for this is what al-Bukhari narrated: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) appointed a man from Azd, called Ibn al-Lutbiyya, over the sadaqah. When he came, he said, 'This is for you, and this was given to me as a gift.' He said to him, 'Why did he not sit in his father's house or his mother's house and see if it would be given to him or not?' And he placed it in the public treasury. Similarly, Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did with Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him) when he brought wealth. Thus we know that accepting a gift is bribery when it is of this description. (Fath, Inaya) 'Except' means that he has the option not to return the gift (Ayni). '[From] his relative' meaning a mahram relative, because returning it from them would be severing ties of kinship, which is forbidden. (Ayni) 'Or from one whose custom it was' etc.: meaning that the gift was before [his appointment to] judgment, even if they have a dispute, or it exceeds the custom, he rejects it then because it is for the sake of judgment, thus it is from ghulul (treachery). The summary: The giver to the judge is either a litigant or not. In the first case, it is absolutely impermissible to accept his gift, whether he is a relative or not, and whether he gave before judgment or not. In the second case: either he is a relative or one who customarily gave before, or not; and the second is likewise because it is eating on account of judgment, so he avoids it. The first [relative or customary giver] is permissible if the one with the custom does not exceed the usual. Then if the judge takes what he should not take and returning it is difficult, he places it in the public treasury; its ruling is that of a lost item (luqata), because it was given to him for his work, and in this work he is a representative of the Muslims, so the gifts are in meaning for them. (Ayni, Inaya) 'And a special invitation': meaning he rejects the special invitation, i.e., he does not attend it because it is made for his sake. There is a difference of opinion on its definition. It is said: it is that which the host does not make except for him. It is said: every invitation made other than for a wedding or circumcision is special. It is not detailed in the special whether it is from a relative or other. It is said: if it is five to ten people, it is special; if more, it is general. The correct view: the host, if he is such that if he knew the judge would not attend, he would not refrain from making the invitation, then this is general, and accepting it is fine. If he is such that if he knew that if he made the invitation the judge would not attend, he would refrain and not make it, then this is a special invitation which the judge does not accept. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 2/119) 'And he attends' etc.: due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'A Muslim has six rights over another Muslim.' And in the narration of Abu Ayyub: 'Six obligatory characteristics: if he leaves a characteristic or something, he has left an obligation upon him: when he invites him, he responds; when he is sick, he visits him; when he dies, he attends his funeral; when he meets him, he greets him; when he seeks his advice, he advises him; when he sneezes, he says yarhamukallah.' The right of a Muslim does not fall due to being a judge. (Fath, Inaya)
The judge refrains from hosting his opponent, and he also refrains [from hosting] with both opponents or with one of them or with others. (Ayni) 'And joking': meaning he absolutely avoids joking. 'And coaching the witness': coaching the witness absolutely, meaning he says, 'Do you testify to such and such?' because it is helping one of the litigants. Abu Yusuf considered coaching commendable in a place where there is no suspicion of help, such as when he [the witness] omitted the wording of testimony and said 'I know' instead of 'I testify' due to awe of the court, or omitted a gesture, or was stuck in speech, or he does not gain additional knowledge by his coaching. And it is proper to give fatwa according to the saying of Abu Yusuf because he is more skilled in matters of judgment, and because coaching in a place not subject to suspicion is reviving a right, and there is no suspicion in such a case. -
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq's commentary presents a series of rulings on the conduct of a judge (qadi). It begins with the general principle that a judge must reject gifts, as they resemble bribery. The exception is for gifts from a mahram relative (to avoid severing family ties) or from someone who customarily gave gifts before the judge's appointment. If the gift cannot be returned, it is placed in the public treasury (bayt al-mal) and treated like a lost item. Similarly, borrowing is equated with gifting. The proof is the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya, where the Prophet criticized him for accepting a gift because of his position. The passage then covers special invitations: a judge should not accept an invitation made solely because of his position; the criterion is whether the host would have made the invitation even if the judge would not attend. Next, the judge must attend funerals and visit the sick, as these are rights of Muslims that do not cease due to his office. The judge must treat litigants equally in seating and attention, based on the Prophet's command. He must avoid private conversations, hints, coaching arguments, hosting one litigant, joking, and coaching witnesses. Coaching a witness (talaqin al-shahid) is generally prohibited as it assists one party, but Abu Yusuf made an exception when there is no suspicion of bias, e.g., if the witness is simply nervous and uses an incorrect phrase. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is Abu Yusuf's, as it revives rights without suspicion. The passage emphasizes maintaining the dignity and impartiality of the court.

Key Terms
تلقين الشاهد (talaqin al-shahid): Coaching a witness by suggesting or prompting testimony. Generally prohibited, but Abu Yusuf allows it when no suspicion of bias.
مسارة (musarrah): Private whispering to one litigant; prohibited because it raises suspicion.
تسوية (taswiyah): Equality; the judge must treat both litigants equally in every aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the primary reason the Hanafi school generally prohibits coaching a witness?
A) It is a form of disrespect to the court.
B) It involves helping one of the litigants, creating partiality.
C) Witnesses must never be questioned.
D) It may cause the witness to lie.

Key Takeaway
A judge must treat litigants equally and avoid coaching witnesses, except to correct wording without adding facts and without suspicion of bias, per Abu Yusuf's mufta bihi view.

The Obligation of Equality Between Litigants
Ruling with proof: obligation of equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that equality between litigants is wajib for the judge, identify the hadith proof, and explain its scope in seating, looking, and signaling.

Core Idea
A judge must treat both litigants equally in court — equality is wajib by hadith.

The passage begins by establishing a foundational principle for judicial conduct: the judge must observe complete equality between the two litigants. This duty is derived from a direct command of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and applies to seating, eye contact, and gesture. The text further clarifies that this equality is external; internal inclinations beyond the judge's control do not affect his ruling, analogous to the distribution of nights among wives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge's treatment of litigants equally in court. The ruling is wajib (obligatory).
The imperative command indicates obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'If one of you is tried with judging, then let him make them equal in the sitting, signaling, and looking.'
The text quotes the hadith partially.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'falisaw' (let him make equal) indicates obligation per Hanafi usul that al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
This is a standard usul principle.
4
Explain the scope of equality
Equality must be observed in sitting (both on the ground), in facial expression (iqbal), and in looking (naẓar). It applies even between the caliph and the subject, father and son, etc.
External equality is required; internal feelings are exempt.
5
Distinguish internal inclination
If the judge feels inclination in his heart but rules justly, it does not discredit him, as it is beyond control, just as a husband cannot control love equally among wives.
This is a key point against accusations of bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء أحكام القضاء الجنازة ويعود المريض، ويسوّي بينهما جلوساً وإقبالاً، ويتقي عن مسارة أحدهما أي من حيث الجلوس أي يجتنب القاضي وإشارته وتلقين حجّته وضيافته والمزاح وتلقين الشاهد ويعود المريض: إذا لم يكن من المتخاصمين؛ لأن العيادة من حقوق المسلمين. (مع، عيني) ويسوي بينهما: أي يراعى التسوية بين الخصمين في الجلوس؛ لقوله عليه: "إذا ابتلي أحدكم بالقضاء، فليسو بينهم في المجلس والإشارة والنظر؛ ولأنه إذا قدم أحدهما على الآخر، يتجرأ على خصمه، وتنكسر همة الآخر، ويراعي التسوية بين الكبير والصغير، والأب والابن والخليفة ،والرعية والدني والشريف. وهذا دليل على أن للقاضي أن يقضي على الملك الذي ولاه القضاء هكذا فعل شريح لعلي له مع خصمه، وخصمه واحد من الرعية فإذا سوّى بينهما بالفعل، فلا جرح عليه فيما يجده في قلبه من الميل إلى أحدهما بعد أن حكم بينهما بالحق؛ لأن ذلك لا قدرة له عليه كما في القسم بين نسائه. وقوله: "إقبالاً" يريد به تسوية النظر من الجانبين، والحاصل أنه إذا حضر الخصمان بين يدي القاضي، سوى بينهما في المجلس، فيجلسان بين يديه على الأرض؛ لأنه لو أجلسهما في جانب واحد كان أحدهما أقرب إلى القاضي فيفوت التسوية، ولو أجلس أحدهما عن يمينه والآخر عن يساره، فكذلك؛ لفضل اليمين. (فتح، عناية) وإقبالاً: أي ويسوي بينهما من حيث الإقبال فلا يقبل على أحدهما دون الآخر. (عيني) مسارة: أي لا يكلم أحد الخصمين سراً دون الآخر؛ لأن فيه تهمة (عيني) وإشارته أي ويجتنب عن الإشارة إلى أحدهما بيده ولا يشير ولا بحاجبه. (عيني) وتلقين حجته أي ويحترز عن تلقين حجة أحدهما؛ لأنه إعانة له. (عيني) وضيافته: [ أي ويجتنب عن ضيافة أحد الخصمين، فلو أضاف الخصمين جميعاً فلا بأس به. (عيني)] أي يجتنب أحد الخصمين، وفي "المبسوط": لا ينبغي أن يضيف أحد الخصمين إلا أن يكون عن الضحك في وجهه؛ لأنه يجترئ على خصمه، وقوله: "والمزاح" قال الزيلعي: أي يجتنب المزاح مطلقاً؛ لأنه يذهب هيبة القاضي، ولا يكثر المزاح في غير مجلس الحكم أيضاً، فإنه يذهب بالهيبة، والحاصل أنه لا يكلمهما بغير ما تقدما إليه لأجله، فإن ذلك يذهب حشمة مجلس القضاء، فإذا حضرا فهو بالخيار، إن شاء بدأهما بالكلام فقال مالكما، وإن شاء تركهما حتى يبدئاه بالمنطق، وهو أحسن كي لا يكون مهيجاً للخصومة؛ لأنه قعد لقطعها. (عيني، فتح) برأسه [؟]. أن القاضي من يضيف خصمه، ويجتنب أيضاً والمزاح: أي ويجتنب عن المزاح مطلقاً . وتلقين الشاهد: الشهادة مطلقا، مع غير رهما [؟]. (عيني) معه مع الخصمين أو مع أحدهما أو ومعناه أن يقول : أتشهد بكذا وكذا؛ لأن فيه إعانة لأحد الخصمين، واستحسن التلقين أبو يوسف في موضع ليس فيه ظن الإعانة كما إذا ترك لفظ الشهادة، وقال: "أعلم" مكان "أشهد"؛ لمهابة المجلس، أو ترك الإشارة، أو حصر في الكلام، أو لم يستفد زيادة علم بتلقينه، وينبغي أن يفتي بقول أبي يوسف؛ لأنه أكثر مهارة في مسائل القضاء، ولأن في تلقينه في غير موضع التهمة إحياء الحق، ولا تهمة في مثله، - = فكان من المدعى عليه باب التعاون على البر كإرسال الرجل لإحضار الخصم وتكفيله، وحيلولته بينه وبين أشغاله، وأما في موضع التهمة كإن ادعى المدعي ألفاً وخمس مائة والمدعى عليه ينكر خمس مائة، وشهد الشاهدان بالألف، فالقاضي إن قال يحتمل أنه أبرأه من خمس مائة واستفاد الشاهد علما بذلك، ووفق في شهادته كما وفق القاضي فهذا لا يجوز بالاتفاق. (فتح، وجامع الرموز)
Book of Judiciary. Rulings of the Judiciary. The funeral and visiting the sick, and he makes them equal in sitting and facial expression, and he guards against whispering to one of them — meaning in terms of sitting, i.e., the judge avoids his signaling, prompting his argument, his hospitality, joking, and prompting the witness. Visiting the sick: if they are not among the litigants, because visiting the sick is among the rights of Muslims. (Ma', 'Ayni) And he makes them equal: i.e., he observes equality between the litigants in sitting, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'If one of you is tried with judging, then let him make them equal in the sitting, signaling, and looking.' And because if he prefers one over the other, the latter becomes emboldened against his opponent, and the other's spirit is broken. And he observes equality between the great and the small, and the father and the son, and the caliph and the subject, and the lowly and the noble. And this is evidence that the judge may judge over the property concerning which he has been appointed to judge. Thus did Shurayh act with 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) with his opponent, and his opponent was one of the subjects. So if he makes them equal in action, there is no blame on him for what he finds in his heart of inclination towards one of them after he has judged between them with truth, because that is beyond his control, as in the division between his wives. And his statement: 'facial expression' means equal looking from both sides. The conclusion is that when the two litigants appear before the judge, he makes them equal in the sitting; they sit before him on the ground, because if he sat them on one side, one would be closer to the judge and equality would be lost, and if he sat one on his right and the other on his left, similarly, due to the merit of the right. (Fath, 'Inayah) And facial expression: i.e., he makes them equal in terms of facial expression, so he does not favor one over the other. ('Ayni) Whispering: i.e., he does not speak secretly to one litigant without the other, because it involves suspicion. ('Ayni) And his signaling: i.e., he avoids signaling to one of them with his hand, and he does not gesture, not even with his eyebrow. ('Ayni) And prompting his argument: i.e., he refrains from prompting one's argument, because it is assistance to him. ('Ayni) And his hospitality: [i.e., he avoids the hospitality of one of the litigants; if he hosted both litigants together, there is no harm. ('Ayni)] i.e., he avoids one of the litigants. And in 'Al-Mabsut': It is not appropriate to host one of the litigants unless it is (like laughing in his face) because he becomes emboldened against his opponent. And his statement: 'And joking': Al-Zayla'i said: i.e., he avoids joking absolutely, because it destroys the judge's dignity. And he should not joke excessively outside the court as well, for it destroys dignity. The conclusion is that he does not speak to them about anything other than what they came for, because that destroys the reverence of the court. So when they appear, he has the choice: if he wishes, he begins speaking to them saying 'What is your case?' and if he wishes, he leaves them until they begin speaking, and that is better so that he is not provoking the dispute, because he is seated to end it. ('Ayni, Fath) ... The judge who hosts his opponent, and he also avoids joking: i.e., he avoids joking absolutely. And prompting the witness: absolutely, with ... ('Ayni) ... meaning that he says 'Do you testify to such and such?' because it is assistance to one of the litigants. Abu Yusuf approved prompting in a place where there is no suspicion of assistance, such as when he omits the wording of testimony and says 'I know' instead of 'I testify' due to the awe of the court, or he omits the gesture, or is tongue-tied, or does not gain additional knowledge from his prompting. And it is appropriate to give fatwa according to Abu Yusuf's statement, because he is more skilled in matters of the judiciary, and because in prompting in a place that is not suspicious there is revival of the truth, and there is no suspicion in such a case. So it was from the defendant: The chapter on cooperation in righteousness, like sending a man to bring the opponent and take surety from him, and preventing him from his occupations. But in a place of suspicion, such as if the plaintiff claims one thousand and five hundred and the defendant denies five hundred, and the two witnesses testify to one thousand, then if the judge says 'perhaps he has been discharged from five hundred and the witness gained knowledge of that and succeeded in his testimony as the judge succeeded', this is not permissible by agreement. (Fath, Jami' al-Rumuz)
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Imam al-Quduri's al-Mukhtasar, explaining the rules of judicial conduct. It begins by stating that the judge should visit the sick if they are not litigants, as visiting the sick is a Muslim's right. The main theme is equality between the two litigants: the judge must treat them equally in seating, facial expression, and attention. The hadith 'If one of you is tried with judging...' commands this equality. The reasoning given is that favoring one leads to arrogance in that litigant and loss of spirit in the other. Equality applies regardless of status—the caliph and the subject, the noble and the lowly are treated alike. This equality is purely external; internal inclination beyond control does not disqualify the judge, analogous to a husband's equal division among wives. Then the text details specific prohibitions: whispering (musarat) to one litigant, signaling (ishara) with hand or eyebrow, prompting (talqin) one's argument or the witness's testimony, hosting one litigant (unless both are hosted), and joking (mizah) which erodes the court's dignity. The judge may start the conversation or let them begin. On prompting the witness, the text records an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf permits prompting when there is no suspicion of partisanship, e.g., if the witness is so awed that he says 'I know' instead of 'I testify'. The preferred fatwa is with Abu Yusuf because of his expertise in judiciary matters, and it helps revive a rightful claim. However, prompting in suspicious circumstances (e.g., when the witness might have partial knowledge) is unanimously forbidden.

Key Terms
تسوية (taswiyah): Equal treatment of litigants in court proceedings.
إقبال (iqbāl): Facial expression and attention given by the judge to each litigant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary proof for the obligation of equality between litigants?
A) The practice of the caliphs
B) The hadith: 'If one of you is tried with judging...'
C) Analogy to the judge's own family
D) consensus of the scholars

Key Takeaway
Equality between litigants in court is a wajib duty for the judge, proven by a hadith imperative, and applies to all external aspects of conduct.

Prohibited Conduct and the Ruling on Prompting Witnesses
Prohibitions and intra-Hanafi dispute on talqin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the prohibited acts for a judge that create suspicion of bias, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on prompting witnesses, including the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A judge must avoid whispering, signaling, prompting, hosting, and joking to preserve impartiality; prompting is allowed only when no suspicion arises.

This passage continues from the obligation of equality to specific prohibitions that safeguard impartiality. The judge must avoid actions that create suspicion of bias: whispering, signaling, prompting arguments, hosting one litigant, and joking. An important intra-Hanafi difference arises regarding prompting a witness (talqin al-shahid): Abu Yusuf permits it when there is no suspicion of assistance, and the preferred fatwa follows his view, as it helps revive the truth without partiality.

Guided Path
1
List the prohibited acts for the judge
The text enumerates: whispering (musarah), signaling (ishara), prompting a litigant's argument (talqin hujjatih), hospitality (diyafah) of one litigant, joking (mizah), and prompting a witness (talqin al-shahid).
These are all because they create suspicion (tuhmah) or erode reverence.
2
Understand the ruling on prompting the witness
The general Hanafi ruling is prohibition because it is assistance. However, Abu Yusuf makes an exception: if prompting does not create suspicion of assistance (e.g., witness awed and says 'I know' instead of 'I testify'), it is permissible.
The exception is based on istihsan.
3
Identify the mufta bihi
The text says: 'It is appropriate to give fatwa according to Abu Yusuf's statement, because he is more skilled in judiciary matters, and because it revives the truth without suspicion.'
Thus, the practical ruling is to allow prompting in non-suspicious cases.
4
Distinguish suspicious from non-suspicious scenarios
Suspicious: when the witness might have gained partial knowledge (e.g., plaintiff claims 1500, two witnesses testify for 1000, judge suggests discharge of 500). Non-suspicious: witness omits word 'ashhad' due to awe, or is tongue-tied.
The key is the absence of tuhmah (suspicion).

Source Text

(عيني)] أي يجتنب أحد الخصمين، وفي "المبسوط": لا ينبغي أن يضيف أحد الخصمين إلا أن يكون عن الضحك في وجهه؛ لأنه يجترئ على خصمه، وقوله: "والمزاح" قال الزيلعي: أي يجتنب المزاح مطلقاً؛ لأنه يذهب هيبة القاضي، ولا يكثر المزاح في غير مجلس الحكم أيضاً، فإنه يذهب بالهيبة، والحاصل أنه لا يكلمهما بغير ما تقدما إليه لأجله، فإن ذلك يذهب حشمة مجلس القضاء، فإذا حضرا فهو بالخيار، إن شاء بدأهما بالكلام فقال مالكما، وإن شاء تركهما حتى يبدئاه بالمنطق، وهو أحسن كي لا يكون مهيجاً للخصومة؛ لأنه قعد لقطعها. (عيني، فتح) برأسه [؟]. أن القاضي من يضيف خصمه، ويجتنب أيضاً والمزاح: أي ويجتنب عن المزاح مطلقاً . وتلقين الشاهد: الشهادة مطلقا، مع غير رهما [؟]. (عيني) معه مع الخصمين أو مع أحدهما أو ومعناه أن يقول : أتشهد بكذا وكذا؛ لأن فيه إعانة لأحد الخصمين، واستحسن التلقين أبو يوسف في موضع ليس فيه ظن الإعانة كما إذا ترك لفظ الشهادة، وقال: "أعلم" مكان "أشهد"؛ لمهابة المجلس، أو ترك الإشارة، أو حصر في الكلام، أو لم يستفد زيادة علم بتلقينه، وينبغي أن يفتي بقول أبي يوسف؛ لأنه أكثر مهارة في مسائل القضاء، ولأن في تلقينه في غير موضع التهمة إحياء الحق، ولا تهمة في مثله، - = فكان من المدعى عليه باب التعاون على البر كإرسال الرجل لإحضار الخصم وتكفيله، وحيلولته بينه وبين أشغاله، وأما في موضع التهمة كإن ادعى المدعي ألفاً وخمس مائة والمدعى عليه ينكر خمس مائة، وشهد الشاهدان بالألف، فالقاضي إن قال يحتمل أنه أبرأه من خمس مائة واستفاد الشاهد علما بذلك، ووفق في شهادته كما وفق القاضي فهذا لا يجوز بالاتفاق. (فتح، وجامع الرموز)
Book of Judiciary. Rulings of the Judiciary. The funeral and visiting the sick, and he makes them equal in sitting and facial expression, and he guards against whispering to one of them — meaning in terms of sitting, i.e., the judge avoids his signaling, prompting his argument, his hospitality, joking, and prompting the witness. Visiting the sick: if they are not among the litigants, because visiting the sick is among the rights of Muslims. (Ma', 'Ayni) And he makes them equal: i.e., he observes equality between the litigants in sitting, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'If one of you is tried with judging, then let him make them equal in the sitting, signaling, and looking.' And because if he prefers one over the other, the latter becomes emboldened against his opponent, and the other's spirit is broken. And he observes equality between the great and the small, and the father and the son, and the caliph and the subject, and the lowly and the noble. And this is evidence that the judge may judge over the property concerning which he has been appointed to judge. Thus did Shurayh act with 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) with his opponent, and his opponent was one of the subjects. So if he makes them equal in action, there is no blame on him for what he finds in his heart of inclination towards one of them after he has judged between them with truth, because that is beyond his control, as in the division between his wives. And his statement: 'facial expression' means equal looking from both sides. The conclusion is that when the two litigants appear before the judge, he makes them equal in the sitting; they sit before him on the ground, because if he sat them on one side, one would be closer to the judge and equality would be lost, and if he sat one on his right and the other on his left, similarly, due to the merit of the right. (Fath, 'Inayah) And facial expression: i.e., he makes them equal in terms of facial expression, so he does not favor one over the other. ('Ayni) Whispering: i.e., he does not speak secretly to one litigant without the other, because it involves suspicion. ('Ayni) And his signaling: i.e., he avoids signaling to one of them with his hand, and he does not gesture, not even with his eyebrow. ('Ayni) And prompting his argument: i.e., he refrains from prompting one's argument, because it is assistance to him. ('Ayni) And his hospitality: [i.e., he avoids the hospitality of one of the litigants; if he hosted both litigants together, there is no harm. ('Ayni)] i.e., he avoids one of the litigants. And in 'Al-Mabsut': It is not appropriate to host one of the litigants unless it is (like laughing in his face) because he becomes emboldened against his opponent. And his statement: 'And joking': Al-Zayla'i said: i.e., he avoids joking absolutely, because it destroys the judge's dignity. And he should not joke excessively outside the court as well, for it destroys dignity. The conclusion is that he does not speak to them about anything other than what they came for, because that destroys the reverence of the court. So when they appear, he has the choice: if he wishes, he begins speaking to them saying 'What is your case?' and if he wishes, he leaves them until they begin speaking, and that is better so that he is not provoking the dispute, because he is seated to end it. ('Ayni, Fath) ... The judge who hosts his opponent, and he also avoids joking: i.e., he avoids joking absolutely. And prompting the witness: absolutely, with ... ('Ayni) ... meaning that he says 'Do you testify to such and such?' because it is assistance to one of the litigants. Abu Yusuf approved prompting in a place where there is no suspicion of assistance, such as when he omits the wording of testimony and says 'I know' instead of 'I testify' due to the awe of the court, or he omits the gesture, or is tongue-tied, or does not gain additional knowledge from his prompting. And it is appropriate to give fatwa according to Abu Yusuf's statement, because he is more skilled in matters of the judiciary, and because in prompting in a place that is not suspicious there is revival of the truth, and there is no suspicion in such a case. So it was from the defendant: The chapter on cooperation in righteousness, like sending a man to bring the opponent and take surety from him, and preventing him from his occupations. But in a place of suspicion, such as if the plaintiff claims one thousand and five hundred and the defendant denies five hundred, and the two witnesses testify to one thousand, then if the judge says 'perhaps he has been discharged from five hundred and the witness gained knowledge of that and succeeded in his testimony as the judge succeeded', this is not permissible by agreement. (Fath, Jami' al-Rumuz)
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Imam al-Quduri's al-Mukhtasar, explaining the rules of judicial conduct. It begins by stating that the judge should visit the sick if they are not litigants, as visiting the sick is a Muslim's right. The main theme is equality between the two litigants: the judge must treat them equally in seating, facial expression, and attention. The hadith 'If one of you is tried with judging...' commands this equality. The reasoning given is that favoring one leads to arrogance in that litigant and loss of spirit in the other. Equality applies regardless of status—the caliph and the subject, the noble and the lowly are treated alike. This equality is purely external; internal inclination beyond control does not disqualify the judge, analogous to a husband's equal division among wives. Then the text details specific prohibitions: whispering (musarat) to one litigant, signaling (ishara) with hand or eyebrow, prompting (talqin) one's argument or the witness's testimony, hosting one litigant (unless both are hosted), and joking (mizah) which erodes the court's dignity. The judge may start the conversation or let them begin. On prompting the witness, the text records an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf permits prompting when there is no suspicion of partisanship, e.g., if the witness is so awed that he says 'I know' instead of 'I testify'. The preferred fatwa is with Abu Yusuf because of his expertise in judiciary matters, and it helps revive a rightful claim. However, prompting in suspicious circumstances (e.g., when the witness might have partial knowledge) is unanimously forbidden.

Key Terms
مسارة (musārah): Whispering to one litigant without the other.
تلقين (talqīn): Prompting a litigant or witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in which situation is prompting a witness (talqin al-shahid) permitted?
A) When the witness is a relative of the plaintiff
B) When the judge is unsure of the truth
C) When there is no suspicion of assistance, e.g., witness says 'I know' out of awe
D) When both litigants consent

Key Takeaway
While most partial acts are absolutely prohibited, prompting a witness is allowed under Abu Yusuf's view when it revives the truth without creating suspicion; this is the mufta bihi.

Imprisonment for Debt in Hanafi Law
Ruling with proof on imprisonment for debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a debtor may be imprisoned according to the Hanafi school and apply the preferred view on when imprisonment is initiated.

Core Idea
Imprisonment for debt is a coercive measure, not a first resort; the judge must first inquire about the debtor's ability to pay.

The Hanafi school permits imprisonment for a debtor who refuses to pay despite having the means. This lesson explores the legal basis from the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, the strict conditions of the prison, and the key debate within the madhhab about whether a debtor proven by evidence should be imprisoned immediately or only after questioning. By the end, you will be able to apply the preferred ruling that the judge must ask first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sources of legislation
List the three sources cited: Quran (5:33 interpreted as imprisonment), Sunnah (Prophet's practice), and Ijma' (Companion consensus).
The Quranic proof uses tafsir of 'exile' as imprisonment.
2
Examine the physical conditions of imprisonment
Note the harsh environment: no bedding, rough place, limited visits, no release for prayers or funerals. This is meant as a deterrent.
These conditions are for discretionary punishment (ta'zir).
3
Understand the procedural steps before imprisonment
The judge should not imprison immediately upon proof; he must first ask if the debtor has money. If the debtor admits ability and refuses, then imprisonment ensues.
This is the preferred view (al-sawab).
4
Distinguish between proof by evidence and confession
Al-Hidayah advised immediate imprisonment when the debt is proven by evidence (because denial indicates procrastination) but not by confession. The preferred view rejects that distinction and requires questioning in both cases.
The tarjih (preferred view) is found in later texts like Fath al-Qadir.
5
Apply the rule to a modern scenario
Consider a debtor who confesses a debt but asks for time. The judge cannot imprison him until he ascertains whether the debtor has money now or is genuinely unable to pay.
The goal is compulsion, not punishment for inability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: أي في بيان الحبس ولما كان بعض الناس يستحق العقوبة بسبب تمرده، والحبس يصلح للعقوبة، ذكره في كتاب القضاء، وجعله من جملته وهو مشروع بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع، أما الكتاب: فقوله تعالى في قطاع الطريق: أَوْ يُنْفَوْا مِنَ الْأَرْضِ الله (المائدة:۳۳) والمراد به الحبس، وأما السنة: فلأنه عليها رجلاً بالتهمة، وأما حبس الإجماع: فلأن الصحابة له ومن بعدهم أجمعوا عليه، ولم يكن في زمنه عليه السلام وأبي بكر وعمر وعثمان الله سجن، وكان يحبس في المجلس أو في المسجد، ولما كان في زمن علي الله بنى السجن، وهو أول من بناه في الإسلام. تبيين الحقائق: ۹۰/٥، ۹۱] وصفة الحبس: أن يكون في موضع خشن ليس فيه فراش ولا وطاء، ولو جيء له بذلك لا يمكن كما لا يمكن أحد يدخل عليه للاستيناس إلا أقاربه أو جيرانه ولكن لا يمكثون، ولا يخرج الجمعة ولا جماعة ولا لحج فرض ولا لحضور جنازة، ولو بكفيل، وكذا لا يخرج بمجيء رمضان والعيدين، وإن مرض مرضاً أضناه فإن وجد من : لا يخرج، وإلا أخرج بكفيل، ولا يخرج لمعالجة وكسب، ولو له دين أ أخرج ليخاصم، ثم يحبس، ولا يغل، ولا يجرد، ولا يؤاجر، ولا يقام بين يدي صاحب الحق إهانة، ولا يضرب ويجعل للنساء سجن على حدة؛ نفيا للفتنة. (فتح) الحق ولو دانقا وهو سدس درهم. (فتح) أمره إلخ: ينبغي أن يقيد بما إذا لم يتمكن القاضي من الدفع إليه بنفسه كما إذا ادعى عيناً في يد غيره، أو وديعة له عنده، وبرهن أنها هي التي في يده، أو ديناً له عليه وبرهن على ذلك، فوجد معه ما هو من جنس حقه، كان للقاضي أ أن يأخذ العين منه، وما هو من جنس حقه، ويدفعه إلى المالك غير محتاج إلى أمره بدفع ما عليه. (فتح) فإن أبى: أي امتنع عن دفع ما عليه إلى المدعي. (عيني) حبسه في الثمن [ أي في كل دين لزمه بدلا عن مال حصل في يده مثل الثمن. (ط، عيني) ] إن الشيخ لم يفصل بين ثبوت الحق ببينة أو بإقرار، وفصل بينهما في "الهداية" فقال: إذا ثبت بالبينة يحبسه ؛ لظهور المطل بإنكاره، وإن ثبت بإقراره لم يعجل بحبسه؛ لأنه لم يعرف كونه مماطلاً في أول الوهلة، فلعله طمع في الإمهال، فلم يستصحب المال، فإذا امتنع بعد ذلك حبسه؛ لظهور مطله، والصواب: أن لا يحبسه فيهما إذا طلب المدعي ذلك، حتى يسأله، فإن أقر أن له مالاً أمره بالدفع، فإن أبي حبسه؛ = يخدمه
Section: On imprisonment. Since some people deserve punishment due to their rebellion, and imprisonment is suitable for punishment, the author mentioned it in the Book of Judiciary and made it part of it. It is legislated by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book: His statement, the Most High, concerning highway robbers: 'Or they be exiled from the land' (al-Ma'idah: 33)—and what is meant by exile is imprisonment. As for the Sunnah: because he (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion. As for consensus: because the Companions and those after them agreed upon it. There was no prison in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and 'Uthman (may Allah be pleased with them). People were imprisoned in the assembly or the mosque. When it was the time of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), he built the prison, and he was the first to build it in Islam. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 5/90-91) The description of the prison: it should be in a rough place without bedding or cushion. Even if he is brought such, it is not possible, and no one can enter for company except his relatives and neighbors, but they do not stay. He does not go out for Jumu'ah, congregation, obligatory Hajj, or funeral, even with a surety. Similarly, he does not go out at the advent of Ramadan or the two 'Ids. If he falls ill with a debilitating illness, then if he finds someone to serve him [؟], he does not go out; otherwise, he is taken out with a surety. He is not taken out for treatment or earning. Even if he has debt, he is taken out to litigate, then imprisoned. He is not shackled, stripped, rented out, or placed before the claimant as a humiliation. He is not beaten. A separate prison is made for women to avoid fitna. (Fath) The right (is pursued) even if it is a daniq, which is a sixth of a dirham. (Fath) His statement 'ordered him' etc.: It should be qualified for when the judge cannot make the payment himself, such as when the plaintiff claims an item in another's hand, or a deposit with him, and proves that it is the item in hand, or a debt owed to him and he proves that; if he finds with him something of the same type as his right, the judge may take that item and what is of the same type and give it to the owner without needing to order him to pay. (Fath) 'If he refuses': i.e., he refrains from paying what he owes to the plaintiff. ('Ayni) 'Imprison him for the price': i.e., for any debt due as a substitute for property obtained in his hand, like the price. (T, 'Ayni) The Shaykh did not distinguish between the right proven by evidence or by confession; but al-Hidayah distinguished: if proven by evidence, he imprisons him because the delay is apparent in his denial; if proven by confession, he does not hasten to imprison him because his procrastination is not known at first glance; perhaps he hopes for a postponement and did not bring the money. If he refuses after that, he imprisons him because his delay becomes apparent. The correct view is that he does not imprison him in either case if the plaintiff requests it, until he asks him: if he admits he has money, he orders him to pay; if he refuses, he imprisons him.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This section discusses imprisonment (habs) as a legal punishment for a debtor who refuses to pay. The text establishes its legitimacy from three sources: Quran (verse on brigands interpreted as 'exile' meaning imprisonment), Sunnah (the Prophet imprisoned a man on suspicion), and Ijma' (Companions' consensus). Historically, there was no formal prison until Caliph 'Ali built one. The description of the prison is harsh: no bedding, rough conditions, limited visits. The prisoner cannot leave for prayers or funerals even with a surety, and not even for Hajj or Eid. If ill, he may be taken out with a surety if no one cares for him. He can be taken out to litigate his own debts, then re-imprisoned. The conditions of imprisonment are strict: no shackling, stripping, or humiliating display. Women have separate prisons. The right of the creditor is pursued even for a small amount (daniq = 1/6 dirham). The text then discusses when the judge can take property directly without ordering the debtor, and the distinction between proof by evidence (bayyinah) and confession (iqrar): according to al-Hidayah, a debtor proven by evidence is imprisoned immediately because his denial shows procrastination; one who confesses is not imprisoned immediately because he may need time. The preferred view is that imprisonment only after the debtor admits he has money and refuses to pay.

Key Terms
حبس (habs): Imprisonment as a punishment or coercive measure for a debtor who refuses to pay a debt.
دانق (dāniq): A unit of currency equal to one-sixth of a dirham.
مطل (maṭl): Procrastination or delay in paying a debt despite ability to pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does the text use to justify imprisonment?
A) وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا (المائدة: 38)
B) أَوْ يُنْفَوْا مِنَ الْأَرْضِ (المائدة: 33)
C) يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ (البقرة: 183)
D) الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِائَةَ جَلْدَةٍ (النور: 2)

Key Takeaway
Imprisonment for debt in Hanafi law is a last resort, permissible only after the judge verifies the debtor's ability to pay and the debtor refuses.
